Nutrition: Definition and Three Meanings
Definition of nutrition and clarification of its three meanings with a critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of nutrition, the three distinct meanings (potential, near-actual, actual) in Imam al-Razi's wording, and a critique of the view that simple substances cannot serve as nutrition.

Core Idea
Nutrition has three meanings: potential, near-actual, and actual.

This lesson unpacks a key concept in the study of the vegetative soul: nutrition. The author begins by defining nutrition and then clarifies the three meanings found in Imam al-Razi's works. Finally, he raises a doubt about the standard medical view that simple substances cannot be nutrition for animals.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and three meanings
Read the passage and translation carefully. Note the definition of nutrition and the three distinct meanings: potential (like wheat), close-to-actual (needs only adhesion), and actual (become part of the body).
Pay attention to the wording: 'wa qad yuqalu lahu' indicates a detail, not separate categories.
2
Resolve the apparent four meanings
The author notes that the phrasing might suggest four meanings, but Imam al-Razi's intended meanings are three. Understand how the third and fourth apparent meanings are actually the same.
If a 'fa' had been used, the threefold distinction would have been clear.
3
Examine the critique of simple substances
Read the final part where the author questions the physicians' claim that simple substances cannot nourish animals. Consider the analogy with plants absorbing water.
This is an open critique without a definitive answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبيه الثاني : الغذاء ما يقوم بدل ما يتحلل من الشيء بالاستحالة إلى نوعه. ويقال لما هو غذاء بالفعل وبالقوة القريبة والبعيدة هذه العبارة توهم أن للغذاء معاني أربعة وعبارة الإمام الرازي في كتابيه هكذا : الغذاء هو الذي يقوم بدل ما يتحلل عن الشيء بالاستحالة إلى نوعه. وقد يقال له غذاء وهو يعد بالقوة غذاء كالحنطة ويقال له غذاء إذا لم يحتج إلى غير الالتصاق في الانعقاد ويقال له غذاء عند ما صار جزءاً من المغتذى تشبيهاً به بالفعل. فقوله : وقد يقال له تفصيل لما قبله بلا شبهة فلو كان بالفاء لكان أظهر ولم يشتبه على أحد أن معانيه ثلاثة. والمشهور فيما بين الأطباء أن البسيط لا غذاء للحيوان ولا برهان عليه بل فيه إشكال إذ لا شك أن النبات يجذب الماء ويصير ذلك الماء جزءا منه فلم لا يجوز مثله في الحيوان؟
The second note: Nutrition is that which takes the place of what dissolves from a thing by transformation into its type. And it is said for what is nutrition in actuality and in potentiality (close and distant). This expression suggests that nutrition has four meanings, but the expression of Imam al-Razi in his two books is as follows: Nutrition is that which takes the place of what dissolves from a thing by transformation into its type. And it may be said of it as nutrition, and it is considered potentially nutrition like wheat; and it is said to be nutrition when it does not need anything other than adhesion in curdling; and it is said to be nutrition when it becomes a part of the nourished by way of resemblance to actual nutrition. So his statement 'and it may be said' is a detail of what preceded without doubt; had it been with a 'fa', it would have been clearer, and no one would have doubted that its meanings are three. And the well-known opinion among physicians is that the simple does not serve as nutrition for animals, but there is no proof for that; rather there is a problem, for there is no doubt that plants absorb water and that water becomes a part of it, so why should the like not be permissible in animals?
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a definition and clarification of the concept of nutrition (al-ghidha') in the context of the vegetative soul. The author begins with a definition: nutrition is that which replaces what dissolves from a living body by transforming into its type. He then notes that terms like 'nutrition' can be used in different senses: actual nutrition, potential nutrition (close), and potential nutrition (distant). The phrasing of Imam al-Razi might suggest four meanings, but the author argues that it actually indicates three distinct meanings: (1) something considered nutrition potentially, like wheat; (2) something that only needs adhesion in curdling to become nutrition; (3) something that has actually become part of the nourished body. The author remarks that if Imam al-Razi had used a conjunctive 'fa' instead of 'wa', it would have been clearer that these are details of the same definition, not separate categories. Finally, the author addresses the common medical view that simple substances cannot be nutrition for animals, questioning this by pointing out that plants absorb water and make it part of themselves, so why should animals not do the same? This opens a critique without providing a definitive answer.

Key Terms

استحالة
بالقوة
بالفعل
بسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of nutrition yields four distinct meanings.
Correction: Imam al-Razi's wording actually shows three meanings, not four, because the later phrases are detailing the same definition.

Checkpoint
How many distinct meanings of 'nutrition' does the author identify from Imam al-Razi's wording?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Nutrition is defined as replacement of what dissolves, and it has three stages: potential, near-actual, and actual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that nutrition involves transformation into the type of what is nourished. Is there a kind of knowledge or habit you have been allowing to 'dissolve' without transforming you? What step towards 'adhesion' (making it part of yourself) are you missing?
?
The author questions the physicians' claim because it lacks proof. Reflect on a belief you hold about yourself or your life that you have never truly examined for evidence. Will you examine it today?
?
The phrase 'potential nutrition' refers to something that has not yet become actual. What in your spiritual life is still potential—prayer, charity, repentance—that you keep at a distance? What would it take to move it to near-actual?

The Retentive Power (al-masika): Proofs in Stomach and Uterus
Defines and demonstrates the retentive power with anatomical proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the retentive power (al-masika) and the evidential basis for its existence in the stomach, uterus, and other organs.

Core Idea
The retentive power holds until digestion; its proof lies in anatomical observation.

This lesson explores the third of the four serving powers of the vegetative soul: the retentive power (al-masika). The author not only defines it but also provides empirical and rational proofs for its existence, drawing on observations of the stomach and uterus. Understanding this power helps explain how the body retains nourishment long enough for digestion to occur.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the retentive power
The author defines al-masika as the force that retains food until digestion. Notice that the author comments on its proper sequence relative to the digestive power.
Compare with al-Razi and Ibn Sina's ordering.
2
Examine the proof in the stomach
See how the containment of food from all sides demonstrates an active retentive force, especially when food is scarce. Consider the implications of a weak stomach.
The dissection evidence is key.
3
Consider the proof in the uterus
The uterus retains the fetus and seed. The author uses dissection and the natural downward tendency of semen to argue for a retentive faculty.
This extends the argument to different organs.
4
Understand the general argument
The summary contrasts liquids that should descend yet remain with solids that should remain yet descend, proving a specific retaining power in each part.
This is a neat comparative proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة من الأربع الخادمة الماسكة وهي القوة التي تمسك الغذاء ريثما تفعل فيه الهاضمة فعلها فالأنسب أن يقدم ذكرها على الهاضمة كما فعله الإمام الرازي وابن سينا، وكأنه إنما أخرها لأخذه الهاضمة في تفسيرها ويثبتها أي يثبت وجود الماسكة في المعدة احتواءها على الغذاء من كل الجوانب وليس ذلك لامتلاء المعدة فإنها غير موجودة تحتوي وإن قل الغذاء بحيث ليس بينهما فضاءً أصلاً وإذا ضعفت المعدة لم يحصل ذلك الاحتواء المذكور فلا يحسن الهضم وإن كثر الغذاء مع المعدة حصلت القراقر والنفخ ببطء الاستمرار وبالتشريح نشاهده هذا في بعض النسخ ومعناه ما ذكره الإمام في المباحث المشرقية من أنا إذا أعطينا حيوانا غذاءً رطباً كالأشربة والأحساء الرقيقة وشرحنا في ذلك الوقت بطنه وجدنا معدته محتوية عليه من كل جانب قال ووجدنا البواب منطبقاً بحيث لا يمكن أن يسيل منه شيء من ذلك الغذاء الرطب ولو أن حيواناً تناول عظماً أعظم من سعة البواب فإنه يندفع فلما رأينا الرقيق الذي من شأنه النزول غير نازل والكثيف الذي ليس من شأنه النزول نازلاً علمنا أن هناك قوة تمسك شيئاً غير شيء ويثبتها في الرحم احتواءها على الزرع الذي هو الولد وأطواره بحيث لا ينزل ولو شق الحيوان الحامل من أسفل السرة إلى جانب الفرج وكشف عن الرحم برفق لوجد الرحم منضمة من جميع الجوانب منطبقة الفم بحيث لا يمكن أن يدخل فيه الميل فلو لم يكن في جواهر الرحم قوة تمسكه لما كان الأمر كذلك وأيضاً جرم المني يقتضي بطبعه الحركة إلى أسفل فلولا أن في الرحم قوة تمسكه لما وقف وكذلك يثبت بهذا الطريق القوة الماسكة في الأعضاء كلها فإنها تمسك الرطوبات التي هي أغذيتها وبالجملة فلما رأينا الرقيق والثقيل أي الجسم الجامع بين الرقة والثقل كالمشروبات والأحساء الرقيقة في المعدة على ما مر والمني في الرحم والأخلاط في الأعضاء الذي من شأنه النزول لا ينزل ورأينا خلافه أي الغليظ الخفيف الذي ليس من شأنه النزول كالعظم الكبير الحجم الخفيف الوزن على ما تقدم ينزل علمنا أن ثمة أي في كل واحد من المعدة والرحم والأعضاء قوة ماسكة
The third of the four serving powers: the retentive (al-masika), and it is the power that retains the food until the digestive power does its action. So it is more appropriate to mention it before the digestive power, as Imam al-Razi and Ibn Sina did, and it seems he only delayed it because he used the digestive power in its explanation. And one proves it—meaning one proves the existence of the retentive power—in the stomach by its containment of food from all sides, and that is not due to the fullness of the stomach, for it contains even when the food is little, such that there is no space between them at all. And when the stomach weakens, that mentioned containment does not occur, so digestion is not good; and when the food is abundant with the stomach, gurgling and bloating occur due to slowness of passage. And by dissection we observe it. This is in some copies, its meaning being what the Imam mentioned in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: that if we give an animal moist food like drinks and thin soups, and then dissect its belly at that time, we find its stomach containing it from all sides. He said: we find the pylorus closed such that nothing of that moist food can flow out of it. And if an animal swallows a bone larger than the width of the pylorus, it gets pushed out. So when we see the thin liquid which is prone to descend not descending, and the thick solid which is not prone to descend descending, we know that there is a power that retains one thing and not another. And one proves it in the uterus by its containment of the seed which is the offspring and its stages, such that it does not descend. And if one cuts open a pregnant animal from below the navel to the side of the vulva and gently uncovers the uterus, one would find the uterus closed from all sides, its mouth sealed, such that a probe cannot enter. So if there were not in the substance of the uterus a power to retain it, the matter would not be so. Also, the substance of semen naturally moves downward; were there not a power in the uterus to retain it, it would not stop. Similarly, one proves by this method the retentive power in all the limbs, for they retain the moistures that are their nutrition. In summary: when we see the thin and heavy—meaning the body combining thinness and heaviness, like drinks and thin soups in the stomach as previously, and semen in the uterus, and the humors in the limbs—that which is prone to descend not descending, and we see its opposite—meaning the thick and light, which is not prone to descend, like the large bone of light weight as previously—descending, we know that there is, in each of the stomach, uterus, and limbs, a retentive power.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage defines and demonstrates the existence of the retentive power (al-masika), one of the four serving faculties of the vegetative soul. The author first defines it as the power that holds food long enough for digestion to occur. He notes that logically it should be discussed before the digestive power, as al-Razi and Ibn Sina did; the author's own sequence is justified because he uses the digestive power to define the retentive. He then provides proofs: (1) In the stomach: the stomach holds food on all sides even when not full; if it were just due to fullness, it wouldn't hold small amounts tightly. Weak stomach fails to contain properly, causing poor digestion; excess food causes gurgling. (2) By dissection: experiments show that liquid food stays in the stomach while a large bone passes through, indicating a selective holding force. (3) In the uterus: the uterus holds the fetus and its membranes without descending; dissection shows the uterus tightly closed. Also, semen naturally tends downward, so without a retentive force it would not stay. (4) By analogy in all limbs: they retain their nourishing moistures. The summary argument contrasts thin heavy substances (which should flow but stay) with thick light ones (which should stay but flow), proving a specific retentive power in each organ.

Key Terms
الماسكة
الغذاء
الهاضمة
التشريح
الرحم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the stomach holds food only by fullness or by anatomical structure.
Correction: The author shows it is an active power: the stomach holds even when not full, and the pylorus selectively closes.

Checkpoint
What is the main function of the retentive power (al-masika)?
A) To expel waste
B) To hold food until digestion acts
C) To attract food
D) To dissolve food

Key Takeaway
The retentive power is a distinct faculty proven by the stomach's ability to hold food tightly and the uterus's retention of the fetus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the retentive power in your own stomach, do you recognize that your ability to digest food is not merely mechanical but a gift from Allah? How often do you thank Him for the hidden faculties that sustain you every moment?
?
The retentive power holds the fetus even though its nature is to descend. What in your life is held by Allah's power when your own nature would cause you to fall? Are you aware of His preservation?

The Expulsive Power: Definition and Evidence
Definition and evidence of the expulsive power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition and dual function of the expulsive power (al-dafi'a) and the personal experience that constitutes its evidence.

Core Idea
The expulsive power pushes food to organs and expels waste, proven by the force of defecation.

This passage introduces the fourth of the serving faculties: the expulsive power. The author defines it by its two roles: assisting in nutrition and removing waste. He then provides a striking empirical proof that anyone can verify within themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the two functions
The passage presents two distinct roles of the expulsive power: pushing prepared food toward the organ and expelling waste. Underline these in the text.
Both are active, not passive.
3
Understand the evidence
The author provides evidence from the mixture of humors in the blood and from personal experience during defecation. Notice how this appeals to common human experience as proof.
4
Reflect on the marginal note
The passage ends with a clarification of wording: 'al-dafi'a' means 'that which pushes'. Consider how precise terminology grounds the discussion.
This is a typical scholarly gloss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة من القوى الخادمة الدافعة إما للغذاء المهيأ للعضو إليه فتعين بدفعها جاذبة العضو في جذب الغذاء وإما للفضل عنه فإن الدم الوارد على الأعضاء مخلوط بالأخلاط الثلاثة فيأخذ كل عضو ما يلائمه ويدفع ما ينافيه ولولا دفعه إياه لم يخل شيء من التي تفسده وأيضاً يجده ترك هذه الكناية أولى أي يجد كل أحد من نفسه عند التبرز إذا كان البراز معتقلا وكان في الأمعاء فضل لداغ كأنه معدته وأمعاءه وسائر أحشائه تنتزع من موضعها وتتحرك إلى أسفل لدفع الفضل حتى أنه ربما انخلع المعاء المستقيم عن موضعه لقوة الحركة الدافعة بمنزلة قوله : الدافعة جعل الألف واللام هاهنا بمعنى التي تدفع الغذاء إليه أي إلى العضو وقوله للفضل أي التي تدفع الفضل عنه أي عن العضو عند التبرز أي عند التغيط وقوله فضل
The fourth of the serving powers: the expulsive (al-dafi'a). It is either for the food prepared for the organ, so by its pushing it assists the attractive power of the organ in attracting the food; or for the waste from it, for the blood arriving at the organs is mixed with the three humors, so each organ takes what is suitable for it and expels what is incompatible. Were it not for this expulsion, none of the organs would be free of that which corrupts it. Also, one finds it—the author preferred to drop this metaphor—meaning that everyone finds in himself during defecation, when the stool is impacted and there is waste in the bowels, as if his stomach, intestines, and all his viscera are being pulled from their places and moving downward to expel the waste, such that sometimes the rectum is dislocated from its place due to the force of the expulsive movement. His statement: 'al-dafi'a'—he made the definite article here meaning 'that which pushes the food to it, i.e., to the organ.' And his statement: 'for the waste'—meaning that which pushes the waste away from it, i.e., from the organ, during defecation, i.e., during excrement. And his statement: 'waste'.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage defines the expulsive power (al-dafi'a), the fourth of the serving faculties. The author explains its two functions: first, it pushes prepared food toward the organ, thereby assisting the attractive force in the organ's attraction of food; second, it pushes waste away from the organ. The blood reaching the organs contains a mixture of humors; each organ selects what is suitable and expels what is incompatible. Without this expulsion, no organ would remain free of corrupting substances. The author then provides empirical evidence: everyone can sense during defecation, especially when the stool is hard, that the intestines and stomach seem to contract and move downward with great force, sometimes even causing rectal prolapse. This personal experience is a direct proof of the expulsive power. The marginal note clarifies the wording: 'al-dafi'a' means 'that which pushes food to the organ' and also 'that which pushes waste away from the organ'.

Key Terms
الدافعة
الفضل
الجاذبة
التبرز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think expelling waste is a passive process.
Correction: The author shows it is an active power with two distinct functions, and the body expels waste forcefully.

Checkpoint
What two functions does the expulsive power (al-dafi'a) serve?
A) Attracting food and digesting it
B) Pushing prepared food toward organs and expelling waste
C) Holding food and digesting it
D) Absorbing food and excreting waste

Key Takeaway
The expulsive power is an active faculty with two distinct roles, and its reality is confirmed by the undeniable physical experience of defecation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience the physical sensation of needing to expel waste, do you recognize it as a sign of Allah's design? How can you train yourself to see His wisdom in such everyday functions?
?
The author says that without the expulsive power, organs would be corrupted by waste. What spiritual 'waste' are you holding onto that is corrupting your heart? Will you actively expel it today by seeking forgiveness or changing a habit?
?
The expulsive power assists the attractive power by pushing food. In your life, are there good things you are attracting but failing to actively push forward into action? What is one good intention you have that needs the 'push' of action right now?

Proof of the Retaining Faculty by Dissection
Proof of the retaining faculty via dissection and universal generalization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how empirical observation through dissection demonstrates the existence of a distinct retaining faculty in the stomach, womb, and organs.

Core Idea
Dissection reveals a selective force that holds liquids but not solids.

The author presents a proof for the retaining faculty based on empirical evidence from dissection. Drawing on observations from Imam al-Razi's 'al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya', he describes how the stomach contains liquid food while allowing large bones to pass, indicating a force that holds some things but not others. Similarly, the womb retains the fetus, and organs retain their moisture. This lesson will walk through these observations and the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the author's argument. Note the key observations and the conclusion.
Pay attention to the contrast between liquid and solid in the stomach example.
2
Analyze the stomach observation
The author notes that when an animal is given liquid food and dissected, the stomach contains it from all sides and the pylorus is closed, preventing outflow. However, a large bone passes through. This shows a force that selectively retains liquid.
3
Examine the womb evidence
The womb is found contracted around the fetus, and if not for a retaining force, the semen would descend due to its natural downward motion. This parallels the stomach example.
4
Generalize to all organs
The same reasoning applies to all organs: they retain their moisture. The general pattern is that thin/heavy substances (prone to descend) are retained, while thick/light substances (not prone to descend) descend. This proves the existence of a retaining faculty.
The conclusion is that there is a retaining force in each of these parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالتشريح نشاهده هذا في بعض النسخ ومعناه ما ذكره الإمام في المباحث المشرقية من أنا إذا أعطينا حيوانا غذاءً رطباً كالأشربة والأحساء الرقيقة، وشرحنا في ذلك الوقت بطنه وجدنا معدته محتوية عليه من كل جانب . قال : ووجدنا البواب منطبقاً بحيث لا يمكن أن يسيل منه شيء من ذلك الغذاء الرطب . ولو أن حيواناً تناول عظماً أعظم من سعة البواب فإنه يندفع فلما رأينا الرقيق الذي من شأنه النزول غير نازل والكثيف الذي ليس من شأنه النزول نازلاً علمنا أن هناك قوة تمسك شيئاً غير شيء (و) يثبتها ( في الرحم احتواءها على الزرع الذي هو الولد وأطواره ( بحيث لا ينزل)، ولو شق الحيوان الحامل من أسفل السرة إلى جانب الفرج وكشف عن الرحم برفق لوجد الرحم منضمة من جميع الجوانب منطبقة الفم بحيث لا يمكن أن يدخل فيه الميل فلو لم يكن في جواهر الرحم قوة تمسكه لما كان الأمر كذلك وأيضاً جرم المني يقتضي بطبعه الحركة إلى أسفل فلولا أن في الرحم قوة تمسكه لما وقف، (وكذلك) يثبت بهذا الطريق القوة الماسكة ( في الأعضاء ( كلها فإنها تمسك الرطوبات التي هي أغذيتها ( وبالجملة فلما رأينا الرقيق والثقيل) أي الجسم الجامع بين الرقة والثقل كالمشروبات والأحساء الرقيقة في المعدة على ما مر والمني في الرحم والأخلاط في الأعضاء ( الذي من شأنه النزول لا ينزل و رأينا ) خلافه ) أي الغليظ الخفيف ( الذي ليس من شأنه النزول ) كالعظم الكبير الحجم الخفيف الوزن على ما تقدم ( ينزل علمنا أن ثمة أي في كل واحد من المعدة والرحم والأعضاء (قوة ماسكة.
And by dissection we observe it. This is in some copies, and its meaning is what the Imam mentioned in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: that if we give an animal moist food like drinks and thin soups, and at that time we open its belly, we find its stomach containing it from every side. He said: and we found the pylorus closed such that nothing of that moist food could flow out. And if an animal consumed a bone larger than the capacity of the pylorus, it would be pushed out. So when we saw the thin liquid, which naturally tends to descend, not descending, and the dense solid, which does not naturally tend to descend, descending, we knew that there is a force that holds one thing and not another. And it is established in the womb: its containment of the seed which is the offspring and its stages such that it does not descend. And if one cuts open a pregnant animal from below the navel to the side of the vulva and gently uncovers the womb, one would find the womb contracted from all sides, its mouth closed such that a probe cannot enter it. If there were not in the substances of the womb a force holding it, it would not be so. Also, the substance of semen naturally requires downward motion; were it not for a force in the womb holding it, it would not stop. And likewise, by this method the retaining force is established in all organs, for they retain the moisture that is their nourishment. In summary, when we saw the thin and heavy—that is, a body combining thinness and heaviness like drinks and thin soups in the stomach as previously mentioned, and semen in the womb, and humors in the organs—which naturally tend to descend, not descending, and we saw the opposite—that is, the thick and light—which does not naturally tend to descend—like a large bone, large in size but light in weight, as mentioned earlier—descending, we knew that there is, in each of the stomach, womb, and organs, a retaining force.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of the retaining faculty (القوة الماسكة) based on empirical observation through dissection. It begins by citing an observation from Imam al-Razi in his 'al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya': when an animal is given liquid food and the abdomen is opened, the stomach is found to contain it from all sides, and the pylorus (البواب) is closed so that nothing seeps out. In contrast, if a large bone is eaten, it passes through. This shows that there is a force that retains the liquid but not the bone. Then the same is shown for the womb: dissection shows the womb is closed around the fetus, and if not for a retaining force, the sperm would fall due to its natural downward motion. Finally, this is generalized to all organs, which retain their moisture. The conclusion is that there is a retaining faculty in each of these parts.

Key Terms

البواب
الرحم
المباحث المشرقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The retaining faculty is just a mechanical property of the organs.
Correction: The author argues that selective retention—holding some things but not others—indicates a distinct immaterial force beyond mere mechanical structure.

Proof of the Expelling Faculty
The author proves the expelling faculty through defecation and vomiting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the empirical evidence for the expelling faculty and why it is considered an active force.

Core Idea
The body's expelling actions are active, not passive—they require a dedicated faculty.

The author now turns to the fourth serving faculty: the expelling faculty. He argues that its existence is evident from common bodily experiences like defecation and vomiting. These are not merely mechanical processes but involve a deliberate force that the body exerts to expel what is harmful or unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the author's description of the two functions of the expelling faculty and the evidence he provides.
The passage is detailed; focus on the experiential evidence.
2
Identify the two functions
The author divides the expelling faculty into two: one that expels food prepared for an organ (aiding attraction) and another that expels waste. Find these definitions in the text.
3
Examine the defecation evidence
The author describes the sensation of defecation when stool is impacted: the intestines and stomach move downward with such force that the rectum may dislocate. This shows an active expelling power.
Reflect on the phrase 'as if his stomach and intestines are pulling away'.
4
Examine the vomiting evidence
Involuntary vomiting also demonstrates the expelling faculty: the stomach contracts upward, dragging other organs. The author notes that such actions cannot be purely mechanical.
Consider why the author adds 'sea-related purgations' as further support.

The fourth of the serving faculties: the expelling faculty. It expels either the food prepared for an organ—thus assisting the attracting faculty of the organ in attracting food—or expels the waste from it. For the blood arriving at the organs is mixed with the three humors; each organ takes what suits it and expels what is incompatible. Were it not for its expulsion of it, nothing would be free of that which corrupts it. Also, one finds—it is better to drop this metaphor—i.e., one finds the organs free of humors: every person experiences in himself during defecation, when the stool is impacted and there is irritating waste in the intestines, as if his stomach and intestines and all his viscera are pulling away from their places and moving downward to expel the waste, such that sometimes the rectum is dislocated from its place due to the strength of the expelling motion. This is like his saying: 'al-dafi‘ah'—the definite article here means 'that which expels food to it, i.e., to the organ.' And his saying: 'li-l-fadl' means 'that which expels waste from it, i.e., from the organ, at defecation, i.e., at excretion.' And his saying: 'fadl' refers to what afflicts him in dysentery. Also indicated by involuntary vomiting, and what we see then in the stomach of pulling away from its place upward such that all the viscera move with it. Likewise indicated by other sea-related purgations, for they must have an expeller that expels them.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the expelling faculty (القوة الدافعة). It is a serving faculty that either helps in attracting food by expelling waste, or directly expels waste from organs. The proof is experiential: during defecation, one feels the intestines and stomach moving downward to expel stool, sometimes with such force that the rectum dislocates. Similarly, involuntary vomiting shows the stomach contracting upward, dragging other organs. These observed phenomena cannot be purely mechanical; they require a deliberate expelling faculty.

Ranking the Faculties' Need for Heat and Dryness
Ranks the four faculties' need for heat and dryness with reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how heat and dryness serve the four vegetative faculties in different degrees based on their functions.

Core Idea
Heat and dryness are not equally needed; each faculty's need matches its function.

In this passage, the author explains how the four basic qualities—heat and dryness—assist the four vegetative faculties in their actions. He ranks the faculties by their need for heat and then by their need for dryness, providing the reasoning behind each ranking. Let's read the passage carefully to grasp this hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two rankings
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice that the author first discusses the need for heat, then the need for dryness.
The passage uses 'thumma' to indicate order.
2
Understand the ranking for heat
Focus on the sentence that ranks the faculties by need for heat: digestive first, then attractive, repulsive, retentive. Note the reason for each: digestive needs two movements, attractive needs many spatial movements, repulsive involves pure movement, retentive has more settling.
Movement requires heat, so more movement equals more need.
3
Understand the ranking for dryness
Now look at the ranking for dryness: retentive first, then attractive, then repulsive. The retentive needs dryness for holding, attractive for enabling movement, repulsive for firmness in pushing.
Dryness helps with settling and firmness.
4
Note the technical terms
The passage explains terms like 'al-līf al-muwarrab' (twisted fiber) and 'mukhaddira'. Read the explanations to understand the anatomical references.
These terms are specific to the medical context of the text.
5
Reflect on the overall rationale
Ask yourself why the order is as given. The key idea is that the need for a quality depends on the primary action of the faculty: more movement requires more heat; more holding requires more dryness.
This reflects the principle that qualities serve functions proportionally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيهان آخران على أمرين متفرعين على ثبوت القوى وتعددها (الأول قالوا: وهذه القوى (الأربع) الخادمة للأربع الأولى (تخدمها الكيفيات الأربع فأشد القوى حاجة إلى الحرارة (الهاضمة) لأن فعلها لا يتم إلا بحركتين كيفية وأينية والمتحرك يحتاج إلى الحرارة، ثم الجاذبة لأنها تحتاج إلى حركات كثيرة في الأين، ثم الدافعة لأن مع فعلها تحريك محض، ثم الماسكة لأن فعلها لا يحصل إلا بتحريك الليف لكن مدة تسكينها أكثر، وأشد القوى حاجة إلى اليبوسة (الماسكة) لأن فعلها الإمساك والتسكين واليبوسة نافعة لذلك، ثم الجاذبة لأن حاجتها إلى التحريك أمس من حاجتها إلى التسكين، ثم الدافعة لأن فعلها تحريك واليبوسة تمكن من الاعتماد. وقوله: حركتان كيفية وأينية أي استحالة في الكيف وحركة في الأين. وقوله: الليف المورب هو من باب التفعيل يقال: ورب العرق أي فسد، والمراد هاهنا هو الليف المرخي المرسل. وقوله: الممتنة من آمتنه أي جعله متيناً صلباً. وقوله: مخدرة بالخاء المعجمة أي مؤدية للكسل والفتور، وفي بعض النسخ بالحاء المهملة يقال: حدر جلد الرجل أي ورم. وقوله: في الزراقات وقد مر تفسير الزراقات في آخر مباحث المكان. هذا وجه الترتيب الذي أشار إليه هاهنا بكلمة (ثم) يعني أن الحاجة إلى اليبوسة إنما هو لأجل التسكين، وظاهر أن التسكين في فاعلية الماسكة يكون مقصوداً بالذات، وفي فاعلية الجاذبة يكون مقصوداً لأجل التمكن من التحريك، فتكون حاجة الماسكة إلى اليبوسة أكثر من حاجة الجاذبة إليها.
Two further notices concerning two matters derived from the establishment of the powers and their multiplicity. (The first) They said: These four powers that serve the first four are served by the four qualities. So the strongest of the powers in need of heat is the digestive (al-hāḍima), because its act is only completed by two movements, qualitative and spatial, and that which moves requires heat. Then the attractive (al-jādhiba), because it needs many spatial movements. Then the repulsive (al-dāfiʿa), because its act involves pure movement. Then the retentive (al-māsika), because its act only occurs by moving the twisted fiber, but its duration of settling is longer. And the strongest of the powers in need of dryness is the retentive (al-māsika), because its act is holding and settling, and dryness is beneficial for that. Then the attractive, because its need for movement is more urgent than its need for settling. Then the repulsive, because its act is movement, and dryness enables firmness. And His saying: 'two movements, qualitative and spatial' means a transformation in quality and a movement in place. And His saying: 'the twisted fiber (al-līf al-muwarrab)' is from the form tafʿīl; it is said 'waraba al-ʿirq' meaning it became corrupt, and what is intended here is the relaxed, loosened fiber. And His saying: 'al-mumattina' is from 'amattanahu' meaning he made it strong and solid. And His saying: 'mukhaddira' with the letter khā' means causing laziness and languor, and in some copies with the letter ḥā', it is said 'ḥadara jildu al-rajul' meaning it swelled. And His saying: 'in al-zarrāqāt' and the interpretation of 'al-zarrāqāt' has already passed at the end of the discussion of place. This is the order indicated here by the word 'thumma', meaning that the need for dryness is only for the sake of settling. It is apparent that settling in the action of the retentive is intended in itself, while in the action of the attractive it is intended for the sake of enabling movement; thus the need of the retentive for dryness is greater than the need of the attractive for it.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory notice (tanbīh) on the relationship between the four basic qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) and the four vegetative faculties (digestive, attractive, repulsive, retentive). It first establishes that these four qualities serve the four faculties. Then it ranks the faculties' need for heat: the digestive (hāḍima) needs heat most because its action requires both a qualitative change (cooking food) and a spatial movement (mixing); the attractive (jādhiba) requires many spatial movements; the repulsive (dāfiʿa) involves pure movement; and the retentive (māsika) requires movement only to grip but then holds for a longer period, so its need for heat is least. Next, it ranks the need for dryness: the retentive (māsika) needs dryness most because its essential act is holding and settling, and dryness helps in that; the attractive (jādhiba) needs dryness next because it needs to grip its instrument to move; and the repulsive (dāfiʿa) also needs dryness for the firmness required in pushing. The passage then explains technical terms from the original text: 'two movements, qualitative and spatial' refers to transformation in quality and movement in place; 'twisted fiber' (al-līf al-muwarrab) means the relaxed, loosened fiber (muscle); 'al-mumattina' means made strong; 'mukhaddira' means causing lethargy (with variant reading). The order indicated by 'thumma' (then) reflects the principle that the need for dryness is ultimately for settling, and since settling is the primary function of the retentive, its need is greatest, while the attractive's need for dryness is secondary to its need for movement.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That heat and dryness are equally needed by all faculties, rather than proportionate to their functions.
Correction: The passage shows that the need for heat correlates with the amount of movement a faculty performs, and the need for dryness correlates with the need for settling or firmness. Each faculty has a distinct degree of need.

Key Takeaway
The four vegetative faculties require heat and dryness in degrees proportional to their functions: more movement demands more heat, more holding demands more dryness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Need for Heat in Vegetative Faculties
Explanation and ranking of vegetative faculties' need for heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why heat is necessary for the vegetative faculties and how they are ranked in order of need based on the amount of motion each involves.

Core Idea
The amount of motion determines a faculty's need for heat.

This passage explains a key principle in physiology: the vegetative faculties require heat to perform their motions. The author ranks the digestive, attractive, expulsive, and retentive faculties based on the intensity and number of motions involved, showing that the digestive faculty has the greatest need. This illustrates how motion and heat are intertwined in living beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get a sense of the author's argument.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'f–' and 'thumma'.
2
Identify the four faculties and their motions
Find the digestive, attractive, expulsive, and retentive faculties. Note what kind of motion each involves: digestive has two motions, attractive has spatial motion, etc.
The passage explains that even retention requires motion of the fiber.
3
Understand the ranking
Notice the author's ranking: digestive (most need) then attractive, then expulsive, then retentive (least need). Understand why based on the amount of motion.
The digestive has both qualitative and spatial motion, so its need is greatest.
4
Reflect on the role of cold
Consider the statement that cold is deadly and numbing, highlighting why heat is essential for motion.
This reinforces the principle that motion requires heat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بد من الحرارة للهاضمة لأن الهضم عبارة عن إحالة الغذاء في الكيف وهي تحصل إلا بتفريق الأجزاء الغليظة وجمع الأجزاء الرقيقة ولا يحصلان إلا بحركة مكانية. ففعل الهاضمة حركتان كيفية وأينية وكل واحد من الجذب والدفع حركة واحدة أينية والإمساك وإن لم يكن في نفسه حركة بل هو منع عن الحركة إلا أنه لا يحصل إلا بتحريك الليف المورب إلى هيئة الاشتمال فلا بد فيه أيضاً من الحركة الأينية وإذا ثبت أن أفعال هذه القوى لا تتم إلا بالحركة ولا شك أن البرودة مميتة مخدرة فلا ينفع بالذات شيئاً من القوى بل هي محتاجة في أفعالها وحركاتها إلى الحرارة التي تعاونها فما كانت الحركة فيها أكثر كالهاضمة كانت حاجتها إلى الحرارة أشد، ثم الجاذبة لأنها تحتاج إلى حركات في الأين كثيرة قوية. قالوا: والاجتذاب إما بفعل القوة كما في المغناطيس وإما باضطرار الخلاء كانجذاب الماء في الزراقات وإما بالحرارة كما في السراج، وإن كان هذا الأخير راجعاً في الحقيقة إلى ذلك الاضطرار. فإذا كان الجاذبة معاونة حرارة كان الجذب أقوى ثم الدافعة لأن مع فعلها تحريك محض ثم الماسكة لما مر من أن فعلها لا يحصل إلا بتحريك الليف لكن لما كانت مدة تسكين الماسكة للغذاء أكثر من مدة تحريكها لليف كان احتياجها أقل.

قوله: الليف المورب هو من باب التفعيل يقال: ورب العرق أي فسد والمراد هاهنا هو الليف المرخي المرسل وقوله: الممتنة من آمتنه أي جعله متيناً صلباً. وقوله: مخدرة بالخاء المعجمة أي مؤدية للكسلان، والفتور وفي بعض النسخ بالحاء المهملة يقال: حدر جلد الرجل أي ورم بكسر الراء وأحدرته أنا الحارة مغلظة للأغذية ومخلخلة لها. وقوله: في الزراقات وقد مر تفسير الزراقات في آخر مباحث المكان في مرصد الكم من موقف الأعراض لأنه حاجتها إلى التحريك أمس هذا وجه الترتيب الذي أشار إليه هاهنا بكلمة ثم يعني أن الحاجة إلى اليبوسة إنما هو لأجل التسكين وظاهر أن التسكين في فاعلية الماسكة يكون مقصوداً بالذات وفي فاعلية الجاذبة يكون مقصوداً لأجل التمكن من التحريك من بعض مساكن الأغذية إلى مسكن آخر لها فتكون حاجة الماسكة إلى اليبوسة أكثر من حاجة الجاذبة إلى التحريك كانت أمس من حاجتها إلى تسكين الأغذية.
There is a need for heat for the digestive faculty because digestion is the transformation of food in quality, and it occurs only by breaking up coarse parts and combining fine parts, and these two do not occur except by spatial motion. So the action of the digestive faculty consists of two motions: qualitative and spatial. Each of attraction and expulsion is a single spatial motion. Retention, even if it is not a motion in itself but rather a prevention of motion, does not occur except by moving the twisted fiber (al-lif al-mawrab) into a grasping configuration, so it also necessarily involves spatial motion. Since it is established that the actions of these faculties are not completed except by motion, and there is no doubt that cold is deadly and numbing, it does not benefit any of the faculties essentially; rather, they are in need, in their actions and motions, of heat which aids them. So whichever has more motion, like the digestive, its need for heat is greater. Then the attractive faculty [is next] because it needs many strong spatial motions. They said: attraction is either by the power's action, as in a magnet, or by the necessity of a vacuum, as the attraction of water in syringes, or by heat, as in a lamp, though this last is really reducible to that necessity. So if the attractive faculty is aided by heat, its attraction is stronger. Then the expulsive faculty, because its action is pure motion. Then the retentive faculty, because, as mentioned, its action does not occur except by moving the fiber, but since the duration of the retentive's holding of food is longer than the duration of its moving the fiber, its need is less.

His saying: al-lif al-mawrab is from the form taf`il; one says: wariba al-`irq meaning it became corrupt; intended here is the relaxed, released fiber. His saying: al-mumattana from amattanahu meaning he made it strong, firm. His saying: mukaddira with the khā` mu`jama meaning causing laziness and sluggishness; in some copies with the ḥā` muhmala; one says: ḥadira jildu al-rajul meaning it swelled; and aḥdartuhu anā: the heat thickens foods and loosens them. His saying: fī al-zurrāqāt; the explanation of zurrāqāt has already passed at the end of the investigations of place in the observatory of quantity from the Station of Accidents. Because its need for motion is more pressing: this is the aspect of the order he indicated here with the word 'thumma', meaning that the need for dryness is for the sake of cessation. It is apparent that cessation in the activity of the retentive is intended essentially, and in the activity of the attractive it is intended for the sake of enabling motion from some places of food storage to another place for it. So the retentive's need for dryness is greater than the attractive's need for motion, since its need for cessation was more pressing than its need for motion.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage explains why the vegetative faculties require heat and ranks them in order of need. It begins by focusing on the digestive faculty: digestion involves changing food in quality (how it is) and requires breaking down large parts and combining small parts, which only happens through spatial motion. So the digestive power has two motions—qualitative and spatial. In contrast, attraction and expulsion are each a single spatial motion. Retention is not a motion but a prevention of motion; however, it still requires moving a fiber into a grasping shape, so it also involves spatial motion. Since all these actions require motion, and cold kills and numbs (thus hindering motion), the faculties need heat to aid them. The degree of need corresponds to the amount of motion: the digestive has the most motion (two motions), so its need is greatest; then the attractive, which requires many strong spatial motions; then the expulsive, whose action is pure motion; and finally the retentive, because it holds food longer than it moves the fiber, so its need is least. The passage then includes explanatory notes on terms: 'al-lif al-mawrab' is the relaxed fiber; 'al-mumattana' means made firm; 'mukaddira' means numbing; and it clarifies that the reference to 'zurrāqāt' (syringes) was explained earlier. The note also explains why the order of need for dryness (mentioned later) follows the same pattern: the need for dryness is for cessation, and since the retentive's main function is cessation, its need for dryness is greatest; the attractive needs dryness to enable motion by stabilizing its tool.

Key Terms
القوى النباتية
الهاضمة
الجاذبة
الدافعة
الماسكة
الحركة المكانية
الحركة الكيفية
الليف المورب
الزراقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that heat is only needed for digestion, not for the other faculties.
Correction: The passage shows that all four vegetative faculties require heat, but to different degrees based on their motion.

Checkpoint
Which vegetative faculty has the greatest need for heat according to the passage?
A) The retentive faculty
B) The attractive faculty
C) The digestive faculty
D) The expulsive faculty

Key Takeaway
The amount of motion involved in a faculty's action determines its need for heat; more motion requires more heat.

Services of the Four Qualities to the Faculties
The author explains how dryness, moisture, and cold serve specific vegetative faculties, summarizing the roles of the four qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the four primary qualities (heat, cold, dryness, wetness) serve different vegetative faculties in the body.

Core Idea
Not all qualities oppose; cold and dryness have specific supportive roles.

In classical physiology, the four qualities of heat, cold, dryness, and wetness interact with the body's faculties. This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains how each quality serves certain faculties—sometimes in unexpected ways. Let's see how dryness aids motion, moisture aids digestion, and cold aids retention and expulsion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms like الليف العاصر and الريح.
2
Identify which quality serves which faculty
The passage explains how dryness, moisture, cold, and heat serve the attractive, digestive, retentive, and expulsive faculties. Note the specific roles.
Cold is said to serve accidentally, not essentially.
3
Reflect on the counterintuitive role of cold
Cold is normally opposed to life, but here it aids retention and expulsion. Why? Because it thickens wind and tightens fibers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استرخاء بسبب الرطوبة لتعسر الحركة. وحيث كانت الحركة في الجاذبة أقوى كانت حاجتها إلى اليبوسة أشد) والهاضمة لا حاجة لها إلى اليبس بل إلى الرطوبة) المعينة إياه في التفريق والجمع والطبخ والإنضاج والبرودة مع كونها منافية بالذات لأفعال هذه تخدم بالعرض الماسكة بإعانتها على حبس الليف المورب على هيئة الاشتمال الصالح للإمساك، وتخدم كذلك الدافعة بأنها تمنع تحليل الريح المعينة على الدفع وأيضا تغلظها. وكلما كانت الريح أغلظ كانت أعون وأيضا تجمع الليف العاصر وتكثفه فتكون أقوى في الدفع فظهر ما ذكر أن الحرارة تخدم جميع هذه القوى، والبرودة لا تخدم إلا الماسكة والدافعة وأن اليبوسة تخدم ما سوى الهاضمة، والرطوبة تخدمها فقط.
Relaxation due to moisture makes motion difficult. Since the motion in the attractive faculty is stronger, its need for dryness is more intense. The digestive faculty has no need for dryness but rather for moisture, which aids it in breaking up, combining, cooking, and ripening. Cold, although essentially opposed to the actions of these faculties, serves accidentally the retentive by aiding it in holding the twisted fiber (al-lif al-mawrab) in a grasping configuration suitable for retention, and it also serves the expulsive by preventing the dissolution of the wind that aids expulsion and also thickens it. The thicker the wind, the more helpful it is; also cold gathers and compacts the pressing fiber (al-lif al-`āṣir), making it stronger in expulsion. So it becomes clear from what was mentioned that heat serves all these faculties; cold serves only the retentive and expulsive; dryness serves all except the digestive; and moisture serves only the digestive.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage explains the need for dryness and the specific services of the four primary qualities (heat, cold, dryness, wetness) to the vegetative faculties. It begins by noting that relaxation caused by moisture makes movement difficult, implying that dryness is needed for efficient motion. Since the attractive faculty involves strong motion, it has a greater need for dryness to counteract relaxation. In contrast, the digestive faculty requires moisture, not dryness, because moisture helps in breaking down and combining food, as well as cooking and ripening. The passage then discusses cold: although cold inherently opposes the actions of these faculties (since they need heat), it accidentally helps the retentive faculty by aiding in holding the fiber in a grasping shape, and it helps the expulsive faculty by preventing the dissipation of wind (gas) and by thickening it. Thicker wind is more effective for expulsion; additionally, cold contracts the pressing fiber, making it stronger for pushing. Finally, the passage summarizes: heat serves all faculties; cold serves only the retentive and expulsive; dryness serves all except the digestive; and moisture serves only the digestive. This summary clarifies the specific beneficial roles of each quality.

Correction: Cold actually aids the retentive and expulsive faculties accidentally by helping hold fibers and preventing wind dissolution.

?

?

Multiplication of Powers in the Stomach and Liver
Explanation of doubled vegetative powers in stomach and liver

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how vegetative powers are multiplied in organs like the stomach and liver, with two distinct sets of four faculties each.

Core Idea
The stomach and liver contain two sets of four powers: one for the body and one for themselves.

This lesson delves into a key concept from classical Islamic psychology: the multiplication of vegetative powers in certain organs. The stomach, for instance, not only processes food for the entire body but also nourishes itself, requiring two parallel sets of faculties. We will explore the detailed exposition from the philosophers as presented by al-Sharif al-Jurjani, including Imam al-Razi's extension to the entire digestive tract.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the main claim: vegetative powers are multiplied in some organs.
Focus on the example of the stomach and liver.
2
Identify the two sets of four powers in the stomach
In the quotation from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya, note that the stomach has two distinct sets of four powers: one for the body's nutrition and one for its own nutrition. List each power in both sets.
The first set includes attracting, retaining, changing to blood, and expelling; the second set is similar but for the stomach's own substance.
3
Apply the same logic to the liver
Understand that the liver also has two sets: one for transforming food into blood and one for its own nourishment. The second set is present in all organs, while the first is limited to certain organs like the stomach and liver.
4
Consider al-Razi's extension
Imam al-Razi argues that if this theory holds, it should apply to all parts of the alimentary canal, from the mouth to the intestines and mesentery.
5
Review the commentary on motion and dryness
The final part of the passage explains the ordering of powers (attractive, retentive, digestive, expulsive) based on strength of motion and need for dryness.
This commentary connects to earlier discussions of the four powers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبيه الثاني: قد تتضاعف هذه القوى في بعض الأعضاء فالمعدة فيها جاذبة إليها ما يصلح لها وجاذبة أيضاً لغذاء البدن من خارج، وبالجملة فقد تفعل المعدة تارة للإعداد وتهيئة الغذاء لسائر الأعضاء وتارة للاغتذاء وكذا كثير من الأعضاء كالكبد وسائر أدوات الغذاء. وفي المباحث المشرقية: قال بعض الحكماء: إن هذه القوى الأربع توجد في المعدة مضاعفة إحداهما التي تجذب غذاء البدن من خارج إلى تجويف المعدة والتي تمسكه هناك والتي تغيره إلى ما يصلح أن يكون دما والتي تدفعه إلى الكبد والثانية التي تجذب إلى المعدة غذاءها على الخصوص وتمسكه هناك وتغيره إلى جوهرها وتدفع الفضلات عنها، وكذا الحال في الكبد لأن التغيير إلى الدم غير التغيير إلى جوهر الكبد كما أن التغيير إلى العصارة غير التغيير إلى جوهر المعدة، وهذه الثانية موجودة بأجزائها الأربعة في جميع البدن على اختلاف جواهرها وأما في المعدة والكبد فيوجد معها أيضا الأولى بأجزائها الأربعة، ثم قال: قال الإمام الرازي: إن كان هذا حقاً وجب أن يحكم به في الفم واللسان والمري والأمعاء والعروق المسماة بماساريقا بالجملة في جميع أعضاء الغذاء.

قوله: (وحيث كانت الحركة في الجاذبة أقوى) هذا إشارة إلى وجه الترتيب بين والدافعة كما دل عليه بكلمة ثم وحاصله أن حاجة كل منهما إلى اليبوسة إنما هو لأجل التسكين الممكن للتحيرك فما كان تحريكه أقوى كان حاجته إلى اليبوسة أكثر. وقوله: وتخدم أي تخدم البرودة. وقوله: كذلك أي بالعرض وقوله تغلظها من التغليظ أي تغلظ البرودة الريح: المنحنية وهذا من الإعانة. قوله: (إحداهما) أي إحدى الجملتين وهما وإن لم تكونا مذكورتين صريحاً لكنهما كانتا مفهومتين من وقوع قوله: مضاعفة حالا مقيدة لما قبلها ولكل واحدة من الجملتين أجزاء أربعة كما ذكره.
The second notice: These powers may be multiplied in some organs. The stomach has an attractive [power] that attracts what is suitable for it, and also an attractive [power] for the nutrition of the body from outside. In short, the stomach sometimes acts to prepare and ready food for the other organs, and sometimes for its own nutrition. Similarly, many organs like the liver and all the alimentary instruments. In the al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya: Some philosophers said that these four powers exist in the stomach in a doubled manner. One set: that which attracts the body's food from outside into the stomach cavity, that which retains it there, that which changes it into what is suitable to become blood, and that which pushes it to the liver. The second set: that which attracts to the stomach its own food specifically, retains it there, changes it into its own substance, and expels the wastes from it. The same applies to the liver, because changing to blood is different from changing to the substance of the liver, just as changing to chyle is different from changing to the substance of the stomach. This second set, with its four parts, exists in all the body according to the diversity of their substances. As for the stomach and liver, the first set also exists with them with its four parts. Then he said: Imam al-Razi said: If this is true, then the same must be judged for the mouth, tongue, esophagus, intestines, and the veins called mesentery, in short, all the organs of nutrition.

His saying: (And since the motion in the attractive is stronger) – this refers to the aspect of the order between the attractive and expulsive, as indicated by the word 'thumma'. The gist is that the need of each of them for dryness is only for the sake of cessation enabling motion; so whichever has stronger motion has a greater need for dryness. His saying: 'and it serves' means cold serves. His saying: 'similarly' means accidentally. His saying: 'and thickens it' from tathlīth means that cold thickens the wind: the curved one, and this is of assistance. His saying: (one of them) means one of the two groups; although they were not explicitly mentioned, they are understood from the occurrence of his saying 'doubled' as a circumstantial qualifier. Each of the two groups has four parts as he mentioned.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the second warning (tanbīh) regarding the multiplication of vegetative powers in certain organs. The author states that these powers can be multiplied: for example, the stomach has an attractive power for its own appropriate food and another for the body's food from outside. Thus the stomach acts both to prepare food for the rest of the body and for its own nourishment. The same applies to the liver and other alimentary organs. The passage then quotes from the al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya that some philosophers (like Avicenna) say that the four powers exist in the stomach in a doubled manner: one set for the body's nutrition (attract, retain, digest/churn to chyle, and expel), and another set for the stomach's own nutrition (attract its own food, retain, change into its substance, expel wastes). Similarly in the liver, there is a set for blood formation and a set for liver's own substance. The second set (for self-nutrition) exists in all organs, while the first set (for general nutrition) is found only in the stomach and liver (and possibly other primary organs). Then Imam al-Razi is quoted, saying that if this theory is correct, it should apply to all parts of the alimentary tract from mouth to intestines and the mesentery vessels. The passage then includes commentary notes: one explains that the earlier statement about the attractive faculty's stronger motion refers to the order of need for dryness, and the other note clarifies that the word 'one of them' refers to the two groups of four powers each that are understood from the context of 'doubled'.

ماساريقا

Correction: The text explicitly states that the second set (self-nutrition) exists in all organs, and the first set also in the liver. Al-Razi extends this to all digestive organs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition and Classification of the Animal Soul's Powers
Definition and classification of the animal soul's powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the animal soul and its classification into perceiving and moving powers, including the subdivision of perceiving into outward and inward.

Core Idea
The animal soul is defined by its two unique powers: perception and movement.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif introduces the concept of the animal soul (al-nafs al-hayawaniyya) and its distinctive powers. The commentator explains that what makes an animal different from a plant is its possession of psychic powers, which are either perceiving or moving. This classification is foundational for understanding the soul's faculties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the concise style of the commentator.
2
Identify the three types of powers
The passage mentions three types: outward perceiving, inward perceiving, and moving. Locate each in the text.
3
Understand the ordering rationale
The commentator explains why perceiving is mentioned before moving, and outward before inward. Reflect on the dependency: movement depends on will, which depends on perception.
This shows the logical priority in the classification.
4
Note the distinction between outward and inward
Perceiving powers are divided into outward (e.g., senses) and inward (e.g., imagination). This subdivision is important in later discussions.
5
Consider the common misunderstanding
The passage warns against confusing the animal soul with the rational soul. The animal soul is merely the principle of animal life, not intellect.
This clarifies the scope of the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في النفس الحيوانية وتسمى (قواها) التي لا توجد في النبات (نفسانية وهي إما مدركة وإما محركة ) لأن امتياز الحيوان عن مشاركاته في القوى الطبيعية بهاتين القوتين ) والمدركة إما ظاهرة وإما باطنة ) فهذه أنواع ثلاثة : ( النوع الأول : القوى المدركة الظاهرة ) قدم المدركة على المحركة لأن تحريكها إنما هو بالإرادة المتوقفة على الإدراك، وقدم الظاهرة على الباطنة لظهورها
Concerning the animal soul, and its [powers] which are not found in plants are called (psychic powers), and they are either perceiving or moving, because the animal is distinguished from its associates in the natural powers by these two powers. And the perceiving is either outward or inward. So these are three types: (The first type: the outward perceiving powers.) He advanced the perceiving over the moving because their movement is only through the will which depends on perception, and he advanced the outward over the inward because of their manifestness.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage defines the animal soul (النفس الحيوانية) and classifies its powers. The commentator begins by stating that the animal soul has powers not found in plants, called psychic powers (نفسانية). These are of two kinds: perceiving (مدركة) and moving (محركة), which distinguish animals from plants. The perceiving powers are further subdivided into outward (ظاهرة) and inward (باطنة), giving three types total: outward perceiving, inward perceiving, and moving. The commentator then explains the order: the perceiving is mentioned before the moving because movement depends on will, which depends on perception; and the outward is before the inward because it is more manifest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The animal soul is the same as the rational soul.
Correction: The animal soul is only the principle of animal life, excluding intellect. The rational soul is distinct and deals with intellectual cognition.

The Two Opinions on Vision and Aristotle's Intromission Theory
The commentator presents the two opinions on vision and details Aristotle's intromission theory with the cone geometry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Aristotle's intromission theory of vision, including the role of the imaginary cone and how it explains why near objects appear larger.

Core Idea
Vision happens when images impress on the eye's crystalline humor, not by rays leaving the eye.

The philosophers debated how vision occurs, and this lesson introduces the two main opinions, focusing on Aristotle's view. He argued that vision involves the reflection of the seen object's image through the air onto the crystalline humor in the eye, forming an imaginary cone that explains size perception. This theory is a key foundation in classical optics and was widely discussed in Islamic kalam and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions
Read the opening lines to see that the commentator mentions two (or three) opinions on vision. Note that the third is grouped with the second.
The two main opinions are Aristotle's intromission and the emission theory (mentioned later).
2
Understand Aristotle's mechanism
The passage describes how the image of the object reflects through transparent air to the crystalline humor and impresses on a part of it. This part is the vertex of an imaginary cone whose base is the object.
The cone is not physical; it is a geometric aid.
3
Follow the geometric explanation
The commentator explains why near objects appear larger using the angle at the cone's vertex. Closer objects subtend a larger angle, so they impress a larger image on the crystalline humor.
The soul perceives size based on this angle.
4
Connect the theory to everyday experience
Notice that this explanation aligns with common experience: a nearby object looks bigger than the same object far away, even though its actual size is unchanged.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وهي المشاعر) أي الحواس (الخمس الأول البصر وللحكماء فيه أي في الإبصار ( قولان) بل أقول ثلاثة مشهورة إلا أن الثالث قريب من الثاني فذكره المصنف في قرنه وعدهما قولاً واحداً. (الأول: وهو مذهب أرسطو) وأتباعه من الطبيعيين ) أنه إنما يحصل الإبصار ( بانعكاس صورة المرئي بتوسط الهواء المشف) الذي لا لون له فلا يستر ما وراءه إلى الرطوبة الجليدية التي في العين ( وانطباعها في جزء منها) أي من تلك الجليدية ) وذلك الجزء ) الذي تنطبع فيه الصورة ( زاوية ) رأس ( مخروط) متوهم لا وجود له أصلاً، قاعدته سطح المرئي) ورأسه عند الباصرة ( ولذلك ) أي ولأن ( الإبصار بالانطباع على الوجه المذكور دون خروج الشعاع ) يرى القريب أعظم ) من البعيد مع تساويهما في المقدار بحسب نفس الأمر بل مع اتحاد المرئي في حالتي القرب والبعد وذلك ) لأن الوتر الواحد الذي هو امتداد سطح المرئي ( كلما قرب ) النقطة التي خرج منها إليه خطان مستقيمان محيطان بزاوية (كان أقصر ساقاً فأوتر) عند تلك النقطة ( زواية أعظم ، وكلما بعد ) عنها (كان أطول ساقاً فأوتر) عندها ( زواية أصغر كما تشهد به الفطرة السلمية ) والنفس إنما تدرك الصغر والكبر) في المرئي ) وباعتبار تلك الزواية فإنها إذا كانت صغيرة كان الجزء الواقع من الجليدية فيها صغيراً فترتسم صورة المرئي فيه فيرى صغيراً، وإذا كانت كبيرة كان الجزء الواقع فيها كبيراً فترتسم صورته فيه فيرى كبيراً.
(And they are the senses) i.e., the five senses, the first of which is sight (al-basar). And the philosophers have (two opinions) concerning it, i.e., concerning vision, but I say three well-known opinions, except that the third is close to the second, so the author mentioned it in its group and considered them one opinion. (The first: it is the doctrine of Aristotle) and his followers among the naturalists, that vision occurs (by the reflection of the image of the seen object through the transparent air) which has no color and does not conceal what is behind it, to the crystalline humor (al-rutuba al-jalidiyya) which is in the eye, (and its impression in a part of it), i.e., of that humor. And that part (in which the image is impressed is a) vertex of (a cone) imagined, having no actual existence, its base (being the surface of the seen object) and its vertex at the optic nerve. (And therefore), i.e., because (vision is by impression in the mentioned manner without the emission of a ray,) one sees the near (greater) than the far, even if they are equal in magnitude in reality, indeed even with the unity of the seen object in the states of nearness and distance. And that is because the one chord which is the extension of the surface of the seen object, (whenever it approaches) the point from which two straight lines enclosing an angle go out to it, (it becomes shorter in leg and therefore) at that point it subtends (a greater angle; and whenever it recedes) from it, (it becomes longer in leg and therefore) at that point it subtends (a smaller angle, as attested by the sound innate disposition. And the soul perceives smallness and largeness) in the seen object, (and by consideration of that angle. For if it is small, the part of the crystalline humor in which it occurs is small, so the image of the seen object is traced in it and it is seen small; and if it is large, the part in which it occurs is large, so its image is traced in it and it is seen large.)
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage discusses the outward perceiving powers, focusing on the first sense: sight. The commentator notes that the philosophers have two (or three) opinions on how vision occurs. He then details the first opinion, Aristotle's intromission theory. According to this theory, vision happens when the image of the seen object reflects through transparent air to the crystalline humor in the eye and impresses itself on a part of that humor. That part is the vertex of an imaginary cone whose base is the seen object. The commentator explains that this theory accounts for why near objects appear larger than distant ones: the angle at the vertex is larger for closer objects, so the image occupies a larger part of the crystalline humor, resulting in a larger perception. The soul perceives size based on this angle.

إبصار

أرسطو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Aristotle's cone is a physical cone emitted from the eye.
Correction: The cone is imaginary, used only for geometric explanation; no actual physical cone exits the eye.

Checkpoint
According to Aristotle's theory, where does the image of the seen object impress?
A) On the lens
B) On the crystalline humor
C) On the retina
D) On the optic nerve

Key Takeaway
Aristotle's intromission theory explains vision through image impression on the crystalline humor, using an imaginary cone to geometrically account for perceived size.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you look at the verses of Allah in creation, do you realize that your own perception is a sign of His design? How does understanding the mechanism of sight increase your awe of the Creator who gave you this faculty?
?
The passage explains that the soul perceives size based on the angle subtended, not the actual size. Are there areas in your life where your perception of 'size'—of trials, blessings, or sins—is distorted by your distance from them? What would it take to see them as they truly are?
?
Our innate disposition (fiṭra) attests to the geometric explanation of vision. In what ways has your fiṭra been clouded by worldly distractions? What step can you take today to realign your fitra with the truth that Allah has placed in your nature?

The Critique of the Emission Theory of Vision
The commentator argues against the emission theory of vision using geometrical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's argument against the emission theory of vision and why it fails to explain size variation.

Core Idea
The emission theory cannot account for size constancy, revealing a critical flaw in its logic.

The classical debate between emission and intromission theories of vision involved complex geometrical models. The commentator here focuses on a key problem: if the eye emits rays forming a cone, the base of the cone would be the seat of vision, yet this fails to explain why objects appear smaller at a distance. This passage presents a critical examination of that inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of geometric terms like 'base' and 'vertex' of the cone.
2
Identify the two theories
The passage contrasts two theories: one where vision occurs at the vertex (intromission) and another where it occurs at the base of the cone (emission). Identify which is being critiqued.
The commentator aligns with intromission theory, where the angle's vertex is the seat of vision.
3
Analyze the objection to emission theory
The commentator argues that if the base of the cone were the seat of vision, objects would appear the same size regardless of distance. This contradicts everyday experience. Understand this logical step.
This is the core argument against emission theory.
4
Consider the counter-critique to intromission
The commentator then points out that even the intromission theory faces a potential problem: if vision is by impression, one might expect no size variation with distance. However, he notes that their cone model actually allows for such variation.
This shows the commentator's balanced approach, not simply dismissing one theory.
5
Reflect on the implications
Think about how this debate shows the complexity of explaining vision. Both theories have strengths and weaknesses, and the commentator's analysis helps refine understanding.
This connects to the broader theme of scientific reasoning in classical Islamic thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن المعلوم أن هذا إنما يستقيم إذا جعلت الزاوية موضعاً للإبصار كما ذهبنا إليه وأما إذا جعل موضع الإبصار قاعدة المخروط كما يقتضيه القول بخروج الشعاع، فيجب أن يرى الجسم كما هو سواء خرجت الخطوط الشعاعية من زاوية ضيقة أو غير ضيقة، هكذا قالوا وفيه بحث لأن الإبصار ليس حاصلاً بمجرد القاعدة بل لرأس المخروط فيه مدخل أيضاً فجاز أن يتفاوت حال المرئي صغراً وكبراً بتفاوت رأسه دقة وغلظاً. ألا ترى أن الإبصار إن كان بالانطباع كما زعموه كان الظاهر أن لا يتفاوت حال المرئي في الصغر والكبر بالقرب والبعد، لكن لما كان الانطباع على ما صوروه من توهم المخروط جاز أن يظهر التفاوت فيه بحسبهما .
And it is known that this is only consistent if we make the vertex the place of vision, as we have gone; but if we make the place of vision the base of the cone, as required by the theory of the emission of rays, then the body would necessarily be seen as it is, whether the radiating lines emerge from a narrow or a wide angle. Thus they said, and there is in it discussion, because vision is not achieved by the base alone, but the vertex of the cone also has an entry, so it is possible that the state of the seen object differs in smallness and largeness according to the difference of its vertex in fineness and coarseness. Do you not see that if vision was by impression as they claimed, it would be apparent that the state of the seen object does not differ in smallness and largeness with nearness and distance; but since the impression is as they pictured it from imagining the cone, it is possible that the difference appears in it according to them.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents a counterargument to the intromission theory by considering the emission theory. The commentator explains that if the seat of vision were the base of the cone (as in emission theory), then the object would always appear the same size regardless of distance. This contradicts experience. He then critiques this: even if the base were the seat, the vertex might still affect size perception. He further points out a potential problem for intromission: if vision is by impression, one might expect no size variation at all, but their cone model actually allows variation.

Correction: While the emission theory fails to explain size variation, the intromission theory also faces a potential problem: if vision is by impression, size should not vary with distance, but their cone model actually allows variation.

Key Takeaway
The commentator uses geometric reasoning to challenge the emission theory of vision, highlighting that size variation remains unexplained if the base of the cone is the seat of vision.
