The Afterimage Argument for Intromission
The commentator gives two afterimage examples as proof for intromission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the afterimage of the sun and the green meadow phenomenon serve as empirical evidence for the intromission theory of vision.

Core Idea
Afterimages are physical evidence that vision occurs by impression.

The commentator now presents a concrete empirical proof for the intromission theory. By examining what happens after staring at bright or colorful objects, he argues that images are physically impressed in the eye. This passage uses common experience to make a theoretical point about the nature of vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Let the examples sink in.
2
Identify the two afterimage examples
The passage gives two cases: staring at the sun and staring at a green meadow. Notice what happens in each.
3
Understand what each example shows
In the sun example, the image persists after closing eyes. In the meadow example, the eyes are affected by the greenness, causing afterimages with other colors.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The commentator argues that this persistence and adaptation would not happen if vision were by emission. It supports that images are impressed in the eye.
5
Consider the alternative theory
Think about the emission theory (rays from the eye). Why would afterimages not occur under that theory? The passage implies the emission theory cannot explain these phenomena.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و)دل على صحة القول الأول أن ( من نظر إلى الشمس ) بتحديق وإمعان ) نظراً طويلاً ثم أعرض عنها ) وغمض عينيه ( فإنها تبقى صورتها في العين مدة ما حتى كأنه بعد التغميض ينظر إليها وكذا من نظر إلى الروضة المخضرة ) جداً ساعة طويلة نظراً بتدقيق، فإن عينيه يتكيفان بتلك الخضرة حتى إذا نظر إلى لون آخر لا يبصره خالصاً بل مخلوطاً بالخضرة أو غمض عينيه فإنه يجده كأنه ناظر إليها فلولا أن الإبصار بانطباع صورة المرئي لما كان الأمر كذلك
(And) indicates the correctness of the first opinion that (whoever looks at the sun) with staring and intent, looking for a long time, then turns away from it and closes his eyes, (the image of it remains in his eye for a while, as if after closing his eyes he looks at it). Likewise, whoever looks at a very green meadow for a long time with careful looking, his eyes become affected by that greenness, so that if he looks at another color, he does not see it purely but mixed with greenness, or if he closes his eyes, he finds it as if he is looking at it. Were it not that vision is by the impression of the image of the seen object, it would not be so.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage provides the first proof for the intromission theory: empirical evidence from afterimages. When one looks at the sun and then closes the eyes, the image remains, which would not happen if vision were by emission. Similarly, looking at a green meadow causes color adaptation, and the afterimage persists even with eyes closed. This shows that the image is physically impressed in the eye, supporting the impression theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you close your eyes after staring at something bright, the afterimage is undeniable. This physical trace reminds you that your eyes are not passive but receptacles of what Allāh has placed before you. How often do you reflect on the fact that your very sight is an āyah of Allāh’s power, and that every image you see leaves an impression on your soul? What one image from this past week do you wish had never entered your eyes? Commit today to guarding your gaze.
?
The afterimage proves that what you see physically affects your eyes. But spiritually, what you look at also imprints on your heart. The Prophet ﷺ said, 'The eyes commit zinā and their zinā is the look.' If afterimages are evidence for intromission, how much more do the images you gaze upon—on screens, in public, in your own home—leave their mark on your soul? What unchecked habit of looking are you willing to change starting right now?
?
This lesson uses a simple afterimage to prove that vision works by impression. But are you content to merely understand this intellectually? Your sight is an amānah (trust) from Allāh. Stand before Him and ask: what impression will the images I see today leave on my heart—will they be like the pure green of a meadow, or the harmful glare of distractions? Make one concrete plan tonight to replace a useless or harmful look with a beneficial one tomorrow.

Analogy of Vision with Other Senses
Analogy between vision and other senses to support intromission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy between vision and other senses supports the intromission theory and refutes the emission theory.

Core Idea
Vision, like other senses, receives impressions rather than emitting something.

The commentator presents a second proof for the intromission theory by drawing an analogy between sight and the other outward senses. Just as hearing and smell perceive by receiving the object, so must vision. This simple comparison provides a compelling indication that the emission theory is false. Let's examine the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalysis, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: the analogy is stated, then explained, then applied to vision.
The key term 'uswa' (أسوة) means 'analogy' or 'example'.
2
Identify the analogy and its components
Identify what the other senses have in common: they perceive by the object coming to them, not by something exiting. Then see how this is applied to vision.
3
Understand the conclusion and its significance
The conclusion is that vision must also work by the image coming to the eye, which supports intromission and refutes the emission theory. Reflect on why this analogy is considered a strong indication.
This lesson is one part of a larger proof for intromission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( و ) مما يدل على صحته أيضاً أن يقال: (له) أي للبصر في إدراكه ( أسوة بسائر الحواس) الظاهرة (إذ ليس إدراكها لمدركاتها ( بأن يخرج منها شيء ويتصل ) ذلك الشيء (بالمحسوس، بل ) إدراكها إياها إنما هو لأن المحسوس يأتيها فوجب أن لا يكون الإحساس بالبصر لخروج شيء منه إلى المبصر بل لأن صورته تأتيه فدل ذلك على صحة الانطباع وفساد الشعاع
(And) what also indicates its correctness is that it can be said: (it), i.e., sight in its perception, (has an analogy with the other) outward (senses), since their perception of their perceptibles (is not by something coming out of them and connecting) that thing (to the sensible, but) rather their perception is only because the sensible comes to them. So it must be that the sensation of sight is not by something coming out of it to the seen object, but because its image comes to it. So that indicates the correctness of impression and the corruption of the ray.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage offers a second proof: an analogy (uswa) between sight and other outward senses like hearing and smell. In those senses, perception occurs because the sensible object comes to the sense organ, not because something leaves the organ. Therefore, the same should apply to vision: the image of the seen object comes to the eye, not a ray going out. This supports intromission and refutes the emission theory.

Key Terms

الحواس الظاهرة
خروج الشيء
المحسوس يأتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think vision is unique and cannot be compared to other senses.
Correction: The analogy shows that the common principle of perception applies to all senses, making the comparison valid.

Checkpoint

B) All senses receive the object
C) All senses are based on rays
D) Only vision uses a medium

Key Takeaway
The analogy between vision and other senses reinforces the intromission theory by showing that all senses operate by receiving the object rather than emitting something.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The analogy shows that all senses are passive receivers. Are you mindful that what you hear, see, and feel shapes you from the outside in? What will you change today about the sensory inputs you allow into your life?

Three Objections to the Intromission Proofs
Three objections to the proofs for intromission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three logical objections that challenge the empirical proofs for the intromission theory of vision.

Core Idea
Empirical proofs for intromission face serious logical objections that cannot be dismissed by ignorance.

We have seen two proofs for intromission: the size variation with distance and the afterimage, plus an analogy with other senses. But the commentator now presents three objections that undermine these proofs. These objections force us to reconsider whether the empirical evidence truly supports intromission. Pay close attention to each objection's premise and conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the three objections
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that each objection begins with 'وأن يقال على' (and it can be said concerning). Identify the target of each: first objection attacks the size variation argument, second attacks the afterimage argument, third attacks the analogy.
2
Analyze the first objection
The first objection suggests that size variation may have another cause besides impression size. This is a powerful logical point: absence of evidence is not evidence of absence. Why must the cause be impression size?
This objection undermines the causal inference from size variation to impression size.
3
Analyze the second objection
The second objection claims the afterimage persists in the imagination, not in the crystalline humor. Note the empirical observation: closing the eyes does not change the afterimage experience. What does that indicate?
If the image were physically impressed on the eye, closing the eyes should change it immediately.
4
Analyze the third objection
The third objection attacks the analogy with other senses. It says the analogy lacks a valid commonality (jami'), because vision might work by emission, not intromission. This challenges the inductive reasoning from other senses to vision.
5
Reflect on the overall significance
Consider why these objections are serious. They show that the intromission theory is not conclusively proven by these empirical arguments. The debate remains open.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ويمكن أن يقال على ( الدليل الأول : لعله ) أي لعل ما ذكرتموه من تفاوت المرئي الواحد في الكبر والصغر بالقرب والبعد ( لسبب آخر لا لانطباعه في جزء أكبر أو أصغر فإن عدم العلم به لا يوجب عدمه (و) أن يقال ( على الثاني : إن الصورة ) أي صورة الشمس أو الروضة ( إنما تبقى في الخيال دون الجليدية، ألا ترى أنه لا يتفاوت الحال بالتغميض والإبصار في هذه الحالة قطعاً (و) أن يقال (على الثالث أنه تمثيل) وقياس للبصر على الحواس الأخر ( بلا جامع) معتبر إذ من الجائز أن يكون إدراك هذه الحاسة بخروج شيء منها إلى مدركها دون باقي الحواس الظاهرة
(And it can be said concerning the first argument: perhaps) what you mentioned of the difference of a single seen object in largeness and smallness with nearness and distance (is due to another cause, not to its impression in a larger or smaller part, for lack of knowledge of it does not necessitate its non-existence. And it can be said concerning the second: the image), i.e., the image of the sun or the meadow, (only remains in the imagination (al-khayal) and not in the crystalline humor. Do you not see that the state does not differ with closing the eyes and vision in this case definitely? And it can be said concerning the third: it is a representation (tamthil) and analogy of sight to the other senses (without a valid commonality (jami')), for it is possible that the perception of this sense is by something exiting from it to its perceptible, unlike the rest of the outward senses.)
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the proofs for intromission. First: the observed size variation might be due to another cause, not necessarily the size of the impression; ignorance of that cause does not disprove it. Second: the afterimage might persist in the imagination (al-khayal), not in the crystalline humor; note that the experience does not change whether eyes are open or closed. Third: the analogy with other senses is merely a representation (tamthil) without a valid commonality (jami'), because vision could be unique in using an emission mechanism.

Key Terms
سبب آخر
الخيال
التمثيل
الجامع المعتبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The afterimage is direct evidence that a physical impression remains on the eye's lens.
Correction: The second objection shows that the afterimage persists even when eyes are closed, suggesting it is mental, not physical.

Checkpoint
According to the second objection, where does the afterimage remain?
A) In the crystalline humor
B) In the lens
C) In the imagination
D) On the retina

Key Takeaway
These three objections show that the empirical proofs for intromission are not conclusive, leaving room for alternative theories like emission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a convincing argument that has objections you cannot answer, do you dismiss the objections simply because you lack knowledge? How can this passage remind you to practice intellectual humility and investigate further?
?
Think of a belief you hold about your own practices. Are you aware of potential counterarguments? Will you examine them fairly rather than assuming they are invalid?
?
The third objection warns against invalid analogies. In your own reasoning about ethical or spiritual matters, where might you be using an analogy that lacks a valid commonality? What will you do about it?

The Sense of Smell: Two Theories and Their Assessment
Defines smell, presents two theories, and answers objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two competing theories of how smell works—the particle theory (tahallul) and the qualification theory (takayyuf)—and the evidence used to support the latter, along with responses to objections.

Core Idea
Smell is a power, but how it reaches us is debated: particles or air qualification.

This passage from al-Mawaqif defines the sense of smell and then presents a debate about its mechanism. Two main theories are introduced: one holds that tiny particles from the odorous body dissolve and mix with the air; the other holds that the air itself becomes qualified by the odor without any particles. The author defends the second theory using evidence like the musk example. He also addresses two objections from the particle theorists. As you read, notice how the argument uses empirical observations to support a theoretical claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and two theories
Start by reading the opening where smell is defined as a power in the brain. Then identify the two theories: one based on dissolution (tahallul) and the other on qualification (takayyuf).
The Arabic terms are key: التحلل and التكيف.
2
Understand the evidence for the second theory
The author gives two pieces of evidence: odor weakens with distance, and a small piece of musk perfumes many places without losing weight. Think about why these support qualification over particles.
Notice the logic: if particles were lost, musk weight would decrease.
3
Examine the objections from the first group
The particle theorists raise two objections: heat/rubbing intensify odor, and apples wither from smelling. The author refutes each. Work through the refutations carefully.
The refutations show that these phenomena can be explained without particle loss.
4
Read the commentator's marginal notes
After the core text, notes clarify ambiguous points, such as the possibility of external parts replacing lost ones in musk, and the meaning of 'perception and qualification'.
These notes deepen your understanding of the debate's subtleties.

(الثاني التفاحة تذبل من كثرة الشم) فلولا أنه يتحلل شيء منها لم يكن كذلك. قلنا :) ( ليس ذبولها من كثرته بل من وصول النفس إليها . وكثرة اللمس ) فإنهما يحللانها ( وأما مجرد انتشار (الرائحة ) منها ( فلا ) يحللها ( وإلا لم يتفاوت) مع الانتشار ( الشم وعدمه ) وهو باطل قطعاً . قوله : ( وأيد ذلك بأن ذا (الرائحة وهذا غير ظاهر فإن مثل ذكر يوجد في صورة تحلل أجزاء ذي الرائحة وتبخره ومخالطته للأقرب فالأقرب من الهواء كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( ولو كان ذلك يتحلل منه لامتنع ذلك قيل : هذا ممنوع لم لا يجوز أن يحصل في ذلك المسك أجزاء من خارج وتكون تلك الأجزاء منضمة إلى المسك بدلاً لما يتحلل منه ومتكيفه بكيفية المسك. قوله : ( يدكها) الذك الدق والكسر ، وقوله : إدراكاً واتصافاً نشر على ترتيب اللف المذكور أعني قوله : بعد الشامة والأهوية المتوسطة.
The third sensor: smell, and it is a power stored in two protrusions at the front of the brain like the nipples of the breast. Some claimed that odor reaches it—i.e., to this sensor—by dissolution of parts of the odorous body and its vaporization and its mixing with the intermediate medium—i.e., the air between the smelling power and that body. Others claimed that the intermediate air becomes qualified by that quality, nearer and nearer until it reaches what is adjacent to the location of this power, and then it perceives it, without any parts of the odorous body mixing with it. And they supported that by the fact that the farther the odorous body is, the weaker the perceived odor is, because each part of the air is only affected by the odor from its neighbor, and there is no doubt that the quality of the affected is weaker than the quality of the effecter. And this is the truth, because a small piece of musk perfumes many places and that continues for the duration of its existence, and its weight does not decrease from what it was. If that were by dissolution from it, that would be impossible. And you know that this only refutes the confinement of smell to the first mode, and does not contradict its occurrence in each of the two modes sometimes together and sometimes alternatively, as some investigators mentioned. The first group argued with two proofs. The first: heat excites and stirs up odors, and likewise rubbing and vaporization intensify and spread them, while cold condenses and hides them, so that indicates that smell is by dissolution. We said: we do not accept what you mentioned; rather, heat and its sisters prepare them—i.e., prepare the smelling faculty and the intermediate air between it and the odorous body—for receiving odor, perception and qualification, either by their effect on the air and preparing it for being qualified by odor, or by their effect on the instrument and preparing it for smell. The second: the apple withers from much smelling; if nothing dissolved from it, it would not be so. We said: its withering is not from the abundance of smelling, but from the arrival of breath to it and much touching, for these two dissolve it. As for mere spread of odor from it, it does not dissolve it; otherwise, there would be no difference between smelling and not smelling with spread, which is absolutely false. His saying: 'And he supported that by the fact that the odorous...' This is not apparent, for the likes of musk exist in the form of dissolution of parts of the odorous body and its vaporization and mixing with the nearest of the air, as is not hidden. His saying: 'If that were by dissolution from it, that would be impossible.' It is said: this is debatable; why is it not permissible that there occur in that musk parts from outside, and those parts are annexed to the musk as a substitute for what dissolves from it, and become qualified by the quality of musk? His saying: 'yudakkihuha' — 'al-dhakku' means pounding and breaking. And his saying: 'perception and qualification' is a disjunction on the order of the mentioned word pair, namely his saying: 'after the smelling faculty and the intermediate air.'
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage defines the sense of smell and presents a debate about how it occurs. It begins with a definition: smell is a power located in two protrusions at the front of the brain. Then it presents two theories. The first theory holds that odor is perceived by particles (تحلل) from the odorous body that dissolve, vaporize, and mix with the air to reach the sensor. The second theory holds that the air itself becomes qualified (تكيف) by the odor without any particles mixing. The author supports the second theory with two proofs: first, the farther the odorous body, the weaker the smell, which suggests transmission through successive qualification of air; second, a small piece of musk perfumes many places without losing weight, which would not happen if it were losing particles. The author notes that this does not rule out both ways occurring together or alternately. Then two objections from the proponents of the particle theory are addressed. First objection: heat and rubbing intensify odors, showing particles are released. Answer: these factors prepare the air or the sensory instrument for reception, not necessarily release particles. Second objection: an apple withers from repeated smelling, indicating loss of substance. Answer: withering is from breath and touch, not from mere smell. The marginal notes added by the commentator clarify the arguments and respond to subtle points, such as the possibility of external particles replacing lost ones in musk.

التحلل
التكيف
المسك
القوة الشامة
الهواء المتوسط
الرائحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The musk argument proves that smell cannot involve any particle loss.
Correction: The author explicitly allows both modes to occur together or alternately, so the musk argument only refutes the exclusive confinement to the first mode.

Checkpoint
What empirical argument does the author use to support the theory that smell occurs by qualification of the air rather than by particle loss?
A) The odor of musk is stronger when closer.
B) The temperature of a body affects its odor.
C) A piece of musk perfumes many places without losing weight.
D) The apple withers from repeated smelling.

Key Takeaway
The sense of smell is explained by two competing theories; the qualification theory is supported by empirical evidence like the musk example, but both mechanisms may occur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a view that contradicts your own, do you immediately try to refute it, or do you honestly examine the evidence as the author does here? What would it take for you to change your mind on a deeply held belief?
?
The author admits that both mechanisms of smell may occur together or alternately. Is there an area in your life where you cling to a single explanation when multiple possibilities could be true? How might your certainty be holding you back from deeper understanding?
?
The musk argument shows that observable facts (no weight loss) can refute a theory. Think of a belief you hold that lacks observable evidence. Are you willing to test it against facts today, or do you prefer comfortable assumptions?

The Sense of Taste and the Role of Saliva
Defines taste and explains saliva's role in perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of taste as a power spread over the tongue, the essential role of tasteless saliva in perception, and the two possible mechanisms by which taste is conveyed.

Core Idea
Taste perception depends on tasteless saliva; without it, accurate taste is impossible.

This passage introduces the sense of taste as the fourth external sensor. The author explains how the power of taste resides in the nerves of the tongue and requires saliva to function. He then presents two theories about how saliva mediates the perception, and discusses what happens when saliva is tainted.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of taste
Read the first part where taste is defined as a power spread in the tongue's nerves. Notice the role of saliva as the medium.
Saliva is tasteless in itself, which is crucial.
2
Examine the two mechanisms
The passage describes two possible ways saliva transmits taste: by mixing with particles or by becoming qualified without mixing. Consider the difference.
3
Understand the distortion effect
Read the example of the sick person whose bile-tainted saliva makes sweet things taste bitter. This illustrates what happens when saliva is not tasteless.
4
Summarize the role of saliva
Reflect on why tasteless saliva is necessary for accurate taste perception.
Saliva is not just a solvent; it actively conveys the taste.

The fourth sensor: taste, and it is a power spread in the nerve spread over the substance of the tongue. This power perceives tastes only by means of moisture emitted from the instrument called the salivary gland (al-mal'abah), which is tasteless—i.e., free in itself from all tastes—mixing with the tasted object. It is possible that its intermediacy is by parts from the tasted object spreading in it, then it dives into the tongue, and then the tasting faculty perceives its taste. In that case, there is no benefit in that moisture except facilitating the arrival of the sensed bearing tastes to the sensing power, and the sensation would be by contact with the sensed object without an intermediary. Or its intermediacy could be by that moisture becoming qualified by tastes without mixing, so the actual sensed object in that case is the moisture, sensed without intermediary. So if the salivary moisture is tasteless, as is its state in itself, it conveys tastes from bodies to the tasting faculty correctly, so it perceives them as they are. And if a taste mixes with it—either by being qualified by it or by parts from its bearer mixing with it—it does not convey them correctly, but mixed with that taste, as is the case with the sick whose saliva is dominated by yellow bile: they find insipid water and sweet sugar bitter. And hence—i.e., because if a taste mixes with it, it does not convey tastes correctly but mixed with what mixed with it—some said: tastes have no existence in the tasted object—i.e., in what is commonly known as having taste, like honey for example—but rather tastes exist in the tasting faculty and the instrument bearing it. And similarly other qualities: heat is only known to exist by sensation, and what sensation gives and testifies to is its existence in the organ in which the touching power is located upon contact with fire. As for its existence in fire, it is an illusion derived from the fact that it—i.e., fire—does not act nor effect in other than it except by assimilation (tashbih), i.e., producing a likeness that exists in it. And on this, if fire were not hot in itself, it would not heat other than it. And this illusion collapses by reflection on the heating of motion for the moving thing despite its lack of heat in itself. The answer is that it is a denial of sensible things whose existence in their loci is known without doubt, and it is sophistry manifestly false, which does not deserve an answer by showing the defect in its premises, because its contradiction with the necessary is sufficient for that. His saying: 'by al-mal'abah'—this is from saliva (al-lu'ab), not from play (al-la'b). His saying: 'then it dives (yaghussu) into the tongue'—it is said: ghassa fi al-ma'i, i.e., descended to its bottom; and the overt pronoun in 'khalatatha' and the covert one in 'tatakayyafu' refer to the salivary moisture. His saying: 'and the instrument bearing it'—i.e., the locus bearing it. His saying: 'innahu mutafarriqhun'—i.e., poured out; it is said: faragha al-ma'u, i.e., poured. His saying: 'shadidan' is the predicate of 'kāna'.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage defines taste and discusses the role of saliva. The power of taste is spread over the nerves of the tongue. Taste is perceived through saliva, which is tasteless in itself. There are two possible mechanisms: either particles from the tasted object mix with saliva and then contact the tongue, or the saliva itself becomes qualified by the taste without mixing. If saliva is tasteless, it conveys tastes accurately; if it is already tinged with another taste (as in illness), the perception is distorted. The passage then reports a view that tastes do not actually exist in external objects but only in the perceiving faculty. This is extended to all sensible qualities, like heat. The example of fire is discussed: heat is only known by sensation in the touching organ, and its supposed existence in fire is an illusion based on the principle of assimilation. A counterargument notes that if fire were not hot, it could not heat other things. The answer is that motion heats without itself being hot. The commentator labels this denial of the senses as sheer sophistry.

Key Terms
الذوق
الرطوبة اللعابية
المذوق
الطعوم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taste is directly perceived by the tongue without any medium.
Correction: The passage shows that tasteless saliva is necessary for accurate taste perception; without it, direct contact would not be efficient or correct.

Checkpoint
What happens to taste perception when the salivary moisture has its own taste?

Key Takeaway
Tasteless saliva is essential for accurate taste perception; any impurity in saliva distorts the perceived taste.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found a truth from Allah—which is sweet in essence—bitter to your soul? What does that reveal about the state of your inner 'saliva'? What concrete step can you take today to purify your heart so that you receive truth as it truly is?
?
The sick man finds sweet sugar bitter because his saliva is tainted. In your daily life, what attitudes or habits might be tainting your perception of the good? Are you willing to ask someone to help you see where you might be distorting reality?
?
If tasteless saliva is essential for accurate taste, what must be removed from your soul for you to taste the sweetness of faith? What specific 'taste' in your heart needs to be neutralized so that you can correctly perceive the commands of Allah?

The Nature of Sensible Qualities: Are They in Objects or in the Perceiver?
Presents and refutes the view that sensible qualities exist only in the perceiver.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that sensible qualities like taste and heat may only exist in the perceiving faculty, not in external objects, and why this view is rejected as sophistry.

Core Idea
Sensible qualities do not exist in external objects; they are products of the perceiving faculty.

This passage presents a radical philosophical view: tastes and other qualities like heat exist only in the perceiver, not in the objects themselves. The author uses the example of fire and heat to illustrate the reasoning behind this view, but then refutes it as a denial of the senses and manifest sophistry.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim about tastes
The passage reports the claim that tastes have no existence in tasted objects like honey, but only in the tasting faculty.
This is an extreme empiricist position.
2
Study the extension to other qualities
The claim is extended to all sensible qualities, with heat as an example. Heat is only known by sensation in the organism, not in fire itself.
The argument uses the principle of assimilation to explain why fire seems hot.
3
Consider the counterargument
The author presents a counter: if fire were not hot in itself, it could not heat other things. But then he shows that this counter is weak because motion heats without being hot.
The heating of motion is a key counterexample.
4
Understand the refutation
The author dismisses this entire view as a denial of the senses and sophistry, not worthy of detailed rebuttal because it contradicts necessary truths.
This shows the importance of sensory evidence in kalam.

(لو لم تكن النار حارة ) في نفسها ( لما سخنت) غيرها (وهو) أي هذا الوهم ( يضمحل ) ويتلاشى ( بالتأمل في تسخين الحركة ) للمتحرك ) مع عدم حرارتها في نفسها. ) والجوب أنه إنكار للمحسوسات) التي علم وجودها في محالها بلا شبهة (وسفسطة ) ظاهرة البطلان ) لا تستحق الجواب ) بإظهار الخلل في مقدماتها لأن متصادمتها للضرورة كافية في ذلك ( قوله : ( بالملعبة) هذا من اللعاب لا من اللعب. وقوله : ثم يغوص في اللسان يقال : غاص في الماء أي نزل في قعره والضمير البارز في خالطها والمستتر في تتكيف راجع إلى الرطوبة اللعابية . وقوله : والآلة الحاملة لها أي المحل الحامل لها . وقوله : فإنه متفرغ أي منصب يقال : فرغ الماء أي انصب . وقوله : شديداً خبر كان .
The fourth sensor: taste, and it is a power spread in the nerve spread over the substance of the tongue. This power perceives tastes only by means of moisture emitted from the instrument called the salivary gland (al-mal'abah), which is tasteless—i.e., free in itself from all tastes—mixing with the tasted object. It is possible that its intermediacy is by parts from the tasted object spreading in it, then it dives into the tongue, and then the tasting faculty perceives its taste. In that case, there is no benefit in that moisture except facilitating the arrival of the sensed bearing tastes to the sensing power, and the sensation would be by contact with the sensed object without an intermediary. Or its intermediacy could be by that moisture becoming qualified by tastes without mixing, so the actual sensed object in that case is the moisture, sensed without intermediary. So if the salivary moisture is tasteless, as is its state in itself, it conveys tastes from bodies to the tasting faculty correctly, so it perceives them as they are. And if a taste mixes with it—either by being qualified by it or by parts from its bearer mixing with it—it does not convey them correctly, but mixed with that taste, as is the case with the sick whose saliva is dominated by yellow bile: they find insipid water and sweet sugar bitter. And hence—i.e., because if a taste mixes with it, it does not convey tastes correctly but mixed with what mixed with it—some said: tastes have no existence in the tasted object—i.e., in what is commonly known as having taste, like honey for example—but rather tastes exist in the tasting faculty and the instrument bearing it. And similarly other qualities: heat is only known to exist by sensation, and what sensation gives and testifies to is its existence in the organ in which the touching power is located upon contact with fire. As for its existence in fire, it is an illusion derived from the fact that it—i.e., fire—does not act nor effect in other than it except by assimilation (tashbih), i.e., producing a likeness that exists in it. And on this, if fire were not hot in itself, it would not heat other than it. And this illusion collapses by reflection on the heating of motion for the moving thing despite its lack of heat in itself. The answer is that it is a denial of sensible things whose existence in their loci is known without doubt, and it is sophistry manifestly false, which does not deserve an answer by showing the defect in its premises, because its contradiction with the necessary is sufficient for that. His saying: 'by al-mal'abah'—this is from saliva (al-lu'ab), not from play (al-la'b). His saying: 'then it dives (yaghussu) into the tongue'—it is said: ghassa fi al-ma'i, i.e., descended to its bottom; and the overt pronoun in 'khalatatha' and the covert one in 'tatakayyafu' refer to the salivary moisture. His saying: 'and the instrument bearing it'—i.e., the locus bearing it. His saying: 'innahu mutafarriqhun'—i.e., poured out; it is said: faragha al-ma'u, i.e., poured. His saying: 'shadidan' is the predicate of 'kāna'.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage defines taste and discusses the role of saliva. The power of taste is spread over the nerves of the tongue. Taste is perceived through saliva, which is tasteless in itself. There are two possible mechanisms: either particles from the tasted object mix with saliva and then contact the tongue, or the saliva itself becomes qualified by the taste without mixing. If saliva is tasteless, it conveys tastes accurately; if it is already tinged with another taste (as in illness), the perception is distorted. The passage then reports a view that tastes do not actually exist in external objects but only in the perceiving faculty. This is extended to all sensible qualities, like heat. The example of fire is discussed: heat is only known by sensation in the touching organ, and its supposed existence in fire is an illusion based on the principle of assimilation. A counterargument notes that if fire were not hot, it could not heat other things. The answer is that motion heats without itself being hot. The commentator labels this denial of the senses as sheer sophistry.

Key Terms
الطعوم
الكيفيات
الحرارة
التشبيه
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensible qualities like heat and taste are mere illusions created by the mind; they have no reality in external objects.
Correction: The passage rejects this as sophistry (safsata) and 'denial of sensible things' (inkār al-mahsūsāt).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author consider the view that tastes exist only in the tasting faculty to be false?
A) Because it contradicts the Qur'an
B) Because it is a denial of the senses and sophistry
C) Because it is logically inconsistent
D) Because it is based on weak evidence

Key Takeaway
The view that sensible qualities exist only in the perceiver is dismissed as sophistry because it contradicts direct sensory evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed obvious evidence—like a clear sign from Allah or a truth you could feel—because accepting it would force you to change your comfortable worldview? What was that sign, and what have you done about it?
?
When you experience the heat of fire or the sweetness of honey, do you ever question whether those qualities are real? What does that say about how much you trust the senses Allah gave you to recognize His creation?
?
If the way you think about a simple truth like heat can be so easily twisted into sophistry, how much more careful must you be when reasoning about matters of faith? What is one belief in your heart that you have accepted without questioning, simply because it feels self-evident?

Touch is a power spread in the nerves, covering the whole body.

In this passage, the author defines the fifth external sense: touch. Rather than being located in a single organ, touch is described as a power spread throughout the body via the nerves, especially in the skin. This definition highlights the unique nature of touch among the senses.

Mechanism of Taste and Refutation of Skepticism
The author explains the role of saliva in taste and refutes skepticism about objective flavors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the taste faculty operates through a tasteless saliva medium and why the skeptical denial of objective tastes is untenable.

Core Idea
Taste perception depends on a tasteless medium; denying objective qualities is sophistry against self-evident truth.

In this passage, the author delves into the physiology of taste, explaining the role of the salivary glands. He then addresses a skeptical objection that questions whether flavors exist in objects at all. Understanding this clarifies the Islamic epistemological stance on sensory knowledge and distinguishes between valid perception and sophistry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without breaking it down. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice that the passage combines a physiological explanation with philosophical refutation.
2
Identify the role of saliva
The author states that the taste faculty perceives tastes via a moisture from the salivary gland, which must be tasteless in itself. Look for the two possibilities of how this moisture mediates.
The two possibilities are: (1) particles mix with saliva, then contact tongue; (2) saliva becomes qualified by taste without mixture.
3
Understand the distorting effect
The passage gives an example: a person with yellow bile tastes sweet things as bitter. This shows that if saliva has a taste, it distorts perception.
This illustrates why saliva must be tasteless for accurate taste.
4
Analyze the skeptical objection and response
Some claim that tastes exist only in the perceiver, not in objects. The author refutes this as sophistry, arguing that it contradicts self-evident sensory knowledge.
The author uses the analogy of heat: if fire weren't hot, it couldn't heat. Sensation directly affirms qualities in their subjects.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the skeptical argument is not worth detailed refutation because it contradicts immediate experience.
This reinforces the reliability of sensory knowledge in Islamic theology.

Then there is no benefit in that moisture except facilitating the arrival of the sensed object carrying tastes to the sensing faculty, and sensation occurs by contact with the sensed object without mediation. And its intermediation could be by that moisture being qualified by tastes without mixture, so the truly sensed object then is the moisture sensed without mediation. But if a taste mixes with it – either by being qualified by it or by parts of its carrier mixing with it – it does not convey them correctly, but mixed with that taste, as with patients whose saliva has changed in one of two ways. Therefore, the one in whom yellow bile predominates finds insipid water and sweet sugar as bitter. And from there, i.e., because when a taste mixes with it, it does not convey tastes correctly but mixed with what mixed with it. And similarly for other qualities: heat is only known to exist by sensation, and what sensation gives and testifies to is its existence in the organ containing the tactile faculty when touching fire. Commentary
This passage defines the faculty of taste and explains its mechanism, then refutes a skeptical objection. The author first states that the taste faculty perceives tastes via a moisture (saliva) emitted from the salivary gland, which must be tasteless in itself. He presents two possibilities for how the saliva mediates: either particles from the tasted object mix with the saliva and then contact the tongue, or the saliva becomes qualified by the taste without mixture. If the saliva is tasteless, it conveys tastes correctly; if it has a taste (e.g., in sick people), it distorts the perception. He gives an example: someone with excess yellow bile tastes sweet things as bitter. Then he reports an objection from some thinkers who claim that tastes do not exist in objects like honey, but only in the perceiving faculty and its instrument, drawing an analogy to heat, which they argue is only known in the sense organ, not in fire. The author responds that this is a denial of the manifestly known existence of qualities in their subjects and a form of sophistry. The argument is self-refuting because it contradicts immediate sensory evidence, so no further refutation is needed.

The Definition and Distribution of the Sense of Touch
Definition and distribution of touch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the sense of touch according to Islamic philosophers, its distribution in the body, and the divine wisdom behind why certain organs lack it.

Core Idea
Touch is a protective faculty spread through nerves, but some organs wisely lack it to avoid pain.

In this passage, the author explains the fifth sense: touch. He describes it as a force diffused in nerves throughout the body, especially the skin, to perceive harmful temperatures. He then lists organs like the kidney, liver, spleen, lung, and bone that lack tactile sensitivity for wise reasons, showing a purposeful design.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the definition of touch and the list of organs that lack it.
Focus on the wording of the definition: 'قوة مبثوثة في العصب المخالط لأكثر البدن'.
2
Understand the purpose of touch
The author explains that touch is meant to perceive whether the surrounding air is burning or freezing, so the body can protect itself and preserve its life-sustaining temperament.
The phrase 'يحترز عنه كيلا يفسد المزاج الذي به الحياة' is key.
3
Examine the wisdom behind insensate organs
The author lists organs without touch and gives reasons: kidney (sharp waste), liver (acute humors), spleen (black bile), lung (constant movement). Bone is discussed separately: it is the body's foundation, so sensation would cause pain from bearing weight. A contrary opinion says bone has dull sensation, causing intense pain when felt.
Consider why this design is deemed 'divine wisdom' (الحكمة الإلهية).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشعر الخامس : اللمس : وهو قوة مبثوثة في العصب المخالط لأكثر البدن سيما الجلد ) فإن العصب يخالطه كله ليدرك به أن الهواء المجاور للبدن محرق أو مجمد فيحترز عنه كيلا يفسد المزاج الذي به الحياة . ( ومن الأعضاء ما ليس فيه قوة لامسة كالكلية فإنها ممر الفضلات الحادة فاقتضت الحكمة) الإلهية أن لا يكون لها حس لئلا تتأذى بمرورها عليها) وكالكبد إذ يتولد فيه الأخلاط الحادة وكالطحال فإنه مفرغة للسوداء، وكالرئة فإنها دائمة الحركة لترويح القلب فلا حس في شيء من هذه الأعضاء بل في أغشيتها، ليدرك بها ما يعرض لها من الآفات، ( وكذلك العظم) ليس فيه قوة لامسة ( لأنه أساس البدن ) وعموده ) وعليه أثقاله ) فلو كان له لتأذى بالحمل، وقد يقال : إن له حساً إلا أن في حسه كلالاً، ولذلك كان إحساسه بالألم إذا أحس شديداً جداً .
The fifth sense-organ: Touch: it is a faculty diffused in the nerve intermingled with most of the body, especially the skin. For the nerve intermingles with all of it to perceive through it whether the air adjacent to the body is burning or freezing, so that it guards against it so that it does not corrupt the temperament by which life exists. And among the organs there are those in which there is no tactile faculty, like the kidney, for it is a passage for sharp waste products; divine wisdom necessitated that it should not have sensation so that it would not be harmed by their passing over it. And like the liver, for acute humors are generated in it; and like the spleen, for it is the repository of black bile; and like the lung, for it is constantly moving to ventilate the heart. So there is no sensation in any of these organs, but rather in their membranes, so that they perceive what afflictions befall them. And similarly, bone has no tactile faculty because it is the foundation of the body, its pillar, and upon it are its weights; so if it had sensation, it would be harmed by bearing. And it is said that it does have sensation, but its sensation is dull; for that reason, its sensation of pain, when it senses, is very intense.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage defines the sense of touch and explains its purpose and distribution. The author states that touch is a force diffused throughout the nerve that intermingles with most of the body, especially the skin. Its function is to detect harmful temperatures in the surrounding air so the body can protect itself and preserve its life-sustaining temperament. Then he lists organs that lack the tactile faculty: the kidney (to avoid pain from sharp wastes), the liver (where sharp humors form), the spleen (repository of black bile), and the lung ( constantly moving). These organs have sensation only in their membranes to detect afflictions. Bone also lacks touch because it is the body's foundation; if it had sensation, it would suffer from bearing weight. However, a contrary opinion is mentioned: bone does have sensation, but it is dull, so when it does sense pain, it is very intense.

Key Terms
القوة اللامسة
اللمس
العصب
المشعر الخامس
الحكمة الإلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think all body parts have equal touch sensitivity.
Correction: The author explains that some internal organs lack the tactile faculty for a wise purpose, and when they do sense (like bone), it is dull but intense.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is located deep in the body.
B) Because it is a passage for sharp waste products.
C) Because it is covered by a membrane.
D) Because it is always moving.

Key Takeaway
Touch is a protective sense distributed in nerves, but some internal organs wisely lack it to avoid pain from their normal functions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel pain or discomfort in a part of your body, do you ever stop to think that this sensation itself is a mercy from Allah that alerts you to harm? How often do you thank Him for the gift of touch that protects your life?
?
The kidney, liver, spleen, and lung do not feel the normal processes that would cause them pain—this is divine wisdom. Are there trials in your life that you wish you could not feel? Can you trust that Allah's wisdom in allowing you to feel them is for a greater good, even when it hurts?
?
Bone, the foundation of your body, lacks sharp sensation except in extreme pain. This is a mercy so you can carry burdens without constant discomfort. What spiritual 'burdens' have you been carrying that you've become numb to? Will you ask Allah to renew your sensitivity to what truly matters before the pain becomes unbearable?

The Argument for Multiple Tactile Faculties
Enumeration of tactile faculties and argument for multiplicity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some philosophers argued for multiple distinct tactile faculties based on the principle 'from one only one proceeds'.

Core Idea
The principle 'from one only one proceeds' forces a multiplicity of faculties to perceive diverse contraries.

In this passage, the author introduces a debate about whether the sense of touch is a single faculty or multiple. Some philosophers, adhering to the causal axiom that a single cause produces only a single effect, argue that the perception of different contrary qualities (like hot/cold, wet/dry) requires distinct faculties. The passage presents their reasoning and illustrates how one bodily instrument can host multiple faculties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of tactile faculties
Read the first part where the four (or five) faculties are enumerated, each judging a pair of contraries.
Notice the order: hot/cold, wet/dry, hard/soft, smooth/rough, and some add heavy/light.
2
Understand the argument from the instrument
The passage argues that the same bodily part (e.g., the vitreous humor of the eye) can host multiple faculties, so tactile faculties can be multiple while using the same instrument.
This counters the objection that diffusion of touch in the body implies it is one faculty.
3
Grasp the underlying principle
The entire argument is built on the axiom 'from one only one proceeds', which demands multiple sources for multiple perceptions.
This is a key principle in Islamic philosophy and theology.

تنبيهان : الأول منهم من قال أن القوة اللامسة أربع متغايرة بالذوات ( الحاكمة بين الحار والبارد و) الحاكمة بين الرطب واليابس (و الحاكمة ( بين الصلب واللين و الحاكمة (بين الأملس والخشن. ومنهم من أثبت ) قوة خامسة تحكم بين الثقيل والخفيف ولا يبعد كون الآلة الحاملة للقوة ( واحدة ) مع تعدد القوى اللامسة الحالة فيها فلا يلزم من سريان اللامسة في البدن وانتشارها فيه كونها قوة واحدة . ( كما أن الرطوبة الجليدية فيها قوة باصرة و قوة ( لامسة ) وإذا جاز اجتماعهما في محل واحد جاز اجتماع اللامستين فيه أيضا إذ ليستا متماثلتين ) وكله بناء على أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد فلا بد من قوى متعددة إما أربع أو خمس لإدراك تلك الملموسات. ( وليت شعري لم لا يجعلون الذائقة أيضاً ) قوى (متعددة لتعدد المذوقات؟) كما يجعلون اللامسة متعددة لتعدد الملموسات. قال الإمام الرازي: لهم أن يجيبوا عن هذا بأنا إنما أوجبنا أن يكون الحاكم على نوع واحد من التضاد قوة واحدة على حده ليتم الشعور بهما والتميز بينهما ، ولا شك أن بين الحرارة والبرودة نوعاً من المضادة مغايراً للنوع الذي بين الرطوبة واليبوسة وكذا الحال في بواقي الملموسات بخلاف الطعوم. قوله : ( ولا يبعد كون الآلة إلخ ) هذا جواب سؤال مقدر يدل عليه السياق . أعني قوله : فلا يلزم من سريان اللامسة إلخ . قوله: (وكله بناء على أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد فعلى هذا يلزم أن يكون إدراك مستنداً إلى قوله : لامسة وإدراك البرودة مستندا إلى قوله : أخرى لامسة أيضاً وأن يكون الحاكم بالتضاد بينهما هو العقل دون القوتين المذكورتين فإن كل واحد منهما منفردة لا تقوى على إدراك الكيفتين المتضادتين معاً فإن كان إدراك كل واحدة منهما يتغاير بالنوع إدراك الأخرى وأما كون التضاد بينهما نوعاً واحداً فلا يقتضي أن يكون إدراكها نوعاً ولو جعل إدراكها نوعاً واحداً فليجعل سائر الإدراكات اللمسية نوعاً واحداً . قوله : ( مغايراً للنوع الذي إلخ ) هذا غير مسلم هناك هو المغايرة الصنفية دون المغايرة
Two notices: First: Among them are those who said that the tactile faculty is four, differing in essence: (the one judging between hot and cold, and) the one judging between wet and dry, and the one judging between hard and soft, and the one judging between smooth and rough. And among them are those who affirmed a fifth faculty that judges between heavy and light. And it is not improbable that the instrument bearing the faculty is one despite the multiplicity of the tactile faculties subsisting in it; so it does not follow from the diffusion of the tactile faculty in the body and its spread therein that it is a single faculty. (Just as the vitreous humor in [the eye] contains a visual faculty and a tactile faculty; and if it is permissible for them to coexist in a single locus, it is permissible for two tactile faculties to coexist therein as well, since they are not identical.) And all of this is based on [the principle] that from one only one proceeds, so there must be multiple faculties – either four or five – to perceive those tangible things. (Would that I knew: why do they not also make the tasting faculty multiple for the multiplicity of tastes, just as they make the tactile faculty multiple for the multiplicity of tangible things?) Imam al-Razi said: They can answer this by [saying] that we only necessitated that the judge over one type of contrariety is a single faculty by itself so that sensation and distinction between them are complete; and there is no doubt that between heat and cold there is a type of opposition different from the type between wetness and dryness, and similarly for the remaining tangible things – unlike tastes. His saying: (And it is not improbable that the instrument etc.) This is an answer to an implied question indicated by the context – I mean his saying: so it does not follow from the diffusion of the tactile faculty etc. His saying: (And all of this is based on that from one only one proceeds) – on this basis, it follows that the perception of heat is attributed to a faculty called tactile, and the perception of cold is attributed to another also called tactile, and that the judge of the opposition between them is the intellect, not the two mentioned faculties; for each of them is singular and cannot perceive two opposing qualities together. For if the perception of each one differs in type from the perception of the other, then the fact that the opposition between them is of one type does not require that their perception be of one type; and if their perception were made of one type, then all other tactile perceptions should be made of one type. His saying: (different from the type which etc.) This is not conceded; there it is generic difference without specific difference.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a debate (tanbīh) about whether the sense of touch is one faculty or multiple. The author reports two positions: some say there are four distinct faculties for judging pairs of contraries (hot/cold, wet/dry, hard/soft, smooth/rough); others add a fifth for heavy/light. They argue that the same bodily instrument can host multiple faculties, citing the eye's vitreous humor which contains both visual and tactile powers. The underlying principle is 'from one only one proceeds' (an axiom in causation), so to perceive multiple contraries, multiple faculties are needed. Then an objector asks: why not apply the same reasoning to taste, which also has many flavors? Imam al-Razi's answer is that the contraries involved in touch (e.g., hot-cold, wet-dry) are different in type from each other, requiring distinct judges, but tastes (e.g., sweet, bitter) are not opposed in the same way, so taste can remain a single faculty. The editorial comments further clarify the logic: if the perception of each contrary is of a different type, then a single faculty cannot handle both, and the intellect must judge the opposition. Also, the claim that the opposition types are different is not conceded—it may be only generic, not specific.

القوى اللامسة
الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد
التضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sense of touch feels unified, so it must be a single faculty.
Correction: According to the argument, the unity of sensation does not prove a single faculty; the principle 'from one only one proceeds' demands multiple faculties for multiple contrary perceptions, even if they share the same instrument.

Checkpoint
According to the argument presented, why do some philosophers posit multiple tactile faculties?
A) Because each finger has a different sensitivity.
B) Because the principle 'from one only one proceeds' requires multiple sources for multiple perceptions.
C) Because the skin is composed of layers.
D) Because the sense of touch is located in different organs.

Key Takeaway
The principle 'from one only one proceeds' is used to argue for multiple distinct tactile faculties, each responsible for perceiving a specific pair of contraries.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your sense of touch is not a single faculty but multiple—each perceiving a different pair of opposites. When you last felt the hot sun and cold water on the same hand, did you realize you were engaging multiple faculties at once? How does this complexity deepen your appreciation for the Creator's design?
?
The principle 'from one only one proceeds' forces a multiplicity of faculties to perceive diverse tangibles. Consider your own life: where have you assumed simplicity when there is actually intricate diversity? What would it mean to recognize that every phenomenon you experience is a sign of Allah's wisdom?
?
If multiple faculties can coexist in one locus without conflict—like visual and tactile in the vitreous humor—how can you apply this lesson to your inner life? Can you hold multiple intentions, emotions, or responsibilities in your heart without them contradicting? What does this teach you about the unity of your soul under the command of Allah?

The Objection from Taste and al-Razi's Response
Objection and response regarding taste and touch, with editorial glosses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that taste should also be multiple if touch is multiple, and al-Razi's response based on the nature of contrariety.

Core Idea
Al-Razi defends the multiplicity of touch by distinguishing types of contrariety, which do not apply to tastes.

After presenting the argument for multiple tactile faculties, the author raises an objection: why not apply the same reasoning to taste, which also has many flavors? Imam al-Razi provides a response: the contraries involved in touch (e.g., heat-cold, wet-dry) are of different types, each requiring a distinct faculty, whereas the differences in tastes are not genuinely contrary in the same way. The passage also includes editorial clarifications that elaborate on the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector asks why taste is not also made multiple given the multiplicity of tastes, just as touch is made multiple for tangible things.
This is a challenge to the consistency of the argument.
2
Understand al-Razi's answer
Al-Razi responds that touch involves different types of contrariety (e.g., hot-cold opposition is different from wet-dry opposition), so each type needs its own faculty. Tastes, however, are not opposed in the same way.
He does not elaborate on why tastes are not contrary.
3
Review the editorial commentary
The editor clarifies that if each touch perception is of a different type, then a single faculty cannot perceive both, and the intellect judges the opposition. Also, the claim that the types are different is contested.
The passage ends with a note that the difference may be generic, not specific.

Why Some Organs Lack Touch
Explains why certain internal organs lack the sense of touch and why their membranes have it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why certain internal organs like the kidney, liver, spleen, lung, and bone lack the sense of touch, and how their membranes provide sensation.

Core Idea
Divine wisdom places touch in membranes, not organs, to prevent constant pain.

The passage explains a beautiful design in the human body: why some vital organs do not directly feel pain. Al-Sanusi argues that if these organs had the sense of touch, they would suffer from their normal functions. Instead, the sensitive power resides in their outer membranes, allowing the body to detect harm without damaging the organs themselves.

Guided Path
1
Understand the role of touch
The passage begins by noting that the sense of touch protects the body from harmful air. Then it lists organs that lack touch.
2
Identify the organs listed
Find the kidney, liver, spleen, lung, and bone. Notice the reason given for each: kidney passes sharp wastes, liver generates sharp humors, etc.
The membranes surrounding these organs have the sense of touch instead.
3
Reflect on the wisdom
Consider why divine wisdom placed sensation in the membranes rather than the organs themselves. This prevents constant pain while still allowing detection of problems.
The passage also mentions a view that bone has dull sensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حس الهواء المجاور للبدن محرق أو مجمد فيحترز عنه كيلا يفسد المزاج الذي به الحياة . ( ومن الأعضاء ما ليس فيه قوة لامسة كالكلية فإنها ممر الفضلات الحادة فاقتضت الحكمة) الإلهية أن لا يكون لها حس لئلا تتأذى بمرورها عليها) وكالكبد إذ يتولد فيه الأخلاط الحادة وكالطحال فإنه مفرغة للسوداء، وكالرئة فإنها دائمة الحركة لترويح القلب فلا حس في شيء من هذه الأعضاء بل في أغشيتها، ليدرك بها ما يعرض لها من الآفات، ( وكذلك العظم) ليس فيه قوة لامسة ( لأنه أساس البدن ) وعموده ) وعليه أثقاله ) فلو كان له لتأذى بالحمل، وقد يقال : إن له حساً إلا أن في حسه كلالاً، ولذلك كان إحساسه بالألم إذا أحس شديداً جداً .
The air adjacent to the body is burning or freezing, so one guards against it so as not to spoil the temperament by which life exists. And among the organs there is that which has no sensitive power (quwwa lāmisa) such as the kidney, for it is a passage for sharp wastes, so divine wisdom necessitated that it should not have sensation (hiss) lest it be harmed by their passing upon it. And like the liver, for sharp humors are generated therein, and like the spleen, for it is a receptacle for black bile, and like the lung, for it is in constant motion to cool the heart; so there is no sensation in any of these organs, but rather in their membranes, in order to perceive by them what afflicts them of ailments. And likewise the bone has no sensitive power because it is the foundation of the body and its pillar and upon it are its weights; if it had sensation, it would be harmed by carrying. And it is said that it has sensation, but in its sensation there is dullness, and therefore its sensation of pain, when it feels, is very intense.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage defines the purpose of the sense of touch and explains why certain internal organs lack it. The author begins by noting that the air surrounding the body can be hot or cold, so the sense of touch alerts the organism to avoid harm to its vital temperament. He then lists several organs: the kidney, which passes sharp wastes; the liver, where sharp humors form; the spleen, which stores black bile; and the lung, which moves constantly to ventilate the heart. Each of these organs could suffer pain if they had direct touch, so divine wisdom placed the sensitive power in their outer membranes instead, allowing the organism to detect harmful conditions without direct injury to the organ itself. Similarly, the bone, as the structural foundation supporting the body, would be pained by carrying loads if it had acute touch; it either lacks touch or has a dulled sense, so that when pain does occur it is very intense. The passage thus highlights teleological design in the animal body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absence of touch in an organ means it is completely insensate.
Correction: The text explains that the membranes surrounding the organ have sensation, allowing detection of harm.

Checkpoint
Why does the bone lack a strong sense of touch according to the passage?
A) Because it is dense and cannot feel
B) Because it is the body's foundation and would be harmed by carrying loads
C) Because it is covered by flesh
D) Because it has no nerves

Key Takeaway
Divine wisdom places the sense of touch in membranes rather than the organs themselves to prevent constant pain while still enabling detection of harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah placed touch in membranes to protect organs from constant pain, where in your life might you be spared pain by His mercy? Will you pause today to thank Him for every ache you do not have?
?
The bone has dull sensation but intense pain when it does feel. Is there an area of your heart that has become dull to sin, only to be shocked when its consequences strike? Ask Allah to give you a sensitive heart without hardness.
?
The kidney passes sharp wastes without feeling them because of divine wisdom. What 'sharp wastes' in your own character—like harsh words or pride—do you pass through others without noticing the harm? How can you become more aware and grateful for Allah's protection?

The Number of Touch Powers and the Principle of the One
Sanusi presents the view that touch has four or five powers, the principle behind it, and an objection with al-Razi's answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about whether the sense of touch consists of four or five distinct powers and how the principle 'from the one only one proceeds' is used to argue for multiple powers.

Core Idea
The principle that one cause yields one effect forces multiple touch powers for distinct opposites.

This lesson examines the claim that the sense of touch involves multiple distinct powers rather than a single faculty. The argument relies on the famous kalam principle 'al-wāḥid lā yaṣduru ʿanhu illā al-wāḥid' (from the one only one proceeds). We will see how this principle is applied to sensory perception and the initial objection that taste should similarly require multiple powers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four or five powers
Read the passage and list the pairs of opposites that some scholars assign to distinct touch powers. Notice the fifth power for heavy/light.
The powers are defined by the opposites they judge between.
2
Understand the principle
The passage states that all this is built on the principle 'from the one only one proceeds'. Reflect on why this principle necessitates multiple powers for different opposites.
3
Consider the objection from taste
The author objects: why not make taste also multiple powers? Pause and think about the logic of the objection before reading al-Razi's answer.
4
Study al-Razi's answer
Al-Razi argues that touch involves distinct types of opposition for each pair, while tastes share a single type. Evaluate this reasoning.

(تنبيهان : ) الأول منهم من قال أن القوة اللامسة أربع متغايرة بالذوات ( الحاكمة بين الحار والبارد و) الحاكمة بين الرطب واليابس (و الحاكمة ( بين الصلب واللين و الحاكمة (بين الأملس والخشن. ومنهم من أثبت ) قوة خامسة تحكم بين الثقيل والخفيف ولا يبعد كون الآلة الحاملة للقوة ( واحدة ) مع تعدد القوى اللامسة الحالة فيها فلا يلزم من سرية اللامسة في البدن وانتشارها فيه كونها قوة واحدة . ( كما أن الرطوبة الجليدية فيها قوة باصرة و قوة ( لامسة ) وإذا جاز اجتماعهما في محل واحد جاز اجتماع اللامستين فيه أيضا إذ ليستا متماثلتين ) وكله بناء على أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد فلا بد من قوى متعددة إما أربع أو خمس لإدراك تلك الملموسات. ( وليت شعري لم لا يجعلون الذائقة أيضاً ) قوى (متعددة لتعدد المذوقات؟) كما يجعلون اللامسة متعددة لتعدد الملموسات. قال الإمام الرازي: لهم أن يجيبوا عن هذا بأنا إنما أوجبنا أن يكون الحاكم على نوع واحد من التضاد قوة واحدة على حده ليتم الشعور بهما والتميز بينهما ، ولا شك أن بين الحرارة والبرودة نوعاً من المضادة مغايراً للنوع الذي بين الرطوبة واليبوسة وكذا الحال في بواقي الملموسات بخلاف الطعوم
قوله : ( ولا يبعد كون الآلة إلخ ) هذا جواب سؤال مقدر يدل عليه السياق . أعني قوله : فلا يلزم من سريان اللامسة إلخ . قوله: (وكله بناء على أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد فعلى هذا يلزم أن يكون إدراك مستنداً إلى قوله : لامسة وإدراك البرودة مستندا إلى قوله : أخرى لامسة أيضاً وأن يكون الحاكم بالتضاد بينهما هو العقل دون القوتين المذكورتين فإن كل واحد منهما منفردة لا تقوى على إدراك الكيفتين المتضادتين معاً فإن كان إدراك كل واحدة منهما يتغاير بالنوع إدراك الأخرى وأما كون التضاد بينهما نوعاً واحداً فلا يقتضي أن يكون إدراكها نوعاً ولو جعل إدراكها نوعاً واحداً فليجعل سائر الإدراكات اللمسية نوعاً واحداً . قوله : ( مغايراً للنوع الذي إلخ هذا غير مسلم هناك هو المغايرة الصنفية دون المغايرة النوعية. وقوله : وكذا الحال في البواقي إلخ، هناك أيضاً ممنوع، وكذا قوله: ليس بينهما إلا نوع التضاد واحد من الحلاوة والمرارة مثلا يغاير بالنوع التضاد بين الحموضة والقبض، ولو سلم ذلك ولا نسلم أنه يكفي في إدراك المضادين قوة واحدة حتى يكفي في إدراك التضاد بينهما قوة واحدة وقد أشار إلى هذا المنوع بقوله : لظهور ضعف . فإنها مع كثرتها ليس بينها إلا نوع واحد من التضاد فيكفيها قوة واحدة . ولم يلتفت إليه المصنف لظهور ضعفه.
(Two warnings: ) The first of them: Some say that the touching power (al-quwwa al-lāmisa) is four, different in essences: the ruling between hot and cold, and the ruling between wet and dry, and the ruling between hard and soft, and the ruling between smooth and rough. And some affirm a fifth power ruling between heavy and light. And it is not unlikely that the instrument bearing the power is one while the touching powers subsisting in it are multiple; so the pervasion of the touching sense in the body and its spread does not necessitate that it be one power. (Just as the aqueous humor contains a seeing power and a touching power, and if their coexistence in one locus is permissible, then the coexistence of two touching powers in it is also permissible since they are not similar.) And all of this is built upon the principle that from the one only one proceeds; so multiple powers, either four or five, are necessary for the perception of those tangible objects. (Would that I knew why they do not make the taste also multiple powers according to the multiplicity of tastes?) as they make the touching multiple according to the multiplicity of tangibles. Imam al-Razi said: They may answer this by saying: We only require that the judge over one type of opposition be a single power, so that sensation of both and discrimination between them be complete. And there is no doubt that between heat and cold there is a type of opposition different from the type between wet and dry, and similarly for the remaining tangibles, unlike tastes. Then the commentary: His statement: 'And it is not unlikely that the instrument etc.' This is the answer to an implicit question indicated by the context, namely his statement: 'So the pervasion of the touching sense etc.' His statement: 'And all of this is built upon the principle that from the one only one proceeds' – accordingly, it follows that the perception of heat is attributed to one touching power and the perception of cold to another touching power, and that the judge of the opposition between them is the intellect, not the two mentioned powers, for each of them alone is not strong enough to perceive both opposite qualities simultaneously. For if the perception of each one differs in type from the perception of the other, then the fact that the opposition between them is one type does not necessitate that their perception be one type. And if their perception were made one type, then let all other tactile perceptions be made one type. His statement: 'different from the type which etc.' – this is not conceded; there it is specific difference (al-mughāyara al-ṣinfiyya), not generic difference (al-mughāyara al-nawʿiyya). And his statement: 'and similarly for the remaining etc.' – there also it is denied. And likewise his statement: 'there is between them only one type of opposition' – for example, the opposition between sweetness and bitterness differs in type from the opposition between sourness and astringency. And even if that is granted, we do not concede that one power suffices for perceiving two opposites, such that one power would be sufficient for perceiving the opposition between them. And he indicated this denial by his statement: 'due to the appearance of its weakness.' For they, despite their multiplicity, have between them only one type of opposition, so one power suffices for them. And the author (al-muṣannif) did not pay attention to it due to the appearance of its weakness.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the question: how many distinct sensitive powers (quwwa lamisa) constitute the sense of touch? The author reports that some scholars hold there are four distinct powers governing the discrimination of hot/cold, wet/dry, hard/soft, smooth/rough; others add a fifth for heavy/light. The argument is that a single power cannot simultaneously perceive two opposite qualities because one simple thing cannot produce two effects (the principle 'al-wāḥid lā yaṣduru ʿanhu illā al-wāḥid'). Therefore, distinct powers are needed for each opposite pair. An objector raises the case of taste: if touch requires multiple powers for each type of opposite, why does taste not also require multiple powers for the many tastes? Imam al-Razi answers that in touch each pair of opposites involves a distinct type of opposition (e.g., hot-cold is a different kind of opposition than wet-dry), whereas tastes like sweetness and sourness fall under a single type of opposition (flavor), so one power suffices. The commentator then dissects this response, arguing that the distinction between generic (nawʿiyya) and specific (ṣinfiyya) opposition is not granted, and that even if accepted, it is not conceded that one power can perceive two opposites. The commentator concludes that the author (al-Iji) did not adopt this argument because of its weakness. The passage thus illustrates the application of causal principles to sensory psychology.

Key Terms
قوة لامسة
الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد
التضاد
الحاكم بين الحار والبارد
الإمام الرازي
المضادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the principle 'al-wahid la yasduru' is being applied directly to the soul; it is used here to argue for distinct faculties, not for the oneness of the soul.
Correction: The principle is applied to the sense of touch to argue for multiple powers, not to the soul's unity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for arguing that the sense of touch consists of multiple distinct powers?
A) The diversity of tangible qualities
B) The principle that from the one only one proceeds
C) The structure of the nervous system
D) The existence of five fingers

Key Takeaway
The principle 'al-wahid la yasduru' is used to argue for multiple touch powers, but the objection from taste challenges its consistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the principle 'one cause yields one effect' applies to sensory powers, what does it imply about your heart? Can your heart truly serve both Allah and your desires without being divided? How do you reconcile multiple attachments with the oneness of devotion required by tawḥīd?
?
The text describes distinct discriminative powers for different opposites. Think of the opposites in your own life – truth and falsehood, good and evil. Do you have a clear, active faculty that distinguishes between them, or do you allow mixed feelings to blur moral clarity?
?
Al-Razi answers that taste needs only one power because all flavors share a single type of opposition. Are your trials and temptations all of one kind, or do they demand multiple kinds of discernment? How can you cultivate the ‘touch powers’ of your soul to judge each situation correctly?

5 min
Critique of al-Razi's Defense of Touch Powers
Commentary critiquing al-Razi's answer, denying generic difference, and arguing against one power perceiving two opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed critique of al-Razi's argument that touch requires distinct powers while taste does not, including the denial of generic difference and the claim that one power cannot perceive two opposites.

Core Idea
The critique denies that different types of opposition exist in touch and argues that one power cannot perceive two opposites.

This lesson delves into the detailed commentary on al-Razi's response. The commentator challenges the claim that touch involves distinct types of opposition, arguing that the differences are merely specific, not generic. Moreover, even if they were distinct, one power still cannot perceive two opposites. The lesson concludes with the author's dismissal of the argument as weak.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's critique
The commentary dissects al-Razi's answer. First, it explains the implicit question and then challenges the distinction between generic and specific difference.
Pay attention to the terms 'المغايرة الصنفية' and 'المغايرة النوعية'.
2
Understand the denial of generic difference
The commentator denies that the opposition between hot-cold is generically different from wet-dry. He says it is only specifically different.
3
Grasp the argument about one power perceiving opposites
Even if the types of opposition were distinct, the commentator argues that one power cannot perceive two opposites, so the whole approach is flawed.
4
Note the author's dismissal
The author (al-Iji) ignores this weak argument. Reflect on why it is considered weak.

?

?

intermediate
