The Sense of Taste and Its Distinction from Touch
Distinguishes taste from touch and argues against a sixth sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the sense of taste is distinct from touch despite its reliance on contact, and why a sixth sense is impossible according to the philosophers.

Core Idea
Taste and touch are opposites in purpose: one attracts, the other repels.

The passage addresses a potential confusion: since taste requires physical contact, one might think it's a type of touch. However, it argues that taste is fundamentally different, both in its mechanism (requiring saliva) and in its purpose (attracting what sustains life versus repelling what harms). The philosophers then argue that no additional sense beyond the five is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the structure: first the dependence on touch, then the refutation of identity, then the philosophers' claim.
2
Identify the two distinctions
Find the two reasons why taste is not touch: (1) need for saliva, (2) opposite purposes.
The text says 'it needs the mediation of salivary moisture' and 'taste was created for sensation of what is suitable... touch for what is not suitable'.
3
Understand the argument against a sixth sense
Consider why a sixth sense is impossible: nature does not skip degrees, so all possible senses at that level are already present.
The philosophers use this to show that the five senses are complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثاني) من التنبيهين (قوة الذوق ) في إدراكها مشروطة باللمس ) إذ لا يتصور إدراك ذوقي بلا ملامسة بين اللسان والمذوق فربما يتوهم من ذلك اتحاد الذائقة ،باللامسة، فدفعه بقوله : ( ولا شك أنها غيرها إذ لا يكفي فيها أي في إدراك الذائقة (اللمس) وحده (بل يحتاج) إلى توسط الرطوبة اللعابية واختلاطها على ما مر فلا بد من التغاير وكيف لا ؟ والذوق ( يضاده ) أي اللمس باعتبار الغاية ( لأن الذوق) إنما (خلق للشعور بما يلائم) من المطعومات التي تستبقى بها الحياة ( ليجتلب واللمس خلق للشعور بما لا يلائم ليجتنب). وتلخيصه أن الحيوان مركب من العناصر الأربعة فصلاحه باعتدالها وفساده بغلبة بعضها على بعض فلا بد له من قوة يدرك بها ما ينافي مزاجه ويخرجه عن اعتداله،، وهي اللامسة الدافعة للمضرة كما لا بد له من قوة جاذبة للمنفعة . فبهذا الاعتبار كان بينهما تضاد وتخالف، ولما كان الاجتناب عن جميع المنافيات واجباً الملائمات عمت اللامسة البدن . قال الحكماء : لا يمكن وجود حاسة سادسة لأن الطبيعة لا تنتقل من درجة الحيوانية إلى درجة فوقها إلا وقد استكملت ما في الدرجة الأولى فلو كان في الإمكان حس آخر لكان حاصلاً للإنسان . ( وهاهنا أبحاث ( أي بحثان نختم بها هذا النوع أي الأول من الأنواع الثلاثة. ( أحدها أن الحواس الظاهرة مختلفة بالقوة والضعف ( في إدراكاتها، ( وتفاوتها ) في ذلك إنما هو ( بحسب القوة الممانعة وضعفها ) فكل ما كان أقوى ممانعة لمدركه كان أقوى إحساساً به ( وذلك ) أي التفاوت في الممانعة قوة وضعفاً إنما هو (لغلظ الآلة ورقتها ) فما هو أغلظ آلة كان أشد ممانعة ( و ) على هذا ( أضعفها ) في الإحساس ( البصر إذ التها النور وهو ألطف ) من آلات سائر الحواس ) ثم السمع وآلتها الهواء ثم قوله : ( وكل ما كان أقوى ممانعة لمدركه كان أقوى إحساساً (به) ويعلم هذا باعتبار زيادة اللذة والألم أو نقصانهما بسبب تعلق تلك الحواس بمتعلقاتها كما أشار إليه الشارح بقوله : فلذا كانت ملائماته الذ ومنافراته أشد إيلاماً . وقوله : فلو وجب إلخ إشارة إلى المعارضة وقوله : وقد يجاب إلخ. إشارة إلى منع الملازمة المذكورة وقوله : ولذلك أي ولأجل كونها محسوسة بالعرض أو وللاستعانة المذكورة.
(The second) of the two warnings: The power of taste (quwwat al-dhawq) is conditioned in its perception by touch, for taste perception cannot be conceived without contact between the tongue and the tasted object. So perhaps one might imagine from that that the taste is identical with touch. So he repelled this by his saying: (And there is no doubt that it is other than it, for touch alone is not sufficient for it, i.e., for the perception of taste, but it needs the mediation of salivary moisture and its mixture, as previously, so there must be difference. And how not? And taste opposes it, i.e., touch, in terms of purpose, because taste was created only for sensation of what is suitable (mulā'im) from among edibles by which life is preserved, so that it may attract it, and touch was created for sensation of what is not suitable, so that it may repel it.) And its summary: The animal is composed of the four elements; its well-being lies in their balance and its corruption in the predominance of some over others. So it must have a power by which it perceives what is contrary to its temperament and removes it from its balance, and that is the touching power that repels harm, just as it must have a power that attracts benefit. In this respect there is opposition and contrariety between them. And since avoidance of all harmful things is obligatory, and attraction of all suitable things, the sense of touch pervades the body. The philosophers said: A sixth sense cannot exist, because nature does not move from the animal degree to a degree above it unless it has completed what is in the first degree; so if another sense were possible, it would be present in man. (And here there are investigations, i.e., two investigations, by which we conclude this type, i.e., the first of the three types. (One of them is that the external senses differ in strength and weakness in their perceptions, and their disparity in that is according to the resisting power (al-quwwa al-mumāni'a) and its weakness. For whatever is stronger in resistance to its perceived object is stronger in sensation of it. And that, i.e., the disparity in resistance, strength and weakness, is due to the thickness and fineness of the instrument. So whatever has a thicker instrument is stronger in resistance, and on this basis the weakest of them in sensation is sight, because its instrument is light, which is the finest of the instruments of the other senses, then hearing, whose instrument is air, then ...) His statement: (And whatever is stronger in resistance to its perceived object is stronger in sensation of it) – and this is known by considering the increase of pleasure and pain or their decrease due to the attachment of those senses to their objects, as the commentator indicated by his statement: 'Therefore its pleasant things and its unpleasant things were more painful.' And his statement: 'For if it were necessary etc.' indicates the objection. And his statement: 'And it may be answered etc.' indicates the denial of the aforementioned entailment. And his statement: 'And therefore' means either because they are perceived accidentally or due to the aforementioned assistance.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage covers two clarifications. First, it distinguishes the sense of taste from touch. Although taste relies on contact (lamas), it is different because it requires saliva (rutūba luʿābiyya) to mediate; also, their purposes differ: taste attracts what is suitable for life, while touch repels what is harmful. The animal's constitution from four elements necessitates both a repelling power (touch) and an attractive power (taste). Then the philosophers' statement that a sixth sense is impossible is cited, because nature does not skip degrees. Second, the passage introduces two investigations to conclude this section. The first investigation explains that the external senses vary in strength depending on the resistance (qūwa mumāniʿa) of their instruments. The denser the organ, the stronger the resistance and thus the stronger the sensation; the finer the organ, the weaker. For example, sight has the finest instrument (light) and is weakest in sensation of contact. The commentary elaborates that this is known from the intensity of pleasure and pain when these senses are engaged.

Key Terms
قوة الذوق
اللمس
الرطوبة اللعابية
الملائم
المنافي
الحاسة السادسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think taste and touch are the same sense because both involve contact.
Correction: Taste requires saliva and has the opposite purpose (attraction vs. repulsion), making it distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers quoted, why is a sixth sense impossible in animals?
A) Because nature does not skip degrees
B) Because animals have all five senses
C) Because a sixth sense would conflict with the others
D) Because the animal soul is too simple

Key Takeaway
Taste and touch are distinct in means and purpose, and no additional sense beyond the five is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you eat your next meal, pause and reflect: your sense of taste was created to attract you to what sustains your life. How often do you eat without gratitude or awareness of this purpose? What would it look like to turn each bite into an act of thanking your Creator?
?
The passage says touch repels what is harmful, while taste attracts what is beneficial. Think of something you strongly desire (like a habit or relationship). Is it truly beneficial for your soul, or is it harmful but disguised as pleasing? Which sense—taste (attraction) or touch (repulsion)—should you listen to in this situation?
?
The philosophers argue that no sixth sense is possible because Allah has perfected creation at each level. You have exactly the senses you need for this life. Are you content with what you have been given, or do you wish for extra abilities (like seeing the future or knowing others' thoughts)? How can you use your five senses today to draw closer to your Lord?

Variance in Sensory Strength
Explains variance in sensory strength based on resistance and instrument thickness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the external senses differ in strength of perception, and the role of the resisting power and thickness of the sense organ.

Core Idea
The strength of a sense depends on its resistance, determined by the thickness of its instrument.

This passage introduces the first investigation in this section: the disparity among external senses in strength of perception. It explains that the disparity arises from the resisting power (al-quwwa al-mumāniʿa), which itself depends on the thickness or fineness of the sense organ. For example, sight is the weakest because its instrument (light) is the finest, while senses with thicker instruments have stronger sensation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Get an overview of the argument about sensory variance.
The passage states that senses differ in strength according to resisting power.
2
Understand the principle
The stronger the resistance to a perceived object, the stronger the sensation. This is key.
Resistance here means the sense organ's ability to counteract or engage with the stimulus.
3
Identify the cause of resistance
Resistance depends on the thickness or fineness of the instrument. Thicker instruments resist more.
Example: sight has a very fine instrument (light), so it resists least and is weakest.
4
Apply to the senses
See the order: sight weakest, then hearing. The commentator mentions pleasure/pain as evidence.

Taste and Touch Are Distinct Senses
Author distinguishes taste from touch with two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational distinction between taste and touch, including their differing conditions and purposes.

Core Idea
Taste and touch are distinct: one attracts benefit, the other repels harm.

This lesson unpacks the nuanced difference between taste and touch. While both senses require physical contact, the author shows they differ in necessary conditions (moisture for taste) and in their ultimate purposes. Understanding this distinction sharpens your grasp of how the senses serve the animal's preservation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on taste vs. touch
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author anticipates a potential confusion: because taste requires contact, one might think taste and touch are the same.
The Arabic uses 'لمس' for touch and 'ذوق' for taste.
2
Identify the two key arguments for distinction
First, taste requires salivary moisture whereas touch does not. Second, their purposes are opposite: taste seeks agreeable things, touch avoids disagreeable ones.
These are the two main points: condition (moisture) and purpose (attraction vs. repulsion).
3
Understand the teleological explanation
The author explains the animal's composition from four elements and how balance is key. The two senses serve complementary roles: touch repels harm, taste attracts benefit.
This teleological argument reinforces why they must be different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنها مع كثرتها ليس بينها إلا نواع واحد من التضاد فيكفيها قوة واحدة . ولم يلتفت إليه المصنف لظهور ضعفه. (الثاني) من التنبيهين (قوة الذوق ) في إدراكها مشروطة باللمس ) إذ لا يتصور إدراك ذوقي بلا ملامسة بين اللسان والمذوق فربما يتوهم من ذلك اتحاد الذائقة ،باللامسة، فدفعه بقوله : ( ولا شك أنها غيرها إذ لا يكفي فيها أي في إدراك الذائقة (اللمس) وحده (بل يحتاج) إلى توسط الرطوبة اللعابية واختلاطها على ما مر فلا بد من التغاير وكيف لا ؟ والذوق ( يضاده ) أي اللمس باعتبار الغاية ( لأن الذوق) إنما (خلق للشعور بما يلائم) من المطعومات التي تستبقى بها الحياة ( ليجتلب واللمس خلق للشعور بما لا يلائم ليجتنب). وتلخيصه أن الحيوان مركب من العناصر الأربعة فصلاحه باعتدالها وفساده بغلبة بعضها على بعض فلا بد له من قوة يدرك بها ما ينافي مزاجه ويخرجه عن اعتداله، وهي اللامسة الدافعة للمضرة كما لا بد له من قوة جاذبة للمنفعة . فبهذا الاعتبار كان بينهما تضاد وتخالف، ولما كان الاجتناب عن جميع المنافيات واجباً دون اجتلاب جميع الملائمات عمت اللامسة البدن . قال الحكماء : لا يمكن وجود حاسة سادسة لأن الطبيعة لا تنتقل من درجة الحيوانية إلى درجة فوقها إلا وقد استكملت ما في الدرجة الأولى فلو كان في الإمكان حس آخر لكان حاصلاً للإنسان .
For although they are many, among them there is only one type of opposition, so one power suffices for them. The author did not pay attention to it because its weakness is apparent. The second of the two warnings: the power of taste in its perception is conditioned by touch, for a taste perception is inconceivable without contact between the tongue and the tasted object. So perhaps one might suppose from that the unity of the taste faculty with the touch faculty. So he refuted it by his saying: 'And there is no doubt that it is other than it, for in it, i.e., in the perception of the taste faculty, touch alone does not suffice. Rather, it needs the mediation of salivary moisture and its mixing, as has passed, so there must be difference. And how not? Taste opposes it, i.e., touch, in terms of purpose, because taste was created only for feeling what is agreeable among edibles that preserve life, to seek it, while touch was created for feeling what is disagreeable to avoid it.' Its summary is that the animal is composed of the four elements; its well-being is through their balance and its corruption through the predominance of some over others. So it inevitably needs a power by which it perceives what opposes its temperament and removes it from its balance, which is the touch faculty repelling harm, just as it inevitably needs an attractive power for benefit. In this regard, there is opposition and difference between them. And since avoidance of all harmful things is obligatory while seeking all agreeable things is not, the touch faculty covers the whole body. The philosophers said: 'It is impossible for a sixth sense to exist, because nature does not move from the animal level to a level above it except after it has completed what is in the first level; so if another sense were possible, it would exist in man.'
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It begins by noting that some might think taste and touch are the same because both require contact. The author refutes this by showing that taste requires moisture (saliva) which touch does not. Then he explains their different purposes: taste perceives pleasant things to attract them, touch perceives harmful things to avoid them. Then a teleological summary: the animal is composed of four elements; it needs a sense to repel harm (touch) and a sense to attract benefit (taste). They are opposites. Because avoiding harm is obligatory while seeking benefit is not, touch is spread over the whole body. Then the philosophers state that a sixth sense is impossible because nature does not skip stages.

Key Terms
ذوق
لمس
ذائقة
لامسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might believe taste and touch are the same sense because both require physical contact.
Correction: Taste requires moisture (saliva) and serves to attract beneficial things, whereas touch does not require moisture and serves to repel harmful things. Their conditions and purposes are fundamentally different.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives to distinguish taste from touch?
A) Taste uses the tongue, touch uses the skin.
B) Taste requires salivary moisture, touch does not.
C) Touch can feel pain, taste cannot.
D) Both A and B.

Key Takeaway
Taste and touch are distinct senses because taste requires moisture and aims to attract benefit, while touch aims to repel harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you ignore the warnings your body sends through the sense of touch—like pain or discomfort—because you are chasing after something you think is beneficial? What does this teach you about your priorities?
?
The passage says taste was created to attract what preserves life. Are you content with what is truly beneficial for your spiritual life, or do you crave things that harm your soul? This lesson forces you to examine your desires.
?
If touch covers your entire body, then you have no excuse to claim ignorance when a harmful situation arises. What harmful environment have you been tolerating? How will you act on the warning your senses give you?

The Impossibility of a Sixth Sense
Philosophers argue impossibility of sixth sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument for why a sixth sense cannot exist, based on the principle of completeness of stages.

Core Idea
Nature completes each stage before moving to the next, so a sixth sense is impossible.

This lesson presents the philosophers' argument against the possibility of a sixth sense. The argument relies on a teleological principle: nature does not skip levels. Since humans already have the five senses appropriate to the animal level, no additional sense can exist. This reasoning ties into a broader view of ontological hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' statement
Read the final sentence of the source passage. It asserts that a sixth sense is impossible and provides the reason.
The phrase 'لا يمكن وجود حاسة سادسة' is the key claim.
2
Understand the principle of stage completion
The argument says nature does not move from the animal level to a higher level without first completing all that belongs to the first level. Since a sixth sense is not part of the animal level, it cannot exist in humans.
This relies on a teleological view of nature as orderly and complete.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument would be convincing to the philosophers. It assumes a fixed hierarchy of beings (animal, human, etc.) each with its defining characteristics.
Think about how this contrasts with modern evolutionary or scientific views.

For although they are many, among them there is only one type of opposition, so one power suffices for them. The author did not pay attention to it because its weakness is apparent. The second of the two warnings: the power of taste in its perception is conditioned by touch, for a taste perception is inconceivable without contact between the tongue and the tasted object. So perhaps one might suppose from that the unity of the taste faculty with the touch faculty. So he refuted it by his saying: 'And there is no doubt that it is other than it, for in it, i.e., in the perception of the taste faculty, touch alone does not suffice. Rather, it needs the mediation of salivary moisture and its mixing, as has passed, so there must be difference. And how not? Taste opposes it, i.e., touch, in terms of purpose, because taste was created only for feeling what is agreeable among edibles that preserve life, to seek it, while touch was created for feeling what is disagreeable to avoid it.' Its summary is that the animal is composed of the four elements; its well-being is through their balance and its corruption through the predominance of some over others. So it inevitably needs a power by which it perceives what opposes its temperament and removes it from its balance, which is the touch faculty repelling harm, just as it inevitably needs an attractive power for benefit. In this regard, there is opposition and difference between them. And since avoidance of all harmful things is obligatory while seeking all agreeable things is not, the touch faculty covers the whole body. The philosophers said: 'It is impossible for a sixth sense to exist, because nature does not move from the animal level to a level above it except after it has completed what is in the first level; so if another sense were possible, it would exist in man.'
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It begins by noting that some might think taste and touch are the same because both require contact. The author refutes this by showing that taste requires moisture (saliva) which touch does not. Then he explains their different purposes: taste perceives pleasant things to attract them, touch perceives harmful things to avoid them. Then a teleological summary: the animal is composed of four elements; it needs a sense to repel harm (touch) and a sense to attract benefit (taste). They are opposites. Because avoiding harm is obligatory while seeking benefit is not, touch is spread over the whole body. Then the philosophers state that a sixth sense is impossible because nature does not skip stages.

Checkpoint

A) Nature does not waste potential.
B) Nature completes each level before moving to the next.
C) Humans cannot have more than five senses.
D) Animals have simpler senses than humans.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of a sixth sense follows from the principle that nature completes all possibilities of a level before ascending to a higher level.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your five senses every moment. If a sixth sense were possible, would you be willing to accept knowledge from it, or would you reject it as unreliable? What does your answer reveal about your trust in Allah's design of human perception?
?
The argument says that nature leaves no potential unrealized. What hidden potentials or abilities do you still keep dormant in your life – not because they are impossible, but because you fear to develop them? How can you take a concrete step this week to actualize a good potential that Allah has placed in you?
?
If you believe that Allah could have given humans a sixth sense but chose not to, what does that tell you about His wisdom in limiting our sensory knowledge? How does this perspective change the way you seek truth beyond your senses – through revelation, reflection, and sincere du'a?

The Hierarchy of the Senses: Strength and Cause
Classification of senses by strength with causal explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical theologians ranked the external senses from weakest to strongest based on the thickness of their instruments, and why touch is considered the most intense.

Core Idea
Sense strength depends on instrument thickness — touch wins, sight loses.

In this research, the author presents a striking classification of the five external senses. He argues that their perceptual strength is not arbitrary but follows a clear physical principle: the thicker the sense organ, the stronger the resistance to its object, and thus the more intense the sensation. This passage lays out the hierarchy from sight to touch and explains the underlying cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall structure. Then read the literal translation. Notice the logical flow: statement of variation, cause, ranking, conclusion.
Pay attention to the term 'آلة' (instrument) — it refers to the physical medium or organ.
2
Identify the five senses in order
The passage lists the senses from weakest to strongest. Find each one and note its instrument: sight (light), hearing (air), smell (vapor), taste (water), touch (solid earthly organs).
Sight is said to be the weakest because its instrument (light) is the finest.
3
Understand the causal mechanism
The author explains that variation in strength is due to 'قوة الممانعة' (strength of resistance), which depends on 'غلظ الآلة' (thickness of the instrument). A thicker instrument means stronger resistance, hence stronger perception.
4
Reflect on the conclusion about touch
The passage ends by noting that touch has the most intense pleasure and pain because its instrument is the thickest (solid earthly organs). Consider how this fits with everyday experience.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the main point. Then consider the common misconception: strength depends on instrument, not nobility of object.

And here are investigations, i.e., two investigations with which we conclude this species, i.e., the first of the three species. One of them is that the external senses differ in strength and weakness in their perceptions, and their variation in that is only according to the strength of resistance and its weakness. So every sense that is more resistant to its object is stronger in sensing it. And that, i.e., the variation in resistance, strength and weakness, is only due to the thickness and thinness of the instrument. So what has a thicker instrument is stronger in resistance. And on this basis, the weakest in sensation is sight, because its instrument (light) is the finest of the instruments of the other senses. Then hearing, its instrument is air; then smell, its instrument is vapor; then taste, its instrument is water; then touch, its instrument is the solid earthly organs. Thus its agreeable things are most pleasurable and its disagreeable things are most painful. [Commentary follows.] The first observation: ... [the text continues with commentary on the matn, and then the matn of the first research continues:] Smell, its instrument is vapor; then taste, its instrument is water; then touch, its instrument is the solid earthly organs. Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof. It states that the external senses differ in strength of perception due to their resistance power, which depends on the thickness or thinness of their instrument. The thicker the instrument, the stronger the resistance and thus the stronger the perception. Based on this, the weakest sense is sight because its instrument (light) is the finest. Then hearing (air), then smell (vapor), then taste (water), and the strongest is touch (earthly solid organs). Hence touch produces the most intense pleasure and pain. The intervening commentary discusses objections and clarifications related to the matn.

Correction: The passage clearly states that strength depends on the thickness of the instrument, not the object. For example, sight's instrument (light) is fine, making it weak, even though its objects may be noble.

Common Sensibles and the Answer of Incidental Perception
The author enumerates common sensibles and gives the initial answer that they are perceived incidentally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what common sensibles are and why they do not require separate faculties because they are perceived incidentally.

Core Idea
Common sensibles are perceived incidentally through other sensibles, so no extra faculties needed.

In this passage, the author presents a potential problem: common sensibles like magnitude and number are diverse, yet they are not perceived by a single sense. Some argued that each requires a separate faculty. The author answers that these are perceived indirectly, through the medium of other sensibles, as indicated in the matn.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify common sensibles
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the list of common sensibles: magnitudes, numbers, positions, shapes, motion, rest, proximity, distance, contact.
These are examples; the list is not exhaustive.
2
Understand the objection
The objection states that if each type of sensible required its own faculty, then common sensibles would need many new faculties. Notice that this is presented as a potential problem.
3
Grasp the answer
The answer is that common sensibles are perceived incidentally (بالعرض), not essentially. They are sensed through other sensibles (e.g., color, light). There is no need for separate faculties.
The author quotes the matn to support this: 'they are only sensed through the medium of color, light, heat, cold, and the like.'
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why is it important to distinguish essential and incidental perception? This distinction solves the problem of diverse sensibles without multiplying faculties unnecessarily.
This connects to the broader kalam principle of parsimony.

بقوله : ( وقد يقال : المحسوس بالعرض ما لا يحس به أصلا لكن يقارن المحسوس بالحقيقة كإبصارنا أبا عمرو، فإن المحسوس ذلك الشخص وليس كونه أبا عمرو محسوساً أصلا لا أصالة ولا تبعاً بخلاف الأمور السابقة فإنها And the second of them: here are common sensibles, i.e., the external senses share in their perception, so there is no need in sensing them for other faculties, such as magnitudes, numbers, positions, shapes, motion, rest, proximity, distance, and contact. If it were necessary for each type of sensible a separate faculty as a group held, it would be necessary to establish other faculties for perceiving these things because they are diverse types. And it may be answered about them that they are perceived incidentally, not essentially, i.e., by way of dependency, not originally. So there is no need for another faculty, as we indicated; that is only for what is essentially perceived. And he explained that they are perceived incidentally by his saying: that they are only sensed through the medium of color, light, heat, cold, and the like. Its detail is to say: sight perceives magnitude, number, position, shape, motion, rest, and contact through the medium of light and color; touch perceives all of them through the medium of heat, cold, hardness, or softness; taste perceives magnitude by tasting many flavors, and number by finding different flavors; smell perceives number by a kind of measurement, which is to know that whose scent ceased is other than that whose scent occurred secondly, and it perceives motion and rest through touch weakly. As for hearing, it does not perceive magnitude, but it may sometimes indicate it from the fact that great sounds mostly arise from great bodies. And reason may be used for it, i.e., in perceiving some of them, as in perceiving motion and rest, because the moving body must have its relation to other bodies differ; it may become near to a body that was far from it, and vice versa. So when sensation of that difference occurs from its side, consciousness of its being moving occurs. Therefore, it may not be perceived sometimes, like the rider of a ship seeing it stationary while it is moving quickly, and sees the shore moving while it is stationary. Because he did not feel that the difference of its relation to the shore is from its side, he did not feel its motion but attributed it to the shore and imagined it moving. And the use of reason by smell and hearing in number and magnitude has already been mentioned. Then he indicated another meaning for 'perceived incidentally' by his saying: 'And it may be said: the incidentally perceived is what is not perceived at all, but accompanies the truly perceived, like our seeing 'Amr: for the perceived is that person, and his being 'Amr is not perceived at all, neither originally nor dependently, unlike the previous matters, for they are perceived dependently. So the application of 'incidentally perceived' to these two meanings is by homonymy. And with this the answer emerges to what was mentioned in the Eastern investigations that these matters are not perceived incidentally, because the incidentally perceived is what is not perceived in reality but is concomitant with the real perceived. And if you wish the truth of the matter, listen to this discourse; have you not heard?'
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation. It presents the objection that common sensibles (like magnitude, number, position, shape, motion, etc.) would require additional faculties if each type required its own power. The answer is that these are perceived incidentally (بالعرض) through the other sensibles (like color, light, heat, etc.), not essentially. Then it explains how each sense perceives them incidentally: sight via light and color; touch via heat/cold, hardness/softness; taste via number of flavors; smell via inference; hearing via indication. Reason aids in some perceptions, like motion. Finally, it distinguishes two meanings of 'incidental': one where something is perceived by dependency (the common sensibles), and another where something is not perceived at all but is accompanied (like recognizing a person as 'Amr). The objection from the Mashriqi discussions is answered by this homonymy.

Detailed Incidental Perception and Two Meanings
The author details incidental perception per sense and distinguishes two meanings of 'incidental'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how each sense perceives common sensibles incidentally and the distinction between two types of incidental perception.

Core Idea
Each sense perceives common sensibles through its proper objects, and 'incidental' has two distinct meanings.

The author now provides a detailed explanation of how each sense perceives common sensibles incidentally, through the mediation of their proper objects. Then he introduces a second meaning of 'incidental perception'—something that is not perceived at all but accompanies a perception—and uses this to answer an objection from the Eastern investigations.

Guided Path
1
Follow the per-sense explanations
Read how sight, touch, taste, smell, and hearing each perceive common sensibles incidentally. Note the different media for each sense.
Focus on the mechanism: each sense uses its proper object (e.g., light, heat) to perceive the common sensible.
2
Understand the role of reason
Reason aids in perceiving some common sensibles like motion. The example of the ship and shore illustrates how reason corrects perception.
3
Grasp the second meaning of 'incidental'
The second meaning is something not perceived at all but conjoined with perception, like seeing a person and recognizing him as 'Amr. This is different from the first meaning (dependency).
The author calls this homonymy (اشتراك لفظي).
4
See how this answers the objection
The objection from the Mashriqi discussions claimed common sensibles are not incidental because incidental means 'not perceived.' The author answers that the first meaning (dependency) is different; the objection only applies to the second meaning.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

( وثانيها : هاهنا محسوسات مشتركة ) أي يشترك في إدراكها الحواس الظاهرة فلا يحتاج في الإحساس بها إلى قوى أخرى ( كالمقادير والأعداد والأوضاع ) والأشكال والحركة والسكون والقرب والبعد والمماسة فلو وجب لكل نوع محسوس قوة على حده كما ذهب إليه جمع (لوجب إثبات قوى أخرى ) لإدراك هذه الأمور لأنها أنواع متخالفة ( وقد يجاب عنها بأنها محسوسة بالعرض لا بالذات ( أي بالتبعية لا بالأصالة فلا حاجة فيها إلى قوة أخرى كما أشرنا إليه إنما ذاك فيما هو محسوس بالذات وقد بين كونها محسوسة بالعرض بقوله: وأنها إنما تحس بواسطة اللون والضوء والحرارة والبرودة ونحوها) وتفصيله أن يقال : إن البصر يحس بالعظم والعدد والوضع والشكل والحركة والسكون والمماسة يتوسط الضوء ،واللون واللمس يدرك جميعها بتوسط حر أو برد وصلابه أو لين والذوق يدرك العظم بأن يذوق طعماً كثيراً، والعدد بأن يجد طعوماً مختلفة، والشم يدرك العدد بضرب من القياس وهو أن يعلم أن الذي انقطعت رائحته غير الذي حصلت رائحته ثانياً، ويدرك الحركة والسكون بواسطة اللمس إدراكا ضعيفاً وأما السمع فإنه لا يدرك العظم ولكنه قد يدل عليه أحياناً من جهة أن الأصوات العظيمة إنما تحصل في الأغلب من أجسام عظيمة ( وقد يستعان فيه ) أي في إدراك بعضها ( بالعقل) كما في إدراك الحركة والسكون لأن الجسم المتحرك لا بد أن تختلف نسبته إلى أجسام أخرى كان يصير قريباً من جسم كان بعيداً عنه عنه وبالعكس. فإذا حصل الإحساس بذلك الاختلاف من جهته حصل الشعور بكونه متحركاً، ولذلك قد لا يدرك في بعض الأوقات كراكب السفينة يراها ساكنة مع كونها متحركة حركة سريعة (و) يرى (الشط متحركاً) مع كونه ساكناً فإنه لما لم يشعر بأن اختلاف نسبتها إلى الشط إنما هو من جهتها لم يشعر بحركتها بل أسنده إلى الشط فتوهمه متحركاً. وقد وقد مر استعانة الشم والسمع بالعقل في العدد والعظم ثم أشار إلى معنى آخر للمحسوس بالعرض بقوله : ( وقد يقال : المحسوس بالعرض ما لا يحس به أصلا لكن يقارن المحسوس بالحقيقة كإبصارنا أبا عمرو، فإن المحسوس ذلك الشخص وليس كونه أبا عمرو محسوساً أصلا لا أصالة ولا تبعاً بخلاف الأمور السابقة فإنها محسوسة بالتبعية فإطلاق المحسوس بالعرض على هذين المعنيين بالاشتراك اللفظي وبهذا خرج الجواب عما ذكره في المباحث المشرقية من أن هذه الأمور ليست محسوسة بالعرض لأن المحسوس بالعرض ما لا يحس به حقيقة، لكنه مقارن للمحسوس الحقيقي، وإن شئت حقيقة الحال فاستمع لهذا المقال، الست قد سمعت
And the second of them: here are common sensibles, i.e., the external senses share in their perception, so there is no need in sensing them for other faculties, such as magnitudes, numbers, positions, shapes, motion, rest, proximity, distance, and contact. If it were necessary for each type of sensible a separate faculty as a group held, it would be necessary to establish other faculties for perceiving these things because they are diverse types. And it may be answered about them that they are perceived incidentally, not essentially, i.e., by way of dependency, not originally. So there is no need for another faculty, as we indicated; that is only for what is essentially perceived. And he explained that they are perceived incidentally by his saying: that they are only sensed through the medium of color, light, heat, cold, and the like. Its detail is to say: sight perceives magnitude, number, position, shape, motion, rest, and contact through the medium of light and color; touch perceives all of them through the medium of heat, cold, hardness, or softness; taste perceives magnitude by tasting many flavors, and number by finding different flavors; smell perceives number by a kind of measurement, which is to know that whose scent ceased is other than that whose scent occurred secondly, and it perceives motion and rest through touch weakly. As for hearing, it does not perceive magnitude, but it may sometimes indicate it from the fact that great sounds mostly arise from great bodies. And reason may be used for it, i.e., in perceiving some of them, as in perceiving motion and rest, because the moving body must have its relation to other bodies differ; it may become near to a body that was far from it, and vice versa. So when sensation of that difference occurs from its side, consciousness of its being moving occurs. Therefore, it may not be perceived sometimes, like the rider of a ship seeing it stationary while it is moving quickly, and sees the shore moving while it is stationary. Because he did not feel that the difference of its relation to the shore is from its side, he did not feel its motion but attributed it to the shore and imagined it moving. And the use of reason by smell and hearing in number and magnitude has already been mentioned. Then he indicated another meaning for 'perceived incidentally' by his saying: 'And it may be said: the incidentally perceived is what is not perceived at all, but accompanies the truly perceived, like our seeing 'Amr: for the perceived is that person, and his being 'Amr is not perceived at all, neither originally nor dependently, unlike the previous matters, for they are perceived dependently. So the application of 'incidentally perceived' to these two meanings is by homonymy. And with this the answer emerges to what was mentioned in the Eastern investigations that these matters are not perceived incidentally, because the incidentally perceived is what is not perceived in reality but is concomitant with the real perceived. And if you wish the truth of the matter, listen to this discourse; have you not heard?'
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation. It presents the objection that common sensibles (like magnitude, number, position, shape, motion, etc.) would require additional faculties if each type required its own power. The answer is that these are perceived incidentally (بالعرض) through the other sensibles (like color, light, heat, etc.), not essentially. Then it explains how each sense perceives them incidentally: sight via light and color; touch via heat/cold, hardness/softness; taste via number of flavors; smell via inference; hearing via indication. Reason aids in some perceptions, like motion. Finally, it distinguishes two meanings of 'incidental': one where something is perceived by dependency (the common sensibles), and another where something is not perceived at all but is accompanied (like recognizing a person as 'Amr). The objection from the Mashriqi discussions is answered by this homonymy.

Key Terms
محسوسات مشتركة
محسوس بالعرض
محسوس بالذات
بالتبعية
بالأصالة
الاشتراك اللفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That incidental perception means something is not perceived at all (like 'Amr's identity).
Correction: There are two meanings: dependency-based (like numbers) and accompaniment (like 'Amr). Common sensibles follow the first meaning.

Checkpoint

B) Through a medium
C) Through indication from loud sounds
D) Not at all

Key Takeaway
Incidental perception has two meanings: by dependency and by accompaniment, and this resolves the objection from the Eastern investigations.

?
The passage distinguishes between what is perceived dependently (common sensibles) and what merely accompanies perception (like recognizing a person). When you perform acts of worship, are they dependent expressions of your faith, or do they merely accompany your daily routine without being rooted in true understanding? How can you transform a routine act into a dependently perceived act of worship?
?
The author uses reason to correct sensory misperception (the ship example). In your spiritual journey, where might your 'senses' be deceiving you about your own state—perhaps your comfort, your rituals, or your self-image? What would it mean to honestly apply reason to uncover the true condition of your heart today?

Active Powers and Principles of Voluntary Action
Classifies active powers and enumerates the four principles of voluntary action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of active powers into impelling and moving, and the four ordered principles of voluntary action.

Core Idea
Voluntary action follows a four-step sequence: representation, desire, will, and movement.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif classifies the active powers (quwwa faʻila) that drive voluntary actions. The author explains the distinction between the impelling power that motivates and the moving power that executes, then lays out the four principles that must occur in order for any voluntary movement to take place. Pay close attention to the terminology—these concepts are building blocks for later discussions on human agency and divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without parsing it. Then read the English translation. Let the overall structure sink in before analysing details.
2
Identify the two main divisions of active powers
The passage divides active powers into impelling (baaʻitha) and moving (muharrika). Notice how each is further described.
The impelling power is also called appetitive/striving, and splits into concupiscent and irascible.
3
Study the four principles of voluntary action
The four ordered principles are: representation (tasawwur), desire (shawq), will (irada), and movement. Understand why each is necessary in this order.
The movement itself is the proximate effect of the moving power.
4
Reflect on the minority opinion about desire
A minority opinion states that desire only arises when power is incomplete. Consider how this relates to the concept of complete power in the agent.
This connects to later discussions on divine attributes.
5
Review the commentary on the term 'active' vs 'motive'
The gloss explains why the author chooses 'active' over 'motive'—it is more comprehensive. Note the lexical notes as well.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث : القوى الفاعلة) هي التي عبر عنها فيما سبق بالمحركة على معنى أن لها مدخلاً في الحركة إما بالتحريك أو الإعانة على قياس ما مر في المدركة وفائدة العدول ظاهرة ( وتنقسم إلى ( قوة ( باعثة ) على الحركة (و) قوة ( محركة) مباشرة للتحريك . ( أما الباعثة ) وتسمى شوقية ونزوعة ( فإما لجلب النفع وتسمى شهوية وإما لدفع الضرر وتسمى غضبية. وأما المحركة فهي التي تمدد الأعصاب ) بتشنيج العضلات ( فتقرب الأعضاء إلى مباديها كما في قبض اليد ) مثلاً ( وترخيها ) أي ترخي الأعصاب بإرخاء العضلات ( فتبعد الأعضاء عن مبادئها كما في البسط ) أي بسط اليد، وهذه القوة ) المنبتة في العضلات ( هي المبدأ القريب ( للحركة والمبدأ البعيد ) هو ( التصور وبينهما الشوق والإرادة) فهذه مبادئ أربعة مترتبة للأفعال الاختيارية الصادرة عن الحيوان ( فإن النفس تتصور الحركة) أولاً، ( فتشتاق إليها ) ثانياً، بناءً على اعتقاد نفع فيها ( فتريدها ) ثالثاً، (إرادة قصد ) إليها ( وإيجاد لها فتحصل الحركة بتمديد الأعصاب وإرخائها رابعاً، وقال بعضهم: الشوق إنما يوجد فيمن ليس قدرته تامة فتردد وتشتاق وأما الذي يثق بقدرته فلا شوق له . قوله : ( وفائدة العدول ظاهرة ولعل فائدة العدول هي التنبيه على أن تلك القوى كما سميت محركة سميت أيضاً فاعلة والأولى من ذلك أن يقال الفاعلية كما تتناول أيضاً نفس الأخذ والبطش والقبض والبسط مثلا يترتب على المحركية والمحركية لا تتناول نفس الأخذ والبطش ونحوهما فتكون الفاعلية أشمل من المحركة. وقوله : نزوعة يقال : نزع إلى أهله أي اشتاق . وقوله : بتشنيج العضلات الشنج بفتح الشين المعجمة وسكون النون هو تقبض في الجلد وشنجه تشنيجاً كذا في الصحاح.
The third type: the active powers, which were previously referred to as the motive power, meaning that they have a role in movement, either by causing motion or by assisting it, analogous to what was mentioned in the perceiving powers, and the benefit of the change is apparent. And they are divided into a power that impels towards movement and a power that directly moves. As for the impelling power, also called appetitive and striving, it is either for attracting benefit and called concupiscent, or for repelling harm and called irascible. As for the moving power, it is that which extends the nerves by contracting the muscles, thus bringing the limbs closer to their origins, as in grasping the hand, and relaxes them, i.e., relaxes the nerves by relaxing the muscles, thus moving the limbs away from their origins, as in extending the hand. This power, implanted in the muscles, is the proximate principle of movement, while the remote principle is the mental representation, and between them are desire and will. So these are four ordered principles of voluntary actions emanating from the animal: for the soul first represents the movement, then desires it based on a belief of benefit, then wills it with an intention to seek and produce it, then movement occurs by extending and relaxing the nerves. And some said: desire only exists in one whose power is not complete, so he hesitates and desires, but one who is confident in his power has no desire. His saying: 'The benefit of the change is apparent.' Perhaps the benefit of the change is to indicate that these powers, as they are called motive, are also called active. The more appropriate is that activity includes also the very acts of taking, seizing, grasping, and extending, which follow from motive power, while motive power does not include the very acts of taking and seizing etc., so activity is more comprehensive than motive power. And his saying: 'striving' – one says 'nazaʿa ilā ahlihi' i.e., yearned. And his saying: 'by contracting the muscles' – al-shanaj (with fatḥa on shīn and sukūn on nūn) is a contraction in the skin, and 'shanajahu tashnījan' thus in al-Ṣiḥāḥ.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the active powers (al-quwwa al-fāʿila) and the principles of voluntary action. It begins by identifying the active powers with what was earlier called the motive power (muḥarrika), noting their role in causing or assisting movement. The division is into two: the impelling power (bāʿitha), which pushes towards action, and the moving power (muḥarrika), which directly executes movement. The impelling power is further split into concupiscent (shahwiyya) for seeking benefit and irascible (ghaḍabiyya) for repelling harm. The moving power works by extending or relaxing nerves and muscles. The passage then states the four ordered principles of voluntary action: (1) mental representation (taṣawwur) of the movement, (2) desire (shawq) arising from belief in benefit, (3) will (irāda) as intention and volition to produce it, and (4) the actual movement through nervous and muscular action. A minority opinion is noted that desire only exists in those lacking complete power. The gloss explains the benefit of using the term 'active' (fāʿila) instead of 'motive' (muḥarrika) – the former is more comprehensive, including the very acts like taking and grasping. Lexical notes follow on 'nazūʿa' and 'shanaj'.

Key Terms
القوى الفاعلة
الباعثة
الشوقية
النزوعية
الشهوية
الغضبية
المحركة
التصور
الشوق
الإرادة
مبادئ الأفعال الاختيارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the moving power alone causes movement, neglecting the role of representation, desire, and will.
Correction: The passage makes clear that movement is only the proximate principle; the remote principle is representation, and between them come desire and will. All four are necessary principles of voluntary action.

Checkpoint
What are the two main divisions of active powers according to this passage?
A) Perceiving and moving
B) Impelling and moving
C) Concupiscent and irascible
D) Theoretical and practical

Key Takeaway
Voluntary action requires four ordered principles: representation of the act, desire, will, and muscular movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you consciously represent the actions you take before you desire them, or do you often act impulsively? How can you train yourself to pause at the first step of representation to avoid regret?
?
The passage says that desire arises from a belief of benefit, but also that it indicates incomplete power. Where in your life do your strongest desires reveal a lack of trust in God's power, and what would it look like to act from confidence instead?
?
When you will an action, do you truly intend it as a deliberate choice or do you merely follow habit? How would examining your intentions according to these four principles change the quality of your deeds?

Definition of Vapor and Smoke
Defines vapor and smoke and describes their ascent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of vapor and smoke in classical cosmology and their role in forming atmospheric phenomena.

Core Idea
Vapor and smoke are distinct exhalations based on composition and weight.

In this lesson, we explore how the heat of the sun and other sources raise different particles from the earth. The author distinguishes between vapor and smoke by their composition and behavior, correcting a common misconception about smoke. Understanding these definitions is foundational for studying atmospheric effects in Islamic cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overview of the content.
Pay attention to the technical terms بخار and دخان.
2
Identify the two types of exhalations
Notice how the passage classifies exhalations into two categories based on their components: vapor (aerial and aqueous) and smoke (fiery and earthy).
3
Understand the characteristics: weight and ascent
The passage states that vapor ascends heavily, while smoke ascends lightly. Reflect on why this might be significant.
4
Examine the correction of common misunderstanding
The author clarifies that smoke is not limited to black smoke from fire. Consider what other forms of smoke might exist.
5
Reflect on the mixing and atmospheric effects
The passage concludes that vapor and smoke usually ascend mixed and from them arise atmospheric phenomena. Think about examples of such phenomena.
This connects to broader natural philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن حر الشمس وغيرها يصعد إلى الجو أجزاء إما هوائية ومائية مختلطتين وهو البخار وصعوده ثقيل، وإما نارية وأرضية وهو الدخان وصعوده خفيف، وليس ينحصر الدخان كما تعورف في الجسم الأسود الذي يرتفع مما يحترق بالنار، وقلما يصعد البخار والدخان ساذجا بل يتصاعدان في الأغلب ممتزجين، ومنهما يتكون الآثار العلوية.
Know that the heat of the sun and others raises to the atmosphere parts: either aerial and aqueous mixed together, which is vapor, and its ascent is heavy; or fiery and earthy, which is smoke, and its ascent is light. Smoke is not limited, as is commonly known, to the black body that rises from what burns with fire. Rarely do vapor and smoke ascend pure; rather they ascend in most cases mixed together, and from them are formed the atmospheric effects.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage provides a definition and classification of two types of exhalations (بخار and دخان) that rise from the earth due to heat. It distinguishes them by composition: vapor is a mixture of air and water, smoke of fire and earth. It also notes their weight difference, with vapor being heavy and smoke light. The author clarifies that smoke is not restricted to the common notion of black smoke from fire. Finally, he states that they usually ascend mixed and are the source of atmospheric phenomena.

Key Terms
بخار
دخان
آثار علوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think smoke is only the black smoke from fire.
Correction: Smoke includes any fiery and earthy exhalation, not just black smoke from burning.

Checkpoint

B) Vapor is heavy, smoke is light.
C) Vapor is black, smoke is white.
D) Vapor comes from fire, smoke from water.

Key Takeaway
Vapor and smoke are defined by their composition (air/water vs earth/fire) and ascent properties (heavy vs light), and they are not always pure but mix to form atmospheric phenomena.

How can this knowledge transform your next glance at a cloud? Will you pause and make a conscious act of reflection (tafakkur) the next time you see rain?

The author says 'know that' with authority, calling you to pay attention. Are you truly seeking to know the details of Allah's creation, or are you content with surface-level understanding? What is one natural phenomenon you encounter regularly that you could investigate further to increase your awe of the Creator? What step will you take today toward that knowledge?

Rain, Snow, and Hail from Vapor in the Frigid Layer
Explains vapor's transformation into rain, snow, or hail in the frigid layer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how vapor reaching the frigid layer leads to different forms of precipitation based on cold strength and freezing timing.

Core Idea
Rain, snow, and hail are distinguished by when water droplets freeze.

This passage from al-Mawaqif explains the physical process behind rain, snow, and hail. The author traces the journey of vapor from the air to the frigid layer, showing how different conditions yield different precipitation. Understanding this process reveals a coherent natural account in classical Islamic cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without interpreting. Then read the translation. Let the author's own description come through before you analyze.
Notice the two initial conditions: little vapor with intense heat, or abundant vapor with insufficient heat.
2
Identify the role of the frigid layer
The frigid layer (الطبقة الزمهريرية) is the cold air region that condenses vapor into cloud. Understand how cold acts here.
The author assumes you already know what this layer is.
3
Distinguish rain, snow, and hail
The passage gives three types based on freezing timing: rain (no freezing), snow (freezing before aggregation), hail (freezing after aggregation). Note the key difference.
Hail is not just frozen rain; the timing matters.
4
Understand hail's round shape
The final sentence explains why hail becomes spherical: rapid motion through air erases angles from frozen droplets.
This shows the author's attention to physical detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما البخار فإن قل واشتد الحر في الهواء حلل الأجزاء المائية وقلبها إلى الهوائية وبقي الهواء الصرف وإلا أي وإن لم يكن الأمر كذلك بل كان البخار كثيراً ولم يكن في الهواء من الحرارة ما يحلله فإن وصل ذلك البخار بصعوده إلى الطبقة الزمهريرية التي هي الهواء البارد كما عرفت عقده ببرده وتكاثف فصار سحاباً وتقاطرت الأجزاء المائية إما بلا جمود إذا لم يكن البرد شديداً وهو المطر وإما مع جمود إذا كان البرد شديداً فإن كان الجمود قبل الاجتماع والتقاطر وصيرورته حبات كباراً فهو الثلج وإن كان الجمود بعده فهو البرد، وإنما يستدير ويصير كالكرة بالحركة السريعة الخارقة للهواء بمصادمته فتنمحي الزوايا عن جوانب القطرات المنجمدة.
As for vapor, if it is little and the heat in the air is intense, it dissolves the aqueous parts and turns them into aerial, and pure air remains. Otherwise—that is, if the case is not so, but rather the vapor is abundant and there is not in the air enough heat to dissolve it—then if that vapor reaches in its ascent the frigid layer, which is cold air as you have learned, it condenses it by its cold and thickens, so it becomes cloud, and the aqueous parts drip down either without freezing if the cold is not intense—and that is rain—or with freezing if the cold is intense. If the freezing is before aggregation and dripping and its becoming large grains, it is snow; and if it is after, it is hail. And it becomes round and like a sphere by the rapid motion that pierces the air through its collision, so the angles are effaced from the sides of the frozen droplets.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage explains the behavior of vapor under two conditions. First, if vapor is little and heat is strong, it simply dissolves into air. Second, if vapor is abundant and heat insufficient, it rises to the frigid layer (الطبقة الزمهريرية) where it condenses into cloud. The resulting precipitation depends on cold intensity: rain if no freezing, snow if freezing occurs before the water droplets aggregate into large grains, and hail if freezing occurs after aggregation. The round shape of hail is explained by rapid motion through air, which erases angular edges.

Key Terms

طبقة زمهريرية
سحاب
مطر
ثلج
برد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hail is simply frozen rain.
Correction: Hail is distinguished from snow by the timing of freezing: freezing occurs after droplets aggregate, whereas snow freezes before aggregation.

Checkpoint

A) The amount of vapor in the air.
B) The intensity of cold and the timing of freezing.
C) The speed of the wind.

Key Takeaway
Rain, snow, and hail are determined by the intensity of cold and the timing of freezing relative to droplet formation.

How Smoke Creates Thunder, Lightning, and Thunderbolts
Explains how smoke trapped in clouds produces thunder, lightning, and thunderbolts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how trapped smoke in clouds produces thunder, lightning, and thunderbolts through physical processes of tearing, friction, and ignition.

Core Idea
Thunder, lightning, and thunderbolts all result from smoke trapped inside clouds.

This passage explains the natural phenomena of thunder, lightning, and thunderbolts as consequences of smoke mixing with clouds in the frigid layer. It describes how the smoke's motion and density determine which phenomenon occurs, and it even recounts historical observations. Understanding these explanations helps us see how classical scholars integrated physics and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the explanation. Note the three phenomena and the author's logical flow.
2
Identify the three products
Find where the author describes thunder, lightning, and thunderbolt. Notice how each is defined by the behavior of smoke.
Thunder is from tearing, lightning and thunderbolt from ignition.
3
Differentiate lightning and thunderbolt
The passage distinguishes them by density: fine smoke extinguishes quickly (lightning), dense smoke reaches ground (thunderbolt).
4
Examine thunderbolt effects
Read about the two types: one melts metals without burning, another burns and crushes. Note the anecdote from Shiraz as evidence.
5
Trace the smoke's ultimate fate
The passage ends with smoke reaching the sphere of fire and igniting like a candle. Consider how this fits the overall explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدخان فربما يخالط السحاب بأن ترفع أبخرة وأدخنة كثيرة مختلطة إلى الطبقة الزمهريرية، فيتكاثف البخار وينعقد سحابا فينحبس ذلك الدخان في جوف السحاب فيخرقه إما في صعوده بالطبع لبقائه على حرارته المقتضية لتصعيده، أو عند هبوطه للتكاثف أي لتكاثفه بالبرد الشديد الواصل إليه فيحدث من خرقه له ومصاكته إياه صوت هو الرعد، وقد يشتعل الدخان بقوة التسخين الحاصل من الحركة والمصاكة، فإذا اشتعل فلطيفه ينطفئ سريعاً وهو البرق، وكثيفه لا ينطفئ إلى أن يصل إلى الأرض وهو الصاعقة، وإذا وصل إليها فربما صار لطيفاً ينفذ في المتخلخل ولا يحرقه ويذيب الأجسام المندمجة فيذيب الذهب والفضة في الصرة مثلاً ولا يحرقها إلا ما احترق من الذوب. وقد أخبرنا أهل التواتر بأن الصاعقة وقعت بشيراز على قبة الشيخ الكبير أبي عبد الله بن خفيف قدس سره، فأذاب قنديلاً فيها، ولم يحرق شيئاً منها. وربما كان كثيفاً غليظاً جداً فيحرق كل شيء أصابه، وكثيراً ما يقع على الجبل فيدكه دكاً. ويحكى أن صبياً كان في صحراء فأصاب ساقيه صاعقة فسقط رجلاه ولم يخرج منه دم لحصول الكي بحرارتها. وأنه أعني الدخان قد يصل إلى كرة النار وذلك لأنه أجزاء أرضية يابسة جداً فيحفظ الحرارة التي يصعدها بخلاف البخار فيحترق الدخان حينئذ كالشمعة التي تطفأ.
As for smoke, it may mix with the cloud, by the raising of many mixed vapors and smokes to the frigid layer; then the vapor thickens and condenses into cloud, and that smoke becomes trapped inside the cloud. Then it tears through it either in its natural ascent due to its remaining heat that necessitates its rising, or at its descent due to thickening—that is, its thickening by the intense cold reaching it. And from its tearing through it and its rubbing against it arises a sound, which is thunder. And the smoke may ignite by the force of heating resulting from motion and friction. When it ignites, its fine part extinguishes quickly—that is lightning; and its dense part does not extinguish until it reaches the ground—that is the thunderbolt. When it reaches the ground, it may become fine, penetrating porous things without burning them, but it melts compacted bodies, so it melts gold and silver in a purse, for example, and does not burn the purse except what is burnt by the melting. The people of transmission have informed us that a thunderbolt fell in Shiraz on the dome of the great Shaykh Abu 'Abd Allah ibn Khafif (may his secret be sanctified), and it melted a lamp in it and did not burn anything from it. And sometimes it is very dense and thick, and then it burns everything it strikes, and often it strikes a mountain and crushes it. It is related that a boy was in a desert, and a thunderbolt struck his legs; his legs fell off and no blood came out due to the cauterization by its heat. And that—that is, the smoke—may reach the sphere of fire, because it is very dry earthy parts, so it preserves the heat that it ascends with, unlike vapor; then the smoke ignites at that time like a candle that goes out.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage explains the behavior of smoke in the atmosphere. When smoke mixes with cloud in the frigid layer, it becomes trapped. It then tears through the cloud either by rising or descending, causing friction that produces thunder (الرعد). The smoke may also ignite from the heat of motion and friction. Fine smoke extinguishes quickly as lightning (البرق), while dense smoke reaches the ground as a thunderbolt (الصاعقة). The thunderbolt can have different effects: sometimes it melts metals without burning the container, as in the Shiraz dome story; sometimes it is extremely dense and burns or crushes. The smoke can also rise to the sphere of fire and ignite like a candle.

برق
صاعقة

Misconception: Thunder is caused by clouds colliding.
Correction: The text explains thunder is the sound of smoke tearing through the cloud due to friction, not cloud collision.

Dew, Frost, and Clouds from Vapor and Air
Explains how vapor condenses by night cold into dew or frost, and how clouds form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how vapor condenses to form dew and frost, how clouds can also form from air contraction, and the distinction between the two processes.

Core Idea
Dew and frost form from vapor condensation; clouds can also form from air contraction.

This passage from al-Mawaqif explains everyday meteorological phenomena—dew, frost, and clouds—through the lens of condensation. It not only defines each term but also introduces an important nuance: while most such phenomena arise from vapor, some, according to Imam al-Razi, may result from air contraction. Let's examine the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then read the English translation. Notice the key terms: البخار, الطل, الصقيع, السحاب.
The passage uses a cause-and-effect structure.
2
Identify definitions and relationships
Find how the author defines dew (طل) and frost (صقيع) and the analogy he draws between them and rain/snow. Understand what distinguishes the two.
The ratio mentioned is proportional: frost is to dew as snow is to rain.
3
Note al-Razi's commentary
Pay attention to the final sentence by Imam al-Razi. It clarifies that most of these phenomena come from vapor, but a minority come from air contraction. This adds a layer of nuance.
This distinguishes the author's view from a purely vapor-based explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه قد يتكاثف ببرد الليل فينزل نزولاً ثقيلاً في أجزاء صغار لا يحس بنزولها إلا عند اجتماع شيء يعتد به إما بلا جمود بعد النزول وهو الطل، أو معه وهو الصقيع ونسبته إلى الطل كنسبة الثلج إلى المطر. وقد يتكون السحاب من انقباض الهواء بالبرد الشديد فيحصل حينئذ منه الأقسام المذكورة. قال الإمام الرازي: إن تكون هذه الأشياء في الأكثر من تكاثف البخار وفي الأقل من تكاثف الهواء.
For it may condense by the cold of the night and descend heavily in small particles whose descent is not perceived except when a considerable amount collects, either without freezing after descent, and that is dew, or with freezing, and that is frost; and its ratio to dew is like the ratio of snow to rain. And clouds may form from the contraction of air by severe cold, and then from that arise the sections mentioned. Imam al-Razi said: The formation of these things is mostly from condensation of vapor and less often from condensation of air.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies phenomena resulting from the condensation of vapor. It first describes the process: vapor condenses due to the cold of the night and falls in tiny particles. When these particles accumulate noticeably, they form either dew (if no freezing occurs after descent) or frost (if freezing occurs). The relationship between frost and dew is compared to that between snow and rain. The passage then addresses cloud formation, noting that clouds can also result from the contraction of air due to severe cold. Finally, it quotes Imam al-Razi to establish that most of these phenomena are due to vapor condensation, while a minority are due to air contraction. The passage proceeds in the author's own order: definition of dew, definition of frost, analogy, then cloud formation and the authority's opinion.

Thunder from Smoke Trapped in Clouds
Describes how smoke mixing with clouds produces thunder by ripping through the cloud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physical mechanism of thunder as described in kalam cosmology.

Core Idea
Thunder is the sound of smoke tearing through clouds.

This passage explains how the natural phenomenon of thunder is accounted for within the kalam framework. Rather than invoking angels or supernatural causes, it provides a material explanation based on the mixing of smoke and vapor in the atmosphere. Understanding this helps see how kalam thinkers integrated empirical observations into their worldview.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the causal sequence become clear.
Notice the technical terms: دخان (smoke), سحاب (cloud), طبقة زمهريرية (freezing layer).
2
Identify the key components
List the main elements: smoke, vapor, cloud, freezing layer, tearing, friction, sound. Understand their roles.
3
Trace the causal chain
Follow the steps: raising of vapors and smokes → condensation into cloud → smoke trapped → tears through naturally or due to cold → friction produces sound.
The passage explains both ascent and descent scenarios.
4
Connect to kalam worldview
Consider why this explanation matters: it shows that kalam accepts natural causality while still affirming that these are created and sustained by Allah.
This is not a rejection of divine involvement but a description of secondary causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدخان فربما يخالط السحاب بأن ترفع أبخرة وأدخنة كثيرة مختلطة إلى الطبقة الزمهريرية، فيتكاثف البخار وينعقد سحابا فينحبس ذلك الدخان في جوف السحاب فيخرقه إما في صعوده بالطبع لبقائه على حرارته المقتضية لتصعيده، أو عند هبوطه للتكاثف أي لتكاثفه بالبرد الشديد الواصل إليه فيحدث من خرقه له أي خرق الدخان وتمزيقه للسحاب صاعداً أو هابطاً ومصاكته إياه صوت هو الرعد.
As for smoke, it may mix with the cloud by the raising of many mixed vapors and smokes to the freezing layer; then the vapor condenses and congeals into a cloud, and that smoke becomes trapped inside the cloud. It then tears through it, either in its natural ascent because it retains its heat which necessitates its rising, or at the time of its descent for condensation—that is, due to its condensation by the severe cold reaching it. Then from its tearing through it (that is, the smoke's tearing and ripping of the cloud while ascending or descending) and its friction against it, a sound is produced: and that is thunder.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage explains the physical cause of thunder. The author posits that smoke rises together with vapor to a cold atmospheric layer. The vapor condenses into a cloud, trapping the smoke inside. The smoke, being hot, either continues to rise naturally or descends due to condensation from the cold. In either case, it tears through the cloud. The tearing and friction of the smoke against the cloud produce a sound, which is thunder. The passage is a causal explanation: it identifies the material (smoke), the mechanism (tearing and friction), and the result (sound as thunder).

الرعد
الطبقة الزمهريرية
مصاكة

Key Takeaway
Thunder is the sound produced by smoke tearing and rubbing against the cloud as it ascends or descends due to temperature differences.

The Ignition of Smoke and the Origin of Lightning and Thunderbolt
Explains the ignition of smoke and the differentiation of lightning and thunderbolt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the composition of smoke and mechanical actions cause it to ignite, leading to the formation of lightning and thunderbolt.

Core Idea
Lightning and thunderbolt arise from ignited smoke dividing into subtle and dense parts.

We begin by examining the natural process that transforms smoke into lightning and thunderbolt. The author explains how smoke's inherent composition—subtle, with water and earth—combined with heat and motion, makes it highly flammable. Strong heating from friction then ignites it, and the ignition results in two distinct phenomena: the quickly extinguishing lightning and the persistent thunderbolt.

Guided Path
1
Understand the composition of smoke
Notice that smoke is described as subtle, containing wateriness and earthiness.
2
Grasp the effect of heat, motion, and mixing agitation
These actions work on the smoke to bring its temperament close to oiliness.
3
Learn why smoke becomes highly flammable
The smoke becomes so flammable that it ignites with the slightest cause.
4
See the division into lightning and thunderbolt
Upon ignition, the subtle part quickly extinguishes (lightning), while the dense part reaches the earth (thunderbolt).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يشتعل الدخان بقوة التسخين وذلك لأنه شيء لطيف وفيه مائية وأرضية عمل فيهما الحرارة والحركة والخلخلة المازجة عملا قرب مزاجه من الدهنية، فصار بحيث يشتعل بأدنى سبب مشتعل، فكيف لا يشتعل بالتسخين القوي الحاصل من الحركة الشديدة والمصاكة العنيفة؟ وإذا اشتعل فلطيفه ينطفئ سريعاً وهو البرق، وكثيفه لا ينطفئ إلى أن يصل إلى الأرض وهو الصاعقة. وإذا وصل إليها فربما صار لطيفاً ينفذ في المتخلخل ولا يحرقه ويذيب الأجسام المندمجة فيذيب الذهب والفضة في الصرة مثلاً ولا يحرقها إلا ما احترق من الذوب. وقد أخبرنا أهل التواتر بأن الصاعقة وقعت بشيراز على قبة الشيخ الكبير أبي عبد الله بن خفيف قدس سره، فأذاب قنديلاً فيها، ولم يحرق شيئاً منها. وربما كان كثيفاً غليظاً جداً فيحرق كل شيء أصابه، وكثيراً ما يقع على الجبل فيدكه دكاً. ويحكى أن صبياً كان في صحراء فأصاب ساقيه صاعقة فسقط رجلاه ولم يخرج منه دم لحصول الكي بحرارتها.
And the smoke may ignite from the power of heating, because it is a subtle thing containing wateriness and earthiness, upon which heat, motion, and the mixing agitation worked a work that brought its temperament close to oiliness, so it became such that it ignites with the slightest igniting cause; so how would it not ignite with the strong heating resulting from intense motion and violent friction? And when it ignites, its subtle part extinguishes quickly—and that is lightning—and its dense part does not extinguish until it reaches the earth—and that is the thunderbolt. And when it reaches the earth, it may become subtle, penetrating porous bodies without burning them, and it melts fused bodies: it melts gold and silver inside a purse, for example, without burning the purse except what is burned by the melting. And the people of tawatur have told us that a thunderbolt struck in Shiraz on the dome of the great shaykh Abu Abdullah ibn Khafif (may God sanctify his secret), and it melted a lamp inside it without burning anything of it. And sometimes it is very dense and thick, burning everything it strikes, and often strikes the mountain and crushes it completely. And it is related that a boy was in a desert and a thunderbolt struck his legs; his legs fell off and no blood came out, due to cauterization by its heat.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage explains the ignition of smoke due to its composition and the mechanical forces acting on it, leading to lightning and thunderbolt. The author first gives the cause of ignition: the smoke has a subtle nature with water and earth components, and heat, motion, and a mixing agitation make its mixture akin to oil, so it becomes highly flammable. Strong heating from motion and friction then ignites it. Upon ignition, the smoke divides into a subtle part that quickly extinguishes (lightning) and a dense part that persists until it reaches the ground (thunderbolt). The passage then discusses the properties of the thunderbolt: sometimes it becomes subtle, passes through porous objects without burning them, but melts fused metals like gold and silver inside a container without burning the container itself. A historical report from Shiraz is cited as evidence: a thunderbolt melted a lamp in the dome of Shaykh Abu Abdullah ibn Khafif but did not burn anything else. In other cases, the thunderbolt is very dense and burns or crushes whatever it hits, including mountains. An anecdote illustrates its cauterizing effect: a boy's legs were severed by a thunderbolt with no bleeding because the heat cauterized the wound. The passage moves from cause (ignition) to effect (lightning/thunderbolt) and then to empirical examples.

Key Terms

الصاعقة
الدخان
التسخين
الخلخلة المازجة

The Diverse Effects of the Thunderbolt
Describes the two types of thunderbolt and their effects with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two forms of thunderbolt—subtle and dense—and their different effects on objects, supported by historical reports.

Core Idea
Thunderbolt can melt without burning or crush and burn, depending on its density.

Having understood how lightning and thunderbolt form, we now explore the thunderbolt's behavior upon reaching the earth. The author describes two possibilities: sometimes it becomes subtle, penetrating porous bodies and melting metals without burning, as exemplified by a historical report from Shiraz. Other times it is dense, burning or crushing everything, as shown in the anecdote of the boy's legs.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of the subtle thunderbolt
Notice that it penetrates porous bodies and melts fused objects without burning the container.
2

3
Understand the dense thunderbolt's effects
It burns everything it strikes and crushes mountains.
4
