The thunderbolt severed his legs without bleeding due to cauterization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يشتعل الدخان بقوة التسخين وذلك لأنه شيء لطيف وفيه مائية وأرضية عمل فيهما الحرارة والحركة والخلخلة المازجة عملا قرب مزاجه من الدهنية، فصار بحيث يشتعل بأدنى سبب مشتعل، فكيف لا يشتعل بالتسخين القوي الحاصل من الحركة الشديدة والمصاكة العنيفة؟ وإذا اشتعل فلطيفه ينطفئ سريعاً وهو البرق، وكثيفه لا ينطفئ إلى أن يصل إلى الأرض وهو الصاعقة. وإذا وصل إليها فربما صار لطيفاً ينفذ في المتخلخل ولا يحرقه ويذيب الأجسام المندمجة فيذيب الذهب والفضة في الصرة مثلاً ولا يحرقها إلا ما احترق من الذوب. وقد أخبرنا أهل التواتر بأن الصاعقة وقعت بشيراز على قبة الشيخ الكبير أبي عبد الله بن خفيف قدس سره، فأذاب قنديلاً فيها، ولم يحرق شيئاً منها. وربما كان كثيفاً غليظاً جداً فيحرق كل شيء أصابه، وكثيراً ما يقع على الجبل فيدكه دكاً. ويحكى أن صبياً كان في صحراء فأصاب ساقيه صاعقة فسقط رجلاه ولم يخرج منه دم لحصول الكي بحرارتها.
And the smoke may ignite from the power of heating, because it is a subtle thing containing wateriness and earthiness, upon which heat, motion, and the mixing agitation worked a work that brought its temperament close to oiliness, so it became such that it ignites with the slightest igniting cause; so how would it not ignite with the strong heating resulting from intense motion and violent friction? And when it ignites, its subtle part extinguishes quickly—and that is lightning—and its dense part does not extinguish until it reaches the earth—and that is the thunderbolt. And when it reaches the earth, it may become subtle, penetrating porous bodies without burning them, and it melts fused bodies: it melts gold and silver inside a purse, for example, without burning the purse except what is burned by the melting. And the people of tawatur have told us that a thunderbolt struck in Shiraz on the dome of the great shaykh Abu Abdullah ibn Khafif (may God sanctify his secret), and it melted a lamp inside it without burning anything of it. And sometimes it is very dense and thick, burning everything it strikes, and often strikes the mountain and crushes it completely. And it is related that a boy was in a desert and a thunderbolt struck his legs; his legs fell off and no blood came out, due to cauterization by its heat.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage explains the ignition of smoke due to its composition and the mechanical forces acting on it, leading to lightning and thunderbolt. The author first gives the cause of ignition: the smoke has a subtle nature with water and earth components, and heat, motion, and a mixing agitation make its mixture akin to oil, so it becomes highly flammable. Strong heating from motion and friction then ignites it. Upon ignition, the smoke divides into a subtle part that quickly extinguishes (lightning) and a dense part that persists until it reaches the ground (thunderbolt). The passage then discusses the properties of the thunderbolt: sometimes it becomes subtle, passes through porous objects without burning them, but melts fused metals like gold and silver inside a container without burning the container itself. A historical report from Shiraz is cited as evidence: a thunderbolt melted a lamp in the dome of Shaykh Abu Abdullah ibn Khafif but did not burn anything else. In other cases, the thunderbolt is very dense and burns or crushes whatever it hits, including mountains. An anecdote illustrates its cauterizing effect: a boy's legs were severed by a thunderbolt with no bleeding because the heat cauterized the wound. The passage moves from cause (ignition) to effect (lightning/thunderbolt) and then to empirical examples.

Key Terms
الصاعقة
التواتر
الإذابة
الكي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thunderbolts always burn everything they touch, never just melt objects without burning.
Correction: The passage shows that thunderbolts can be subtle and melt objects without burning the surroundings, as in the Shiraz example.

Checkpoint
What happens to the thunderbolt when it is subtle after reaching earth?
A) It burns everything.
B) It penetrates porous bodies without burning and melts fused objects.
C) It crushes mountains.
D) It extinguishes immediately.

Key Takeaway
Thunderbolt's effects vary with its density, ranging from gentle melting to violent destruction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The thunderbolt melted gold and silver inside a purse without burning the purse. What treasures in your life (wealth, status, relationships) are you holding in a 'purse' that you think is secure, but God might melt away without destroying your core? Are you ready to have your attachments refined without losing your faith?
?
The boy's legs were cauterized so that no blood came out—a mercy hidden in tragedy. Think of a recent trial in your life that felt purely destructive. Can you identify a way in which that same trial actually protected you or prevented a greater harm? If not, will you trust that the All-Wise knows the cauterizing effect you cannot see?
?
The report from Shiraz was confirmed by tawatur—certain knowledge. How certain are you that God's wisdom is present in every event, even the ones you don't understand? Based on this lesson, what practical step will you take this week to respond to a difficult situation with trust rather than complaint?

Smoke Reaching the Sphere of Fire and the Candle Analogy
Describes how smoke reaches the sphere of fire and the resulting shooting stars and comets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how dry smoke preserves heat and burns upon reaching the sphere of fire, producing shooting stars and comet-like forms.

Core Idea
Smoke acts like candle wick: reaching fire, it ignites in different forms depending on density.

The author explains that smoke, unlike vapor, can preserve heat due to its dry earthy nature. When it reaches the sphere of fire, it ignites and produces various celestial phenomena. This lesson covers the process and the first two categories: subtle smoke that becomes shooting stars, and dense smoke that forms tufts and tails.

Guided Path
1
Read the candle analogy
Start by reading the passage, focusing on the analogy of the two candles. Notice how the author uses this to explain the ignition of smoke.
The analogy illustrates how fire can travel through smoke.
2
Identify the two smoke types
Find the two categories of smoke based on density: subtle and dense. Understand the difference in how fire interacts with each.
Subtle smoke ignites quickly; dense smoke smolders.
3
Connect to observed phenomena
Relate the two types to what we see in the sky: shooting stars (subtle) and comets or tails (dense).
The author gives specific names like shihab, dhu'aba, dhanab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنه أعني الدخان قد يصل إلى كرة النار وذلك لأنه أجزاء أرضية يابسة جداً فيحفظ الحرارة التي يصعدها بخلاف البخار فيحترق الدخان حينئذ كالشمعة التي تطفأ ويحاذى بها من تحت شمعة مشتعلة فيشتعل الدخان الواصل إلى الشمعة الفوقانية وتتصل النار التي وقعت في ذلك الدخان بالشمعة السفلانية فتشتعل بهذه النار. فما كان منه أي من الدخان لطيفاً صار مشتملاً ونفذ فيه النار بسرعة فيرى ذلك المشتعل كأنه كوكب ينقض وهو الشهاب. وما كان منه كثيفاً لا في الغاية تعلق به النار تعلقاً تاماً من غير اشتعال بل ثبت فيه الاحتراق ودام متصلاً لا ينطفئ أياماً وشهوراً ويكون على صورة ذؤابة أو ذنب أو رمح أو حيوان له قرون، كما أشار إليه بقوله: وهو الذؤابات والأذناب والنيازك وذوات القرون. وما كان من البخار غليظاً أي كثيفاً جداً تعلق به النار تعلقاً ما لا تعلقاً تاماً فيحدث في الجو علامات سود أو حمر على حسب غلظ المادة فإذا كانت غليظة ظهرت الحمرة، وإذا كانت أغلظ ظهر السواد. وقد تقف الذؤابات ونحوها بجنب كوكب فيديرها الفلك معه مشايعة إياه فترى كان لذلك الكوكب ذؤابة أو ذنباً أو قرناً واحداً أو أكثر من واحد. وهذه الأقسام التي ذكرناها للدخان الواصل إلى كرة النار إذا اتصلت بالأرض أحرقت ما عليها وتسمى الحريق. وفي المباحث المشرقية: إذا ارتفع بخار دخاني لزج دهني وتصاعد حتى وصل إلى حيز النار من غير أن ينقطع اتصاله عن الأرض اشتعلت النار فيه نازلة فيرى كأن تنينا ينزل من السماء إلى الأرض. فإذا وصلت إلى الأرض أحرقت تلك المادة بالكلية وما يقرب منها وسبيل ذلك سبيل السراج المطفأ إذا وضع تحت السراج المشتعل فاتصل الدخان من الأول إلى الثاني فانحدر اللهب إلى فتيلته.
And that it—that is, the smoke—may reach the sphere of fire, because it is very dry earthy parts, so it preserves the heat that it rises with, unlike vapor. Then the smoke burns at that time like a candle that is extinguished, and it is placed under a burning candle, so the smoke reaching the upper candle ignites, and the fire that occurred in that smoke connects to the lower candle, so it ignites with that fire. That which from it—that is, from the smoke—is subtle becomes flaming, and fire penetrates it quickly, and that which is ignited is seen as if it is a falling star, and it is the shooting star. And that which is dense—not extremely—the fire clings to it completely without ignition, rather combustion is established in it and it continues unextinguished for days and months, and it takes the form of a tuft, tail, spear, or an animal with horns, as he indicated by his saying: and they are the tufts, tails, rockets, and horned ones. And that which from the vapor is thick—i.e., very dense—fire clings to it with some clinging, not complete clinging, so black or red signs occur in the atmosphere according to the density of the substance; if it is dense, redness appears, and if it is denser, blackness appears. And the tufts and the like may stop beside a planet, and the sphere rotates them with it, following it, so they are seen as if that planet had a tuft, tail, or one horn or more. And these divisions that we have mentioned for the smoke that reaches the sphere of fire, if they connect with the earth, they burn what is on it and are called fire. And in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: If a viscous oily smoky vapor rises and ascends until it reaches the region of fire without its connection to the earth being cut off, fire ignites in it descending, so it is seen as if a dragon descends from the sky to the earth. When it reaches the earth, it burns that substance entirely and what is near it, and its way is the way of an extinguished lamp if placed under a burning lamp, then the smoke from the first connects to the second, so the flame descends to its wick.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage addresses the fate of smoke that rises all the way to the sphere of fire, distinguishing it from vapor that would not preserve heat. The author uses an analogy: the smoke burns like a rekindled candle—when placed under a burning candle, its smoke carries the flame upward. From this, he divides the smoke into three categories: 1) Subtle smoke that quickly ignites and appears as a shooting star (shihab). 2) Dense (but not extremely) smoke that smolders without full flame, persisting for days or months in forms like tufts, tails, spears, or horned animals. 3) Very thick vapor that allows only partial clinging of fire, producing red or black atmospheric signs. The passage then notes that these forms can appear beside planets and be dragged by the sphere, giving the illusion of a planet having a tail or horn. Finally, it describes what happens when these fiery forms reach earth—they cause fire. A quotation from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya describes a dragon-like phenomenon descending from the sky in a continuous column of smoke that ignites. The explanation is naturalistic: the smoke acts like the wick of a lamp drawing flame downward.

Key Terms
كرة النار
الشهاب
الذؤابات
الأذناب
النيازك
ذوات القرون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the smoke that is dense but not extremely become, according to the passage?
A) A shooting star.
B) A tuft, tail, spear, or horned animal shape.
C) Black or red signs in the atmosphere.
D) A dragon descending from the sky.

Key Takeaway
Smoke reaching the sphere of fire produces either shooting stars (from subtle smoke) or comet-like forms (from dense smoke), depending on density.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The smoke in this lesson, when subtle, becomes a shooting star—quickly ignited and fleeting. Are there areas of your worship that are 'subtle'—done hastily and without depth? How can you ensure your good deeds are more like the dense smoke that burns steadily for months, enduring and luminous?
?
The candle analogy shows that smoke from a lower candle can carry fire upward. Who in your life serves as that 'lower candle'—a source of spiritual fire that ignites your faith? Are you actively placing yourself under that influence, or do you isolate yourself from it?

Thick Vapor and Other Phenomena
Describes thick vapor producing atmospheric signs, tufts near planets, and the dragon descending fire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the phenomena caused by very thick vapor, including atmospheric signs, apparent planetary appendages, and dragon-like descending fires.

Core Idea
Very thick vapor produces red/black signs; tufts near planets appear as tails; descending fire can form a dragon.

The passage continues by describing what happens when smoke is extremely dense or when it attaches to planets. It also explains the destructive effects when these fiery forms reach earth and quotes a work describing a dragon-like phenomenon.

Guided Path
1
Examine thick vapor effects
Read the description of very thick vapor: fire clings partially, creating red or black atmospheric signs. Note the inverse relationship: less thick gives red, more thick gives black.
The signs are not permanent like comets but transient.
2
Understand planetary tufts
Notice how tufts can appear stationary beside a planet. The sphere rotates them, so they seem to be appendages of the planet.
This explains why some planets appear to have tails or horns.
3
Explore the dragon phenomenon
Read the quote from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya about a descending dragon. Understand the analogy: like a lamp, the flame descends through the smoke column.
This is a special case where the smoke column is continuous.

Wind from Smoke: Two Paths to Wind
Explains how wind arises from smoke via two causal paths and defines wind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how wind, according to this cosmological model, arises from the behavior of smoke—either by being blocked at the freezing sphere or by rebounding from the sphere of fire.

Core Idea
Wind is the agitation of air caused by the motion of smoke.

In this passage, the author explains the physical mechanism behind wind. Smoke rises from the earth, but its fate depends on whether it reaches the freezing sphere or not. Either way, the air becomes disturbed, and that disturbance is what we call wind. The passage also clarifies a subtle definitional point about whether wind is 'moving air' or 'air in motion'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two scenarios introduced by 'قَدْ يَنكَسِر' and 'أو لا يَنكَسِر'.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two possible fates for rising smoke. Find each one and note what happens to the smoke and how that leads to wind.
3
Understand the rebound mechanism
The second scenario involves the smoke colliding with the sphere of fire and rebounding. Notice the analogy of a rotating stick deflecting arrows. This illustrates how the direction of wind can vary.
The author also corrects a textual variant: it is the sphere of fire, not the celestial sphere.
4
Examine the definition of wind
The passage ends with a quote from Aristotle and a comment by al-Razi. Understand the distinction between 'moving air' and 'air in motion'. This shows the importance of precise definitions in physics.
Al-Razi identifies air as the 'matter' of wind, meaning the substance that undergoes the motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً نقول: فالدخان قد ينكسر الوصول إلى الكرة الزمهريرية فيثقل فيرجع بطبعها إلى الأرض أو لا ينكسر وحينئذ يصعد ويصادم كرة النار لا الفلك على ما وقع في النسخ لأن نفوذه في النار البسيطة العالية على الإحالة إلى طبيعته غير معقول بحسب الظاهر فيرجع ويرتد بمصادمته كرة النار المتحركة بحركة الفلك رجوعاً على جهات مختلفة كما يرد بعصا دائرة سهام على جهات شتى. وعلى التقديرين فيتموج الهواء ويضطرب وهو الريح. قيل: قد وقع في كلام أرسطو أن الريح يحد بأنه متحرك وهو هواء لا بأنه هواء متحرك. قال الإمام الرازي والذي يمكن أن يقال فيه: إن الهواء مادة الريح.
And we also say: the smoke may be prevented from reaching the freezing sphere, so it becomes heavy and returns by its nature to the earth; or it is not prevented, and then it ascends and collides with the sphere of fire—not the sphere (as occurs in some copies), because its penetration into the simple high fire that alters its nature is not intelligible apparently—so it returns and rebounds by colliding with the sphere of fire, which moves with the motion of the sphere, returning in different directions, just as a circular stick repels arrows in various directions. And in both cases, the air undulates and becomes agitated, and that is wind. It was said: it occurs in the speech of Aristotle that wind is defined as 'moving air' not as 'air in motion'. Imam al-Razi said: What can be said about it is that air is the matter of wind.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage provides the physical explanation of wind from the behavior of smoke. The author sets out two possibilities: 1) The smoke is blocked from reaching the freezing sphere (al-kura az-zamhaririyya), becomes heavy, and descends back to earth. 2) The smoke is not blocked, rises and collides with the sphere of fire. However, the author notes a textual variant: it collides with the sphere of fire, not the celestial sphere (al-falak), because penetration into the simple fire is not conceivable. Instead, the smoke rebounds in different directions, like arrows deflected by a rotating stick. In either case, the air becomes undulating and agitated, and that agitation is wind. The passage then discusses the definition of wind, attributing to Aristotle the view that wind is 'moving air' (متحرك وهو هواء) rather than 'air in motion'. It concludes with Imam Razi stating that air is the material substance (matter) of wind. The passage is a causal explanation combined with a definitional clarification.

Key Terms
الدخان
الكرة الزمهريرية
كرة النار
الريح
الهواء
المادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens when smoke is NOT blocked by the freezing sphere?
A) It remains in the air and causes cooling.
B) It ascends and collides with the sphere of fire, then rebounds.
C) It dissolves into the upper atmosphere.
D) It turns into vapor and rises indefinitely.

Key Takeaway
Wind is the agitation of air caused by smoke either descending from the freezing layer or rebounding from the sphere of fire, and it is defined as moving air, with air as its matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage distinguishes between 'moving air' (wind) and 'air in motion'. In your own spiritual life, are you merely 'air in motion'—going through the motions of worship out of habit—or are you 'moving air'—with an intrinsic drive of faith? What specific habit or practice could you change today to move from accidental to intrinsic devotion?
?
Smoke follows two opposite paths (descent and ascent) yet both produce wind. Think of a recent trial in your life that felt like a descent (failure) or an ascent (struggle). How might Allah be using that very path to stir something in your heart—like wind—for a purpose you cannot yet see? Write down one step to cooperate with His plan.
?
Imam al-Razi says air is the matter of wind. What is the 'matter' (substance) of your daily actions—the underlying material of your worship, work, and relationships? Is it divine guidance (like air) or something else? Commit to one way this week to make your 'matter' align more fully with Allah's revelation.

Smoke and Fire: Classifying Celestial Phenomena
Classifies smoke-based celestial phenomena into three types by density.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic cosmology classifies meteors, comets, and other fiery sky phenomena based on the density of smoke rising from the earth.

Core Idea
The density of smoke determines whether we see a shooting star, a comet, or a dark cloud.

Before telescopes, scholars explained shooting stars and comets as earthly exhalations catching fire in the sky. This passage from a classical work on natural philosophy presents a detailed classification based on a simple experiment with candles. Let's see how smoke density dictates the fiery show above us.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the candle analogy and the three categories of smoke density.
The candle analogy is a teaching tool, not a literal claim about the sky.
2
Identify the three categories
Notice the distinction between fine smoke (latif), dense smoke (kathif), and very dense smoke (ghaliz). Each produces a different phenomenon: shooting star, comet-like object, or a dark/red sign.
3
Understand the candle analogy
The passage explains how smoke from a lower candle ignites when it reaches an upper candle. This models how earthly smoke reaches the fire sphere and ignites.
4
Reflect on the bigger picture
These phenomena are not stars but temporary events caused by earthly materials. Classical thinkers distinguished them from the fixed stars.
This corrects a common misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The first observatory - the fifth section: of compound vapors and smoke. And it is made to correspond from under a lighted candle, so the smoke (reaching the upper candle) ignites, and the fire that fell into that smoke connects (to the lower candle, and it ignites) with this fire. So what was of it, i.e., of the smoke, fine became burning and the fire penetrated it quickly, so that burning thing is seen as if it is a falling star (al-shihab). And what was of it dense, not extremely, the fire attached to it completely without ignition, but combustion was established in it, and it remained connected, not extinguishing for days and months, and it is in the form of a tuft (dhu'āba), or a tail (dhanab), or a spear, or an animal with horns, as he alluded to by his saying: 'and they are the tufts (al-dhu'ābāt), the tails (al-adhnāb), the meteors (al-niyāzik), and the horned ones (dhawāt al-qurūn). And what was of the vapor thick, i.e., very dense, the fire attached to it a certain attachment, not a complete attachment, so it produces in the sky black or red signs according to the thickness of the material: if it is thick, redness appears, and if it is thicker, blackness appears. And the tufts and the like may stop beside a planet, and the sphere rotates them with it accompanying it, so it is seen as if that planet has a tuft, or a tail, or a horn, one or more than one.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the fiery phenomena caused by smoke rising to the fire sphere. The author begins with the process: smoke from a lower candle connects to an upper candle, igniting. The nature of the resulting phenomenon depends on the density of the smoke. Fine smoke becomes meteors (shihab) that streak quickly. Dense smoke (but not extremely) ignites completely and persists as burning masses (dhu'aba, dhanab, ramh, hayawan dhu qurun). Very dense smoke only partially ignites, producing dark or red clouds. Finally, when such objects appear near a planet, they look like attachments (tufts, tails, horns).

Misconception: Shooting stars and comets are celestial bodies like planets or stars.
Correction: The passage explains they are fiery phenomena caused by earthly smoke and vapor rising to the fire sphere, not permanent celestial objects.

Checkpoint

B) The density of the smoke
C) The height of the smoke
D) The time of day

Key Takeaway
Celestial fiery phenomena like meteors and comets are classified by the density of smoke rising from the earth, not by their intrinsic nature as stars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage describes phenomena that appear to be attached to planets. In your life, what things appear to be attached to your identity but are really just carried along by the spheres you revolve in? Are you willing to distinguish what is truly from you and what is merely atmospheric?

The Descent of Fire and the Lamp Analogy
Explains how fire descends via smoke using the analogy of a lamp.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how natural philosophers explained the descent of fire from the sky using the analogy of a lamp relighting another through smoke.

Core Idea
Earthly smoke can carry fire from the sky to the earth like a lamp relighting another.

This passage from al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyyah provides a physical explanation for a phenomenon known as 'al-harīq' – fire that descends from the sky. The author uses a vivid analogy of an extinguished lamp placed beneath a lit one to show how fire can travel downward through smoke. We will examine the description and the analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the technical terms and the vivid simile of the dragon.
The passage is a single coherent explanation.
2
Understand the phenomenon
Focus on the description: a smoky, oily vapor rises without losing connection to the earth, then ignites and descends. This is what people might see as a 'dragon' coming down.
The author is providing a natural explanation, not a mythical one.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Think about the lamp analogy. How does an extinguished lamp under a lit one cause flame to descend through the smoke? Relate this to the smoke rising from the earth.
This analogy clarifies the mechanism of downward fire propagation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه الأقسام التي ذكرناها للدخان الواصل إلى كرة النار ( إذا اتصلت بالأرض أحرقت ما عليها وتسمى (الحريق وفي المباحث المشرقية : إذا ارتفع بخار دخاني لزج دهني وتصاعد حتى وصل إلى حيز النار من غير أن ينقطع اتصاله عن الأرض اشتعلت النار فيه نازلة فيرى كأن تنينا ينزل من السماء إلى الأرض. فإذا وصلت إلى الأرض أحرقت تلك المادة بالكلية وما يقرب منها وسبيل ذلك سبيل السراج المطفأ إذا وضع تحت السراج المشتعل فاتصل الدخان من الأول إلى الثاني فانحدر اللهب إلى فتيلته
And these divisions which we have mentioned for the smoke reaching the sphere of fire: when it contacts the earth, it burns what is upon it, and it is called 'al-harīq' (fire). And in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyyah: when a smoky, viscous, oily vapor rises and ascends until it reaches the region of fire without its connection to the earth being cut off, the fire ignites in it descending, so it is seen as if a dragon is descending from the sky to the earth. When it reaches the earth, it burns that material completely and what is near it. And the way of that is the way of an extinguished lamp when it is placed under a lighted lamp, so the smoke connects from the first to the second, and the flame descends to its wick.

Commentary
This passage explains the phenomenon of fire descending from the sky to the earth, known as 'al-harīq'. The author first connects it to the previously mentioned types of smoke: when such smoke contacts the earth, it causes fires. Then he quotes from al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyyah: a viscous, oily vapor rises without losing contact with the earth, ignites as it descends, appearing like a dragon. The analogy of an extinguished lamp placed under a lit lamp illustrates how the flame descends through the connecting smoke to relight the wick.

The Generation of Wind from Reflected Smoke
Explains wind generation from reflected smoke.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how wind is generated according to Aristotelian cosmology, including the two scenarios for smoke and the definitional nuance about wind being a mover rather than simply moving air.

Core Idea
Wind is air agitated by the rebound of smoke against celestial spheres.

This passage dives into the natural philosophy of wind. The author explains how smoke, when blocked by the cold sphere or rebounding from the fire sphere, sets the air into oscillatory motion. It also addresses a subtle definitional point from Aristotle and Imam al-Razi about whether wind is essentially a mover or moving air.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text and its literal translation silently. Notice the two scenarios for smoke and the analogy of the stick and arrows.
The passage is dense with technical terms like 'al-kura az-zamhaririyya' (frozen sphere).
2
Identify the two scenarios
The smoke either breaks upon reaching the frozen sphere and falls back, or it ascends and collides with the fire sphere. In both cases, air becomes wavy and agitated, producing wind.
3
Understand the analogy
The author compares the rebound of smoke from the fire sphere to arrows repelled by a circular stick. This analogy helps visualize the scattering of smoke in different directions.
4
Reflect on the definition of wind
Aristotle defines wind as a mover that is air, not simply moving air. Imam al-Razi clarifies that air is the matter and place of wind, not its genus. Consider why this distinction matters.
This subtle point prevents understanding wind as mere moving air—it has a specific cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً نقول : ( فالدخان قد ينكسر الوصول إلى الكرة الزمهريرية ( فيثقل ( فيرجع بطبعها ) إلى الأرض ( أو ) لا ينكسر وحينئذ ( يصعد ويصادم كرة النار لا ( الفلك) على ما وقع في النسخ لأن نفوذه في النار البسيطة العالية على الإحالة إلى طبيعتها غير معقول بحسب الظاهر (فيرجع) ويرتد بمصادمته كرة النار المتحركة بحركة الفلك رجوعاً على جهات مختلفة كما يرد بعصا دائرة سهام على جهات شتى . ( وعلى التقديرين فيتموج الهواء ) ويضطرب ( وهو الريح) . قيل : قد وقع في كلام أرسطو أن الريح يحد بأنه متحرك وهو هواء لا بأنه هواء متحرك . قال الإمام الرازي والذي يمكن أن يقال فيه : إن الهواء مادة الريح وموضعها فلا يجوز وضعها موضع الجنس
And also we say: the smoke may be broken in its reaching the frozen sphere (al-kura az-zamharīriyya), so it becomes heavy, so it returns by its nature to the earth; or it does not break, and then it ascends and collides with the sphere of fire, not the sphere, according to what occurred in the copies, because its penetration into the simple high fire by conversion to its nature is inconceivable apparently, so it returns and rebounds by its collision with the sphere of fire moving with the motion of the sphere, returning in different directions, just as a circular stick repels arrows in various directions. And in both cases, the air becomes wavy and agitated, and that is wind. It was said: in the speech of Aristotle, wind is defined as a mover which is air, not as moving air. Imam ar-Razi said: what can be said is that air is the matter of wind and its place, so it is not permissible to put it in the place of genus.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage explains wind generation. When smoke is blocked by the cold sphere (zamharir), it becomes heavy and falls back; otherwise it rises and strikes the fire sphere, rebounding like arrows from a spinning stick. In both cases, air oscillates, producing wind. The author then cites Aristotle's definition of wind as 'a mover which is air' (not 'moving air'), and Imam Razi comments that air is the matter and place of wind, not its genus.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wind is simply air that is moving.
Correction: Wind is not just any moving air; it is air that moves due to a specific cause (smoke rebounding from celestial spheres). The Aristotelian definition emphasizes that wind is a mover that is air, not air that happens to be moving.

Checkpoint

A) It turns into flame
B) It rebounds and causes air to oscillate
C) It dissolves

Key Takeaway
Wind is produced when air is agitated by the reflection or rebound of smoke from the celestial spheres, and it is defined as a mover that is air, not merely moving air.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel the wind, do you ever pause to consider the intricate chain of causes—smoke, spheres, collision—that produced it, or do you take it for granted? How might this awareness transform your sense of wonder at Allah's creation?
?
This lesson presents a precise physical explanation for something ordinary. Are you someone who seeks such explanations for the world around you, or do you prefer to remain content with surface-level understanding? What would it take for you to investigate the 'how' behind other everyday phenomena?
?
If you believe all things occur by Allah's decree, does knowing the secondary causes (like smoke rebound) strengthen your trust in His wisdom or diminish it? How can you cultivate both a belief in divine causation and an appreciation for the natural laws He set in place?

The Mechanics of Wind and Whirlwinds
Explains wind generation and wind-rain relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the natural mechanisms of wind generation, the formation of whirlwinds and tornadoes, and the relationship between wind and rain.

Core Idea
Wind is not random; it follows physical laws of push and pull.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif delves into the physical causes of winds, explaining how smoke generates most winds from above, how rarefied air pushes neighboring air to create waves, and how opposing winds compress earthly parts into whirlwinds and tornadoes. It also examines the paradoxical relationship between wind and rain—both impeding and aiding each other.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the author's descriptions of physical processes become clear.
2
Identify the two wind generation methods
Notice that the passage describes two ways wind arises: from smoke rising above, and from air rarefaction causing a chain of pushes. Identify each.
3
Understand whirlwind formation
Focus on how winds from different directions compress earthly parts, causing them to twist and form whirlwinds (zawabi') and tornadoes (i'sar).
4
Analyze wind-rain dynamics
The passage explains both impediment (wind disperses clouds, rain makes smoke heavy) and cooperation (rain moistens earth for more smoke, wind gathers clouds). Understand both sides.
5
Reflect on natural causality
Consider how this passage rejects supernatural explanations for whirlwinds, grounding them in physical interactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولذلك ) الذي ذكرناه من حال الدخان في توليد الريح ) كان أكثر مبادئ الزياح فوقانية كما تشهد به التجربة والريح كما يحدث بهذا الطريق) في الأغلب ( فقد يحدث ) أيضاً ( بأن يتخلخل الهواء فيندفع) مکانه بواسطة عظم مقداره ( فيدافع ما يجاوره فيطاوعه) ويدافع ذلك المجاور أيضاً مجاوره فيتموج الهواء، ( وتضعف) تلك ( المدافعة ) شيئاً فشيئاً ( إلى غاية ما فيقف وقد يحدث رياح مختلفة الجهة دفعة فتدافع تلك الرياح ( الأجزاء الأرضية فتنضغط الأجزاء الأرضية ( بينها مرتفعة كأنها تلتوي على نفسها، وهي الزوابع جمع زوبعة وهي الريح المستديرة على نفسها ) والإعصار) المسمى في الفارسية بکرد باد، هذا وقد قيل : بين الريح والمطر تمانع وتعاون أما التمانع فلأن الريح في الأكثر تلطف مادة السحاب بحرارتها وتفرقها بتحريكها والمطر يبل الأدخنة ويصل بعضها ببعض فيثقل حينئذ ولا يتمكن من الصعود فكل سنة يكثر فيها المطر تقل فيها الريح، وبالعكس، وأما التعاون فلان المطر يبل الأرض فيعيدها لأن يصعد منها دخان إذ الرطوبة تعين على تحلل اليابس وتصعده والريح تجمع السحاب وتهرب برودة السحاب إلى باطنه فيشتد البرد المكثف
And therefore, what we have mentioned of the condition of smoke in generating wind, most origins of winds are from above, as experience testifies. And wind, as it occurs in this way mostly, may also occur by the air becoming rarefied so it is pushed from its place by virtue of the magnitude of its quantity, so it pushes what is adjacent to it, and that neighbor also pushes its neighbor, so the air becomes wavy, and that pushing weakens little by little until it stops. And winds of different directions may occur at once, so those winds press the earthly parts, so the earthly parts are compressed between them, rising as if they twist upon themselves, and they are the 'zawābi' (pl. of zūba'a), which is the wind rotating upon itself, and the 'i'ṣār' called in Persian 'kurdbād'. And it has been said: between wind and rain there is mutual impediment and cooperation. As for the impediment, because the wind mostly softens the substance of the cloud with its heat and disperses it by its movement, and the rain moistens the smokes and connects some to others, so they become heavy then and cannot ascend, so every year in which rain is abundant, wind is scarce, and vice versa. As for cooperation, because rain moistens the earth, making it able to emit smoke, since moisture helps the dissolution of dry things and their ascent, and the wind gathers the cloud and drives the cold of the cloud to its interior, so the thickening cold becomes intense.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage first explains the mechanics of wind: most winds originate from above due to smoke. Wind also occurs when air rarefies and pushes neighboring air successively until it stops. When winds from different directions meet, they compress the earth into rotating columns called zūba'a (whirlwind) and i'ṣār (tornado). Then the author discusses the relationship between wind and rain: there is mutual impediment (wind disperses clouds, rain makes smoke heavy) and cooperation (rain moistens earth to produce more smoke, wind gathers clouds and intensifies cold).

إعصار

مدافعة

تعاون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Whirlwinds and tornadoes are supernatural phenomena.
Correction: The passage explains that whirlwinds and tornadoes arise from natural physical causes: winds compressing earthly parts, causing them to twist and rotate.

Checkpoint

A) The descent of fire
B) The compression of earthly parts by opposing winds
C) The rise of dry vapor
D) The movement of planets

Key Takeaway
Winds, whirlwinds, and rain are interconnected physical phenomena obeying natural laws.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Four Cardinal Winds and Their Arabic Names
Identifies the four cardinal winds and their Arabic names, including the oblique wind 'nakbā'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Arabic classification of winds into four cardinal directions and the term for oblique winds.

Core Idea
Winds are named by their direction in Arabic: East, West, North, South — and between them is 'Nakbā'.

This passage explains how the Arabs categorized winds based on the four cardinal directions. Although winds are countless, scholars identified four principal origins: east, west, north, and south. The Arabic names for these winds and the concept of the oblique wind 'nakbā' are introduced.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage closely
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice how the author first states that winds are not limited to a number, then introduces the four principal directions and their Arabic wind names.
Pay attention to the marginal note about 'nakbā'.
2
Identify the four cardinal wind names
Find the four Arabic terms for the winds from east, west, north, and south: قبول, دبور, شمال, جنوب. Note that these are the names given by the Arabs.
3
Understand 'Nakbā'
Learn that winds blowing between the cardinal directions are called 'nakbā'. The passage also mentions that there are four such oblique winds, each with a specific name.
The word 'nakbā' is on the measure of 'ṣaḥrā'.
4
Reflect on the classification
Think about why this classification might be useful. The author emphasizes that winds are not truly limited to these directions, but these are the principal ones used for naming.
Avoid the misconception that there are only four winds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما مهاب الرياح فغير منحصرة حقيقة في عدد إلا أنهم جعلوا أصولها أربعة هي نقط المشرق والمغرب والشمال والجنوب، والعرب تسمي الرياح التي تهب منها بالقبول والدبور والشمال والجنوب، وتسمي التي تهب مما بينها نكباء. قوله: نكباء على وزن صحراء، والنكباء أيضاً أربع من الرياح بحيث يهب كل واحد منها بين موضعي جنوب، اثنين من تلك الأربعة المذكورة أولاً ولكل واحد من هذه الأربعة أيضاً اسم مخصوص على حده كما ذكر في الصحاح
And as for the directions (mahābb) of the winds, they are not truly limited to a number, except that they made their origins four: the points of the east, the west, the north, and the south. The Arabs call the winds that blow from them 'al-qabūl', 'al-dabūr', 'al-shamāl', and 'al-janūb', and they call the winds that blow from between them 'nakbā'. He said: 'Nakbā' is on the measure of 'Ṣaḥrā', and the 'nakbā' are also four winds, each one blowing between two locations of south, two of those four mentioned first, and each of these four also has a specific name of its own as mentioned in al-Ṣiḥāḥ.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage discusses the cardinal winds. The author states that winds are not exhaustively countable, but scholars have set four principal directions: east (mashriq), west (maghrib), north (shamāl), south (janūb). The Arabs name these winds as al-qabūl (east wind), al-dabūr (west wind), al-shamāl (north wind), and al-janūb (south wind). Winds blowing between these are called nakbā (oblique wind). The marginal note adds that nakbā is on the pattern of ṣaḥrā', and there are four nakbā winds each blowing between two of the cardinal points, each with a specific name in the Arabic lexicon al-Ṣiḥāḥ.

Key Terms

شمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There are only four winds, each corresponding to a cardinal direction.
Correction: The passage states that winds are not limited to a number; the four directions are just origins for naming. Additionally, there are oblique winds called nakbā that blow between these cardinal points.

Checkpoint

B) قبول
C) زوابع
D) إعصار

Key Takeaway
The Arabs classified winds into four cardinal directions (east, west, north, south) with specific names, and the oblique winds between them are called 'nakbā'.

Formation of Halos around the Moon
Explains the formation of a halo around the moon via reflection from fine moisture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the optical explanation for the formation of a halo around the moon as presented in classical Islamic natural philosophy.

Core Idea
A halo is not a physical ring but a reflection from tiny polished particles.

This passage explains a natural phenomenon often seen in the sky: a luminous circle around the moon. The author, a classical scholar, attributes it to fine, polished moisture particles arranged in a circle. These particles act like tiny mirrors, reflecting light and creating the appearance of a glowing ring. The explanation is a good example of how medieval Muslim thinkers combined observation with rational analysis of natural causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic source passage aloud, then read the literal English translation. Pay attention to the technical terms.
The passage includes marginal notes on 'takhṭīṭ' and 'murāṣṣa'.
2
Identify the key components
Notice the three main elements: the moist polished parts, the thin cloud, and the circular arrangement. Understand how they interact.
The thin cloud does not obstruct the view.
3
Understand the reflection process
The light from the eye is reflected from the polished parts to the moon. Because the parts are very small, they convey only light and color, not shape.
This is compared to a small mirror.
4
Contrast with a common misconception
Many people think the halo is a real ring. This passage shows it's an optical effect. Discuss why this misunderstanding occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً نقول : ( فقد يحدث في الجو أجزاء ) رطبة ( رشية صقيلة كدائرة تحيط ) تلك الأجزاء ( بغيم رقيق ) لطيف ( لا يحجب ما وراءه ) عن الأبصار ( فينعكس منها أي من تلك الأجزاء الواقعة على ذلك الوضع (ضوء البصر لصقالتها إلى القمر فيرى في تلك الأجزاء (ضوء دون شكله، فإن الصقيل الذي ينعكس منه شعاع البصر ( إذا صغر جداً ) بحيث لا ينقسم في الحس ( أدى الضوء واللون دون الشكل والتخطيط كما في المرآة الصغيرة)، وتلك الأجزاء الرشية مرايا صغار متراصة على هيئة الدائرة ) فيرى جميع تلك الدائرة كأنها منورة. قوله: والتخطيط بحسب المقدار والوضع بين أجزاء المرئي. وقوله : متراصة بتشديد الصاد المهملة. يقال : تراص القوم في الصف أي تلاصقوا
And also we say: there may occur in the air moist, spray-like, polished parts like a circle surrounding those parts a thin, fine cloud that does not hide what is behind it from sight. Then the light of the eye is reflected from them, i.e., from those parts placed in that position, due to their polish, to the moon, so a light is seen in those parts without its shape. For the polished thing from which the ray of the eye is reflected, if it is very small so that it is not divisible in sensation, it conveys light and color without shape and outlining, as in a small mirror. And those spray-like parts are small mirrors stacked in the form of a circle, so the whole circle is seen as if it is illuminated. He said: and 'takhṭīṭ' is according to the quantity and position among the parts of the visible object. And he said: 'murāṣṣa' with a shaddah on the ṣād. It is said: 'tarāṣṣa al-qawm fī al-ṣaff' meaning they clung together.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage explains the formation of a halo around the moon. Fine, polished mist particles arranged in a circle reflect light to the moon, making the circle appear luminous. The reflection is analogous to a small mirror: if the reflective surface is too small to be distinguished, it only conveys light and color, not shape or detail. The marginal notes clarify the term 'takhṭīṭ' (outlining) and 'murāṣṣa' (stacked, from 'tarāṣṣa' meaning to cling together in a row).

ضوء البصر
انعكاس
مرآة صغيرة
متراصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The halo is a physical ring of light around the moon.
Correction: The halo is an optical effect caused by reflection of light from tiny polished moisture particles arranged in a circle.

Checkpoint

A) Because the particles are too large
B) Because the particles are very small and arranged in a circle
C) Because the moon is very bright
D) Because the cloud is thick

Key Takeaway
The halo around the moon is explained by classical physics as the reflection of light from tiny polished moisture particles arranged in a circle, which appear luminous due to their size and arrangement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Perpetual Existence of Generic Forms and the Temporality of Specific Forms
Explains that while the genus of forms is perpetual, specific forms can be temporally originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the perpetual existence of the genus of forms is compatible with the temporal origination of specific forms.

Core Idea
The genus of forms is eternal, but specific forms may be created in time.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif addresses a subtle point: while the generic aspect of forms is continuously existent through successive species, individual specific forms—including those of compound bodies—can originate temporally. The commentator clarifies this with the example of the human species and addresses a potential objection regarding eternal species.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the author's line of reasoning without interpretation.
Notice the dense argumentative style typical of kalam commentaries.
2
Identify the main claim about forms
The passage begins by asserting that bodies are never devoid of specific forms; one form must always be present. These forms share a genus but differ in specific quiddity, so the genus persists through species.
3
Understand the argument about individualizing forms
The commentator then argues that individualizing forms (الصور المشخصة) cannot be eternal in type or genus, as that would lead to infinite regress. They are temporally originated.
This is a key logical point.
4
Examine the example and objection
Using the human species as an example, the passage shows that there is no impossibility in a specific form being originated. It then addresses the objection that the human species was considered eternal by some, offering a resolution involving the sphere of fire.
The objection and response clarify the intended meaning.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Reflect on how this passage reconciles eternal genus with temporal species, and how it guards against the misconception that specific forms cannot be originated.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلوها عن صورها النوعية بأسرها بل لا بد أن يكون معها واحدة منها لكن هذه الصور متشاركة في جنسها دون ماهيتها النوعية فيكون جنسها مستمر الوجود بتعاقب أنواعه (نعم) الصور المشخصة فيهما أي في الصورة الجسمية والنوعية ( والأعراض المختصة ( المعينة ( محدثة ولا امتناع في حدوث بعض الصور النوعية ) العنصرية كأن يكون مثلاً نوع الإنسان حادثاً غير مستمر الوجود تتعاقب أفراده الشخصية، إذ يجوز حصوله من عنصر آخر بطريق الكون والفساد ولا امتناع أيضاً عندهم في استمراره كذلك ولا في استمرار أنواع المركبات في ضمن أفرادها المتعاقبة بلا نهاية • قوله : (نعم الصور المشخصة فيهما هذا من تتمة قوله : أنهم قالوا إلخ ولا يذهب عليك ) أن الصورة المشخصة لا يتصور أن يكون قديمة بنوعها أو بجنسها وإلا لكانت محتاجة إلى صورة أخرى مشخصة أو منوعة وهلم جراً فيلزم التسلسل وإنما المتصور هو أن يكون قديمة بالعرض العام ولا عبرة بذلك . قوله : ( كأن يكون نوع الإنسان حادثاً مع أن نوع الإنسان كان قديماً عندهم وأن امتزاجه كانت مركبة من العناصر الأربعة فيلزم أن يكون نوعاً النار في ضمن تلك الأفراد قديماً عندهم، هذا ولعلهم أرادوا بحدوثه حدوث كرة النار بحركات الأفلاك بطريق الكون والفساد وأن هذه الكرة مخالفة بالنوع للأجزاء النارية التي في المركبات أو ردوا بقولهم : وبصروها النوعية بجنسها أن المقطوع عندهم هو أن يكون الصور النوعية للعنصريات قديمة بجنسها لا أن يكون قديمة بأنواعها على ما يشعر به قول (المصنف) ولا امتناع في حدوثه إلخ. وكذا قول الشارح: ولا امتناع أيضاً عندهم .
They are free from their specific forms entirely, but there must be one of them with them. Yet these forms share in their genus without their specific quiddities, so their genus is continuous in existence through the succession of its types. (Yes) the individualizing forms in them, i.e., in the corporeal and specific forms, and the specific accidents are temporally originated, and there is no impossibility in the origination of some specific forms, e.g., if the human species is temporally originated, not continuous in existence, its personal individuals succeed one another; for it is possible for it to come from another element by way of generation and corruption. And there is also no impossibility according to them in its continuity likewise, nor in the continuity of types of compounds within their successive individuals without end. He said: 'Yes, the individualizing forms in them' — this is a completion of his statement: 'They said etc.' And it is not hidden from you that the individualizing form cannot be conceived to be eternal in its type or its genus, otherwise it would need another individualizing or specifying form, and so on, leading to infinite regress. Rather, what is conceivable is that it is eternal in a general sense, and no attention is paid to that. He said: 'Such as if the human species is temporally originated' — even though the human species was eternal according to them, and its mixture was composed of the four elements, so it would be necessary that the type of fire within those individuals be eternal according to them. This, and perhaps they meant by its origination the origination of the sphere of fire through the motions of the spheres by way of generation and corruption, and that this sphere differs in type from the fiery parts in compounds, or they rejected by their statement: 'and they made it specific by its genus' that what is certain for them is that the specific forms of elementary things are eternal in their genus, not that they are eternal in their types, as indicated by the statement of the author: 'And there is no impossibility in its origination, etc.' And likewise the statement of the commentator: 'And there is no impossibility also according to them.'
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the continuity of forms in bodies. It begins by stating that bodies are not free from all specific forms; instead, one form must be present. These forms share in genus but differ in specific quiddity, so the genus persists through successive species. The commentator then addresses the possibility of individualizing forms (الصور المشخصة) being eternal: they cannot be eternal in type or genus, as that would cause an infinite regress; rather, they can only be considered eternal in a general sense. Then, using the example of the human species, it argues that there is no impossibility in some specific forms being temporally originated, even if the genus is perpetual. The objection that human species was eternal according to them is answered by suggesting that they meant the origination of the sphere of fire, which differs from elemental fire, or that specific forms are eternal in genus only, not in species.

Key Terms
صور نوعية
جنس
صور مشخصة
حدوث
كون وفساد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if the genus of forms is eternal, then specific forms cannot be temporally originated.
Correction: The passage shows that specific forms can be originated in time, as the genus endures through successive species; for example, the human species could be temporally originated.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the status of specific forms (الصور النوعية) in relation to their genus?
A) They are as eternal as the genus.
B) They can be temporally originated while the genus is eternal.
C) They are eternal in type but not in genus.
D) They are impossible to originate.

Key Takeaway
The genus of forms is perpetual, but specific forms can originate in time, as shown by the example of the human species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that what seems like an eternal truth (like the genus of forms) can coexist with temporal particulars (like individual forms). Where in your own life have you falsely assumed that something personal and temporary is actually eternal and unchangeable? How does that assumption affect your actions today?
?
The passage argues that individualizing forms cannot be eternal without leading to infinite regress. Consider your own identity: do you think your 'individual form' (your essence) is eternal or temporally originated? How would your daily choices change if you truly believed it was the latter?
?
If the genus of forms is eternal but individual forms come and go, what does that imply about your attachment to temporary things? Is there something you hold onto as permanent that this lesson tells you is actually transient? What would it cost you to let go of that illusion?

The Possibility of Another World: Theologians vs Philosophers
Theologians permit another world; philosophers' first argument and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the theologians affirm another world is possible and how they answer the philosophers' first argument about direction.

Core Idea
The boundary of this world does not define all possible directions.

In this lesson, we examine the eighth goal of the text: whether another world similar to this one can exist. The theologians argue yes, citing God's power and the principle of similarity. The philosophers object, claiming that any other world would be confined by this world's boundary. The author presents their first argument and offers a nuanced response that leaves the possibility open.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overall sense of the discussion. Notice the structure: theologians' position, philosophers' argument, and the author's answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the theologians' position: they permit another world based on similarity and the Quranic verse. Then identify the philosophers' position: they deny, giving three reasons. Only the first reason is elaborated here.
The Quranic verse is Surah Ya-Sin 81.
3
Understand the argument and answer
Focus on the philosophers' first argument: an external world would be in a direction already determined by this world's boundary, causing a contradiction. Then see the author's answer: the boundary only determines some directions (like up and down), not all. Other boundaries can define other directions.
This is the key insight: the boundary does not exhaust all possible directions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الثامن: جواز وجود عالم آخر ] جوز المتكلمون وجود عالم آخر مماثل لهذا العالم لأن الأمور المتماثلة تتشارك في الأحكام وإليه الإشارة في الكلام المجيد أو ليس الذي خلق السماوات والأرض بقادر على أن يخلق مثلهم ﴾ [يس : ۸۱] ، وقال الحكماء : لا عالم غير هذا العالم أعني ما يحيط به سطح محدد الجهات لثلاثة أوجه : الأول : لو وجد خارجه عالم آخر لكان في جانب من المحدود) كان ( المحدد في جهة منه، فتكون الجهة قد تحددت قبله ليتصور وقوعه فيها ( لا) (به كما هو الواقع هذا خلف . والجواب أن الذي ثبت بالبرهان تحدد حتى العلو والسفل بالمحدد كما مر . ( وأما تحدد جميع الجهات به فلا ولم لا يجوز أن يكون هاهنا جهات غير هاتين الجهتين تتحدد لا بهذا المحدد ) بل بمحدد آخر فيجوز وقوع هذا في جهة منها ( فإن حصر الجهات ) المتحددة ( في هاتين لم يقم عليه دليل
[The eighth goal: the possibility of another world] The theologians (mutakallimūn) allowed the existence of another world similar to this world, because similar things share in judgments. There is an indication to this in the Noble Speech: "Is not He who created the heavens and the earth able to create the like of them?" (Quran 36:81). The philosophers (ḥukamā’) said: There is no world other than this world — meaning what is surrounded by a surface determining directions — for three reasons. The first: if another world existed outside it, it would be in a side of the bounded (world) — but the boundary already determines a direction for it, so the direction would have been determined before it in order for its position in it to be conceivable — not (determined) by it as is actually the case, which is a contradiction. The answer: what is established by proof is that even up and down are determined by the boundary, as passed. As for the determination of all directions by it — no. And why should it not be possible that here there are directions other than these two directions, determined not by this boundary but by another boundary? Thus it is possible for this (world) to occur in a direction of them. For the confinement of (determined) directions to these two has no proof.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage begins the eighth goal (al-maqṣad al-thāmin) on the possibility of another world. The theologians (mutakallimūn) affirm its possibility based on the principle that similar things share judgments, and they cite the Quranic verse about God's power to create similar to the heavens and earth. The philosophers (ḥukamā’) deny it, giving three arguments. Only the first is fully presented here: if another world existed outside this one, it would be in a certain direction relative to the boundary of this world. But the direction would then be determined by the boundary before the existence of the new world, which is impossible because the boundary itself is supposed to determine directions. The author's answer is that the boundary only determines some directions (such as up and down) but not all directions. There could be other directions determined by other boundaries, and this world could be located in one of those directions. So the argument does not prove that another world is impossible.

Key Terms
عالم آخر
متماثل
أحكام
محدد الجهات
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The boundary of this world determines all possible directions.
Correction: The boundary only determines some directions, like up and down, but leaves room for other directional systems defined by other boundaries.

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' first argument against another world, and how is it answered?
A) Another world would be in a direction already determined by this world's boundary, causing contradiction; answered by denying that all directions are determined by this boundary.
B) Another world would require a void beyond; answered by allowing a filler.
C) Another world would make the universe infinite; answered by saying infinity is impossible.
D) Another world would contradict God's power; answered by citing a Quranic verse.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument that another world is impossible fails because the boundary of this world only defines some directions, not all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine the greatness of Allah's creation, do you limit it to what you can see or conceive? How does this lesson challenge your assumption that 'everything' is already known?
?
The philosophers' argument relied on a logically neat boundary that determines all directions. In your own life, have you ever assumed that your current understanding or situation defines the entire scope of what is possible? What would it take for you to admit that there may be 'other directions' beyond your current vision?
?
If you truly believed that Allah could create another world similar to this one, how would that affect your attachment to this world and your hope in the Hereafter? Are you living as if this world is the only reality?

Avoiding the Void Between Worlds
Second argument and answer on void between worlds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second philosophical argument against multiple worlds (the void argument) and the author's response that the void can be avoided by embedding worlds in a larger sphere.

Core Idea
Two worlds need not create a void if they are embedded in a larger sphere.

This lesson presents the second philosophical argument against the existence of multiple worlds: that there would be an impossible void between them. The author offers a clever answer: the space can be filled by a larger sphere containing both worlds. By studying this passage, you will see how kalam scholars used physical analogies to defend their theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Identify why the philosophers think two worlds necessitate a void. The argument considers both spherical and non-spherical shapes.
Notice that the author concedes the impossibility of void as a premise.
2
Understand the answer
After conceding void is impossible, the author denies that a void must exist. See how he proposes a filler (mālī’) to occupy the space.
3
Examine the analogy
The author uses the example of Mars being three times larger than the sun's sphere to show that a larger sphere could contain many smaller spheres.
This is based on Ptolemaic astronomy, not modern science.
4
Reflect on the implications
The impossibility of void does not prevent multiple worlds, as long as they are embedded in a larger body.
This shows how kalam scholars harmonized philosophy with theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لو وجد عالم آخر لكان بينهما خلاء سواء كانا ( معاً ( كرتين (أولا) وذلك لأن هذا العالم كروي فإن كان الآخر كروياً أيضاً لم يتصور الملاقاة بينهما إلا بنقطة فلا بد أن يقع بينهما خلاء سواء تلاقيا أولا، وإن لم يكن كروياً وقع الخلاء أيضاً لأن ملاقاة الكرة لما ليس بكرة لا تكون إلا مع فرجة . (والجواب ) بعد تسليم امتناع الخلاء أن نقول : ( لا نسلم ذلك لجواز أن يملاهما أي يملأ ما بينهما ( ماليء ولو أردنا ذكر مستند للمنع تبرعاً، قلنا: قد يكونان ) أي العالمان (تدويرين) مركوزين (في ثخن (كرة عظيمة يساوي ثخنها قطريهما أو يزيد عليهما ( وربما تتضمن تلك الكرة (ألوفاً) من الكرات كل واحدة منها ( أعظم من المحدد بما فيها من الأفلاك والعناصر ) ولا استبعاد ) في ذلك ( فإنهم قالوا : تدوير المريخ أعظم من ممثل الشمس بما فيها من الأفلاك الثلاثة والعناصر الأربعة ثلاث مرات ) وإذا جاز ذلك فلم لا يجوز فيما هو أعظم منه ؟ ومن أين لكم أنه
The second: if another world existed, there would be a void (khalā’) between them, whether both are spheres or not. This is because this world is spherical. If the other is also spherical, contact between them can only be at a point, so there must inevitably be a void between them whether they touch or not. If it is not spherical, void occurs as well, because the contact of a sphere with what is not a sphere can only occur with a gap. The answer, after conceding the impossibility of void: we do not concede that, for it is possible that a filler (mālī’) fills what is between them. If we wanted to mention a basis for the denial as a favor, we say: the two worlds could be two spheres embedded in the thickness (thikhn) of a great sphere whose thickness equals or exceeds their diameters. And perhaps that great sphere contains thousands of spheres, each one greater than the boundary (al-muḥaddad) including what is in it of spheres and elements. There is no impossibility in that, for they said: the sphere of Mars is three times greater than the representative of the sun including its three spheres and four elements. If that is possible, why is it not possible for what is greater than it? And from where do you have that...
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This is the second philosophical argument and answer. The philosophers argue that if two worlds exist, there would be a void between them, regardless of their shapes. They assume void is impossible. The author, after conceding the impossibility of void (as a premise), denies that a separate void must exist. Instead, the space between the two worlds could be filled by a filler (mālī’), perhaps a larger sphere in which both worlds are embedded. This larger sphere could be vast, containing many worlds. The author gives an analogy: the sphere of Mars is larger than the sphere of the sun (according to Ptolemaic astronomy), so such a hierarchy is plausible. Thus the impossibility of void does not prevent the existence of multiple worlds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If void is impossible, then any two separate bodies must be in contact or there must be a continuous medium.
Correction: The author suggests that two worlds could be embedded in a larger spherical body that fills the space between them, thus avoiding void without requiring direct contact or a continuous medium.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the second philosophical argument that two worlds would require a void between them?
A) He admits void is necessary but argues it is not impossible.
B) He denies that void exists and instead posits that the space between could be filled by a larger sphere containing both worlds.
C) He argues that the two worlds can touch at multiple points, eliminating void.
D) He says the argument is irrelevant because only one world exists.

Key Takeaway
The existence of multiple worlds does not necessarily imply a void because the space between them could be filled by a larger celestial body that contains them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that God's actions are limited by your understanding of what is possible? How does this argument about multiple worlds challenge that assumption? This week, identify one area where you have been confining God's creative power to your own logic, and repent from that limitation.
?
The philosophers claimed void must exist between worlds, but the author showed that God's creation can fill all spaces. Are there 'voids' in your spiritual life—gaps in your trust, worship, or hope—that you think cannot be filled? How does the concept of al-mālī’ (the filler) inspire you to trust that God can fill every gap? Write a specific prayer asking Him to fill that void today.

Objection and Response on Celestial Faculties and Internal Senses
Objection and response on celestial faculties and internal senses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians use denial of premises to challenge arguments about the purpose of celestial faculties and internal senses.

Core Idea
The purpose of faculties is not limited to preservation; they may exist for perfection or other benefits.

This passage presents an objection to the claim that celestial faculties exist solely for repelling harm and preserving form. The objector denies the premises, allowing for alternative purposes. The argument then extends to internal senses, where the objector similarly denies that their benefit is confined to sensory preservation. This lesson unpacks these logical moves and shows how objections are built on the denial of necessary connection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection on celestial faculties
Read the first part of the passage where the objector denies that the purpose of repelling harm is solely preservation. Notice how each premise is denied.
Focus on the denial of 'preservation' as the only purpose.
2
Analyze the argument on internal senses
Read the second part where Ibn Sina's argument is summarized: internal senses depend on external senses. Identify the claimed dependency and the counterexample.
The dependency argument: if external senses don't exist, internal senses must exist.
3
Understand the response
The response denies the exclusivity of benefit. Reflect on why this denial is effective.
The response also questions the assumption that nothing idle exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودفع الضر، المقصود بهما حفظ الصورة عن الفساد وصورها الجسمية والنوعية ( لا تقبل ذلك ) لامتناع الخرق والالتئام والكون والفساد عليها ( والمقدمات ) المذكورة كلها ممنوعة ( إذ لا نسلم أن هذا القوى إنما خلقت لما ذكر، فإنه يجوز أن يكون خلقها لكونها كمالاً للجسم ولا نسلم أيضاً انحصار النفع والدفع في حفظ الصورة عن الفساد، ولئن سلم فلا نسلم أن صورة الفلك لا تقبل الفساد وما استدل به عليه مدخول، وفي الملخص أن لكلام ابن سينا اضطرب في الحواس الباطنة فحيث نفاها استدل عليه بأنها متعلقة بالحواس الظاهرة، لأن التخيل لحفظ صور المحسوسات، والتوهم لدرك أحوالها الجزئية، والتفكر للتصرف فيها، فإذا لم يوجد الأصل وجب يوجد التبع، ويرد على هذا الاستدلال أنا لا نسلم انحصار فائدتها في حفظ المحسوسات وأحوالها الجزئية والتصرف فيها إذ يجوز أن يكون فيها فوائد أخرى وإن سلم فلا نسلم أنه لا معطل في الوجود .
And repelling harm, the intended [purpose] of which is preservation of the form from corruption and its bodily and specific forms (do not accept that) due to the impossibility of tearing and mending and generation and corruption upon them. And the (premises) mentioned are all denied, (for we do not accept that these faculties were created only for what was mentioned, for it is possible that they were created as a perfection for the body, and we also do not accept that benefit and repelling are confined to preserving the form from corruption. And if it is granted, we do not accept that the form of the celestial sphere does not accept corruption, and what was adduced for it is flawed. And in al-Mulakhkhas, the speech of Ibn Sina is disturbed regarding the internal senses, for when he denied them, he argued that they are connected to the external senses, because estimation (takhayyul) is for preserving sensory forms, imagination (tawahhum) for apprehending their particular states, and reflection (tafakkur) for disposing of them. So if the original [external sense] does not exist, the dependent [internal sense] must exist. In response to this argument, we do not accept that their benefit is confined to preserving the sensory forms and their particular states and disposing of them, for it is possible that they have other benefits. And if it is granted, we do not accept that there is no idle thing in existence.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response concerning the celestial faculties. It begins by stating that the purpose of the faculties of repelling harm is to preserve the form from corruption. The objector denies this by saying that these faculties might exist as perfections of the body, not solely for preservation. He further denies that the celestial sphere's form is incorruptible. Then the passage mentions a summary from al-Mulakhkhas: Ibn Sina's statement about the internal senses is confused, because when he denies them, he argues that they are dependent on external senses—estimation preserves sensory forms, imagination apprehends particular states, reflection disposes them—so if the origin (external sense) does not exist, the dependent (internal sense) must exist. The response to this argument is that we do not accept that their benefit is confined to that; they may have other benefits, and even if granted, we do not accept that there is nothing idle in existence.

Correction: The objector shows it is possible they exist for other purposes, such as being a perfection of the body, or they may have multiple benefits.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ibn Sina argued that internal senses must exist because they are dependent on external senses. The objector replies that the connection is not necessary. In your own life, do you assume that certain things must follow from others based on your limited understanding? What if God's wisdom allows for possibilities you have not considered? How willing are you to accept that your reasoning might be incomplete?

Definition of the Soul's Immateriality and Scholarly Positions
Definition of human soul as immaterial and listing of proponents and opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the human soul as an immaterial substance and the differing views among Muslim scholars on its nature.

Core Idea
The soul is not a body nor a corporeal power; it is a non-spatial entity that manages the body.

The passage begins by defining the human soul as immaterial, contrasting with corporeal powers and bodies. It then mentions that this is the view of the philosophers, al-Ghazali, al-Raghib, and many Sufis, while the majority of Muslim theologians reject immaterial beings. This sets the stage for the first proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic definition carefully, noting what the soul is and is not.
Pay attention to the phrase 'talluq al-tadbir wa al-tasarruf'.
2
Identify the two camps
Identify the proponents (philosophers, Ghazali, Raghib, Sufis) and opponents (majority).
3
Note the transition to the proof
Observe how the passage ends by hinting at the coming proof.
The next part will present the first argument.

( في أن النفوس الإنسانية مجردة) أي (ليست) قوة (جسمانية) حالة في المادة ) ولا جسماً) بل هي لامكانية لا تقبل إشارة حسية ( وإنما تعلقها بالبدن تعلق التدبير والتصرف من غير أن تكون داخلة فيه بالجزئية أو الحلول (هذا مذهب الفلاسفة) المشهورين من المتقدمين والمتأخرين ( ووافقهم على ذلك من المسلمين الغزالي والراغب ) وجمع من الصوفية المكاشفين ( وخالفهم فيه الجمهور بناء على ما مر من نفي المجردات على الإطلاق ) عقولاً كانت أو نفوساً (احتجوا) أي المثبتون لتجريدها (بوجوه ) خمسة : الأول : أنها تعقل البسيط الذي لا تكون مجردة أما الأول فلانها تعقل حقيقة ما من الحقائق أي معنى المعاني ( فإن كانت تلك الحقيقة بسيطة فذاك ) أي ثبت المطلوب أعني تعقلها للبسيط ( وإلا كانت تلك الحقيقة ( مركبة من البسائط ) بالفعل لأن الكثرة متناهية كانت أو غير متناهية يجب فيها الواحد بالفعل لأنه مبدؤها ( وتعقل الكل بعد تعقل أجزائه ) بالضرورة لا يقال : هذا إذا كان الكل معقولاً بالكنه فإن تعقله يستلزم تعقل شيء من أجزائه لأنا نقول كلامنا في ذلك الوجه المعقول فإن كان بسيطاً فذاك وإن كان مركباً كان له بسائط كل واحد بالفعل.
