[That the human souls are immaterial] i.e., (they are not) a (corporeal) power (subsisting in matter) nor a body, but they are non-spatial, not subject to sensory reference. (Their attachment to the body is an attachment of management and disposal without being included in it by parthood or inherence. (This is the doctrine of the famous philosophers) among the earlier and later (and agreed with them among Muslims: al-Ghazali and al-Raghib) and a group of Sufi unveilers (and opposed them, the majority, based on what preceded of negating immaterial beings absolutely) whether intellects or souls. (They argued) i.e., the affirmers of their immateriality (with five aspects): The first: that it apprehends the simple, so it must be immaterial. As for the first, because it apprehends the reality of realities, i.e., the meaning of meanings. (If that reality is simple, then that is it) i.e., the desired is established, namely its apprehension of the simple. (Otherwise, that reality is (composed of simples) actually, because multiplicity, whether finite or infinite, requires the one actually, because it is its principle. (And it apprehends the whole after apprehending its parts) necessarily. It cannot be said: This is when the whole is intellectually comprehended in essence, for its apprehension entails the apprehension of something of its parts, because we say: Our discussion is about that intellectually comprehended aspect; if it is simple, then that is it, and if it is composite, it has simples each one actually. (And as for the second) that it, when attached to the simple, is immaterial, (because the locus of the simple, if it were a body or corporeal) i.e., if it had a position essentially or accidentally, (it would be divisible, and the division of the locus necessitates the division of the indwelling state in it, because the state in one of its parts is different from the state in (the other part, and that) i.e., the division of the state, which is knowledge, (contradicts simplicity) in the known, because knowledge must correspond to its known.) (It was answered that it is based on the soul being a locus for the intelligible) because intellection is the acquisition of the form in the rational power. (And it is denied, for knowledge according to us (is a mere relation between the knower and the known by which the known is distinguished for the knower, and that relation is a mental state attributed to the knower, not an existing state inhering in it. (And if it is granted) that knowledge is by the acquisition of the form of the known, (then the locus) i.e., the soul in that case is a locus (for the form of the simple) to which it is attached, not for the essence of the simple. (And correspondence is not necessary) between the form and the possessor of the form in all respects; the form of the simple may not be (simple). Do you not see what they said that it is possible for the external simple to have two or more intellectual forms, as mentioned in the discussion of states (ahwal). (And if it is granted that the form of the simple must be simple, (we do not accept that every possessor of position is divisible, for it is based on the denial of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajaza')) and it is denied, and in that case it is possible that the soul is an atomic substance (jawhar afrad) as some have said. (And if it is granted) that every possessor of position is divisible, (we do not accept that the state in the divisible is divisible, like the surface inhering in your view in the body divisible in all directions while it is not divisible in depth, and like the line inhering in the surface while it is not divisible in width, and like the point inhering in the line while it is not divisible at all. In summary, division of the state is necessary only if the inherence is diffusive (saryani), which is not granted in our case. (And if it is granted that it i.e., the state in the divisible (is divisible, then it is potential like the body, not actual, and that does not contradict simplicity, for it is possible that the aspect of its division is not the aspect of its simplicity.) For the simple body in your view is potentially divisible infinitely while being actually simple, since it has no actual joints, so there is no actual division, and no contradiction between division and its absence from the two aspects of potentiality and actuality, because they are two different aspects.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage defines the human soul as immaterial and presents the first proof with its detailed response. It begins by stating that the soul is not a corporeal power, nor a body, but a non-spatial entity that manages the body without being physically inside it. This is the view of the philosophers and was agreed upon by al-Ghazali, al-Raghib, and many Sufis, but opposed by the majority who reject immaterial beings. Then it presents the first of five arguments: the soul apprehends the simple. The argument has two parts: (1) the soul apprehends the reality of realities; if that reality is simple, it is proven; if composite, it must have simples, so the soul apprehends simples. (2) If the soul apprehends the simple, it must itself be simple, because if it were a body or corporeal, it would be divisible, and the division of the locus would divide the state (knowledge), contradicting the simplicity of the known. The response denies that the soul is a locus for the intelligible; knowledge is a mere relation, not an inherence. Even if granted that a form is acquired, the soul is a locus for the form, not the essence, so correspondence is not required. The response further denies that every possessor of position is divisible (by affirming indivisible atoms), and even if divisible, the state may not be divisible (like surface, line, point). Finally, even if divisible, it is potential, not actual, and does not contradict simplicity. This multi-layered response systematically dismantles the argument's assumptions.

مجردة
تعلق التدبير والتصرف
الحلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is a body or a physical power.
Correction: The passage explicitly denies that the soul is a body or a corporeal power; it is non-spatial and manages the body.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the soul relate to the body?
A) It is inside the body as a part.
B) It manages the body without being inside it.
C) It is a corporeal power.
D) It is separate from the body completely.

Key Takeaway
The soul is defined as an immaterial substance that governs the body without being physically located within it.

First Proof of Immateriality: Apprehending the Simple
First proof: the soul apprehends the simple, so it must be simple.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for the soul's immateriality, which is that it apprehends the simple and must therefore be simple itself.

Core Idea
The soul apprehends simple realities; a composite locus cannot know the simple without dividing it.

This lesson presents the first of five proofs that the soul is immaterial. The argument has two parts: first, the soul apprehends the reality of realities, which is either simple or composed of simples; second, if the soul were corporeal, it would be divisible, and the knowledge in it would be divided, contradicting the simplicity of the known.

Guided Path
1
Read the first part of the argument
Read the first part where the soul apprehends the reality of realities.
Notice the logic: if reality is simple, done; if composite, it has simples.
2
Understand why the soul must apprehend simples
The soul apprehends the whole, but if composite, it must apprehend parts first.
3
Study the second part about divisibility
If the soul were corporeal, it would be divisible, dividing knowledge.
This contradicts the simplicity of the known.
4
Notice the conclusion that the soul must be simple
Since knowledge of the simple cannot be divided, the soul cannot be corporeal.

( في أن النفوس الإنسانية مجردة) أي (ليست) قوة (جسمانية) حالة في المادة ) ولا جسماً) بل هي لامكانية لا تقبل إشارة حسية ( وإنما تعلقها بالبدن تعلق التدبير والتصرف من غير أن تكون داخلة فيه بالجزئية أو الحلول (هذا مذهب الفلاسفة) المشهورين من المتقدمين والمتأخرين ( ووافقهم على ذلك من المسلمين الغزالي والراغب ) وجمع من الصوفية المكاشفين ( وخالفهم فيه الجمهور بناء على ما مر من نفي المجردات على الإطلاق ) عقولاً كانت أو نفوساً (احتجوا) أي المثبتون لتجريدها (بوجوه ) خمسة : الأول : أنها تعقل البسيط الذي لا تكون مجردة أما الأول فلانها تعقل حقيقة ما من الحقائق أي معنى المعاني ( فإن كانت تلك الحقيقة بسيطة فذاك ) أي ثبت المطلوب أعني تعقلها للبسيط ( وإلا كانت تلك الحقيقة ( مركبة من البسائط ) بالفعل لأن الكثرة متناهية كانت أو غير متناهية يجب فيها الواحد بالفعل لأنه مبدؤها ( وتعقل الكل بعد تعقل أجزائه ) بالضرورة لا يقال : هذا إذا كان الكل معقولاً بالكنه فإن تعقله يستلزم تعقل شيء من أجزائه لأنا نقول كلامنا في ذلك الوجه المعقول فإن كان بسيطاً فذاك وإن كان مركباً كان له بسائط كل واحد بالفعل. ( وأما الثاني ) وهو أنها إذا تعلقت بالبسيط كانت مجردة ( فلان محل البسيط لو كان جسماً أو جسمانياً ) أي لو كان ذا وضع أصالة أو تبعاً ( لكان منقسماً وانقسام المحل يوجب انقسام الحال فيه لأن الحال في أحد جزئيه غير الحال في ( الجزء الآخر وإنه ) أي انقسام الحال الذي هو العلم ( ينافي البساطة ) في المعلوم إذ يجب أن يكون العلم مطابقاً لمعلومه . ) أجيب عنه بأنه : مبني على أن النفس محل للمعقول ) لأن التعقل عبارة عن حصول الصورة في القوة العاقلة ( وهو ممنوع فإن العلم عندنا ( مجرد تعلق بين العالم والمعلوم يمتاز به المعلوم عند العالم وذلك التعلق أمر اعتباري اتصف به العالم لا أمر موجود حال فيه ( وإن سلم ) أن العلم بحصول صورة المعلوم )( فمحل) أي فالنفس حينئذ محل ) لصورة البسيط) الذي تعلقه لا لذات البسيط ) ولا يلزم المطابقة ) بين الصورة وذي الصورة ) من جميع الوجوه فقد لا تكون صورة البسيط ( بسيطة ) ألا ترى إلى ما قالوه من أنه يجوز أن يكون للبسيط الخارجي صورتان عقليتان أو أكثر كما مر في مباحث الحال ( وإن سلم أن صورة البسيط يجب أن تكون بسيطة ( فلا نسلم أن كل ذي وضع منقسم فإنه بناء على نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ) وهو ممنوع وحينئذ جاز أن تكون النفس جوهر أفراد كما قال به بعض ) وإن سلم ) أن كل ذي وضع منقسم ( فلا تسلم أن الحال في المنقسم منقسم كالسطح الحال عندكم في الجسم المنقسم في جيمع الجهات مع أنه لا ينقسم في العمق وكالخط الحال في السطح مع عدم انقسامه في العرض وكالنقطة الحالة في الخط مع أنها لا تنقسم أصلاً. وبالجملة إنما يلزم انقسام الحال إذا كان الحلول سريانياً وهو فيما نحن بصدده غير مسلم ( وإن سلم أنه ( أي الحال في المنقسم ( منقسم فبالقوة كالجسم لا بالفعل وإنه لا ينافي البساطة لجواز أن تكون جهة انقسامه غير جهة بساطته ) فإن الجسم البسيط عندكم منقسم بالقوة إلى ما لا يتناهى مع كونه بسيطاً بالفعل إذ ليس فيه مفاصل متحققة فليس فيه انقسام فعلي ولا منافاة بين الانقسام وعدمه من جهتي القوة والفعل لأنهما جهتان متغايرتان
[That the human souls are immaterial] i.e., (they are not) a (corporeal) power (subsisting in matter) nor a body, but they are non-spatial, not subject to sensory reference. (Their attachment to the body is an attachment of management and disposal without being included in it by parthood or inherence. (This is the doctrine of the famous philosophers) among the earlier and later (and agreed with them among Muslims: al-Ghazali and al-Raghib) and a group of Sufi unveilers (and opposed them, the majority, based on what preceded of negating immaterial beings absolutely) whether intellects or souls. (They argued) i.e., the affirmers of their immateriality (with five aspects): The first: that it apprehends the simple, so it must be immaterial. As for the first, because it apprehends the reality of realities, i.e., the meaning of meanings. (If that reality is simple, then that is it) i.e., the desired is established, namely its apprehension of the simple. (Otherwise, that reality is (composed of simples) actually, because multiplicity, whether finite or infinite, requires the one actually, because it is its principle. (And it apprehends the whole after apprehending its parts) necessarily. It cannot be said: This is when the whole is intellectually comprehended in essence, for its apprehension entails the apprehension of something of its parts, because we say: Our discussion is about that intellectually comprehended aspect; if it is simple, then that is it, and if it is composite, it has simples each one actually. (And as for the second) that it, when attached to the simple, is immaterial, (because the locus of the simple, if it were a body or corporeal) i.e., if it had a position essentially or accidentally, (it would be divisible, and the division of the locus necessitates the division of the indwelling state in it, because the state in one of its parts is different from the state in (the other part, and that) i.e., the division of the state, which is knowledge, (contradicts simplicity) in the known, because knowledge must correspond to its known.) (It was answered that it is based on the soul being a locus for the intelligible) because intellection is the acquisition of the form in the rational power. (And it is denied, for knowledge according to us (is a mere relation between the knower and the known by which the known is distinguished for the knower, and that relation is a mental state attributed to the knower, not an existing state inhering in it. (And if it is granted) that knowledge is by the acquisition of the form of the known, (then the locus) i.e., the soul in that case is a locus (for the form of the simple) to which it is attached, not for the essence of the simple. (And correspondence is not necessary) between the form and the possessor of the form in all respects; the form of the simple may not be (simple). Do you not see what they said that it is possible for the external simple to have two or more intellectual forms, as mentioned in the discussion of states (ahwal). (And if it is granted that the form of the simple must be simple, (we do not accept that every possessor of position is divisible, for it is based on the denial of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajaza')) and it is denied, and in that case it is possible that the soul is an atomic substance (jawhar afrad) as some have said. (And if it is granted) that every possessor of position is divisible, (we do not accept that the state in the divisible is divisible, like the surface inhering in your view in the body divisible in all directions while it is not divisible in depth, and like the line inhering in the surface while it is not divisible in width, and like the point inhering in the line while it is not divisible at all. In summary, division of the state is necessary only if the inherence is diffusive (saryani), which is not granted in our case. (And if it is granted that it i.e., the state in the divisible (is divisible, then it is potential like the body, not actual, and that does not contradict simplicity, for it is possible that the aspect of its division is not the aspect of its simplicity.) For the simple body in your view is potentially divisible infinitely while being actually simple, since it has no actual joints, so there is no actual division, and no contradiction between division and its absence from the two aspects of potentiality and actuality, because they are two different aspects.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage defines the human soul as immaterial and presents the first proof with its detailed response. It begins by stating that the soul is not a corporeal power, nor a body, but a non-spatial entity that manages the body without being physically inside it. This is the view of the philosophers and was agreed upon by al-Ghazali, al-Raghib, and many Sufis, but opposed by the majority who reject immaterial beings. Then it presents the first of five arguments: the soul apprehends the simple. The argument has two parts: (1) the soul apprehends the reality of realities; if that reality is simple, it is proven; if composite, it must have simples, so the soul apprehends simples. (2) If the soul apprehends the simple, it must itself be simple, because if it were a body or corporeal, it would be divisible, and the division of the locus would divide the state (knowledge), contradicting the simplicity of the known. The response denies that the soul is a locus for the intelligible; knowledge is a mere relation, not an inherence. Even if granted that a form is acquired, the soul is a locus for the form, not the essence, so correspondence is not required. The response further denies that every possessor of position is divisible (by affirming indivisible atoms), and even if divisible, the state may not be divisible (like surface, line, point). Finally, even if divisible, it is potential, not actual, and does not contradict simplicity. This multi-layered response systematically dismantles the argument's assumptions.

Key Terms
تعقل البسيط
البساطة
الحقيقة
المعنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul can be a body and still know simple things.
Correction: The argument shows that if the soul were a body, its knowledge would be divided, contradicting the simplicity of the known.

Checkpoint
What is the second part of the argument based on?
A) The soul's ability to manage the body.
B) The divisibility of a corporeal locus leads to division of knowledge.
C) The soul's attachment to the body.
D) The simplicity of the soul itself.

Key Takeaway
The soul must be immaterial because it apprehends simple realities; a corporeal soul would divide knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a concept you truly understand, like 'justice' or 'mercy.' Is that understanding divided into parts in your mind, or is it a unified grasp? What does this tell you about the nature of your soul?
?
If your soul were purely material, could it grasp the meaning of 'infinity' or 'unity' without fracturing it? This means then that you are not merely your brain — so who are you, really?
?
You claim to believe in God, who is absolutely simple. How can a composite, material organ like the brain truly know a simple God? If your soul is not immaterial, your knowledge of God is impossible. Will you accept the logical consequence and affirm your soul's immateriality?

advanced
8 min
Responses to the First Proof of Immateriality
Multiple responses to the first proof, denying premises and offering alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed responses to the first argument for the soul's immateriality, including alternative theories of knowledge and defenses of atomism.

Core Idea
The argument assumes the soul is a locus of knowledge; but knowledge is a relation, not an inherence, and even if it were, many objections prevent the conclusion.

This lesson covers the responses to the first proof. The opponent questions the assumption that the soul is a locus for intelligible forms, proposing instead that knowledge is a mere relation. Even if the soul were a locus, the form of the simple need not be simple, the locus may not be divisible (if atoms exist), and even if divisible, the state may not divide (like surface, line, point), or division could be potential and not contradict simplicity. These responses systematically dismantle the argument's premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first response: knowledge is a relation
The first response denies the soul is a locus; knowledge is a relation between knower and known.
2
Examine the second response: the form of the simple may not be simple
Even if knowledge involves a form, the form of a simple object can be complex.
Multiple forms can represent the same simple thing.
3
Understand the third response: indivisible atoms
The opponent rejects the premise that every spatial thing is divisible; atoms exist, so the soul could be an atomic substance.
4
Analyze the fourth response: states like surface/line/point
Even in a divisible locus, the state (like surface on body) may not be divisible in some dimensions.
Thus, knowledge need not divide.
5
Consider the fifth response: potential division
Even if divisible, division could be potential, not actual, and that does not conflict with simplicity.
Example: a simple body is potentially divisible.

Commentary

It begins by stating that the soul is not a corporeal power, nor a body, but a non-spatial entity that manages the body without being physically inside it. This is the view of the philosophers and was agreed upon by al-Ghazali, al-Raghib, and many Sufis, but opposed by the majority who reject immaterial beings. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The responses all rely on atomism.
Correction: The responses use multiple strategies: relational theory of knowledge, forms not mirroring objects, atomism, non-divisible states, and potentiality.

Checkpoint
What is the first response given against the argument?
A) The soul is an atomic substance.
B) Knowledge is a relation, not an inherence.
C) The form of the simple must be simple.
D) The soul is not a locus for anything.

Key Takeaway
The responses challenge every premise of the argument, offering alternative views on knowledge, substance, and division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your knowledge of Allah is a relation rather than a fixed possession, what are you actively doing to strengthen that connection? Are you relying on a passive inherence or an active, ongoing relationship?
?
The author seriously entertains multiple objections to a core belief. When was the last time you examined a foundational tenet of your faith with the same intellectual honesty? Are you willing to consider counterarguments not to doubt, but to deepen your understanding?
?
The debate questions whether your soul is a simple, unified reality or a composite. In your daily life, do you experience your soul as a single, consistent self or as a battleground of conflicting desires? How does that affect your struggle for inner coherence?

The Second Proof: Apprehending Existence
Second proof for soul's immateriality: apprehending existence as simple

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the soul's immateriality—its apprehension of existence as a simple concept—and how it is answered by referring to earlier objections.

Core Idea
The soul's apprehension of simple existence points to its own simplicity.

In this brief passage, al-Sanusi presents the second of five proofs for the soul's immateriality: the human soul apprehends existence, and existence is a simple concept (not composed of parts). The argument relies on earlier discussions about the simplicity of existence. The response, however, is short—it directs us back to the objections already raised against those earlier proofs and the first argument. Thus, no new refutation is offered; the familiar counterarguments suffice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

The phrase 'as has preceded' indicates reliance on earlier sections.
2
Analyze the structure: argument and response
Identify the two parts: the proof (the soul apprehends existence, existence is simple) and the answer (references previous objections). Consider why the author does not give a new refutation.

Third Proof: Apprehending Universals
Third argument from universals for immateriality with incomplete response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for the soul's immateriality based on its apprehension of universal concepts.

Core Idea
The soul's ability to grasp universals proves it has no physical location.

This passage presents the third proof that the soul is immaterial, drawing on our capacity to understand universal concepts and make judgments about them. The argument is structured as a reductio ad absurdum, showing that if the soul were material, it could not apprehend universals. The response is cut off in the text, but the argument itself is complete.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two parts
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two parts of the proof: first, that apprehending universals is obvious; second, the reductio argument against the soul having a position.
The passage begins with 'The third' and ends with 'And the answer'.
2
Understand why apprehending universals requires immateriality
Consider why the soul must be immaterial to judge universals. The first part is stated as self-evident: the soul judges among universals, so it must apprehend them. Reflect on why a material entity could not perform such judgments.
The author assumes that judgment about universals cannot be done by a physical brain alone.
3
Follow the reductio argument
Walk through the second part: assume the soul has a position. Then a universal meaning would inhere in a thing with position, restricting it to a specific quantity and position. But universals apply to many different quantities and positions, so a contradiction arises. Therefore, the soul cannot have a position.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Note the incomplete response
Observe that the text cuts off at 'And the answer'. This means the author's response to the argument is missing. Consider what possible objections or explanations might follow.
The response likely addresses the strength of the argument or provides a different perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : ( من تلك الوجوه ( أنها تعقل المفهوم الكلي فتكون مجردة أما الأول فظاهر) لأنها تحكم بين الكليات أحكاماً إيجابية وسلبية فلا بد من تعقلها ( وأما الثاني فلان ) النفس إذا كانت ذات وضع كان المعنى الكلي حالاً في ذي وضع ولا شك أن الحال في ذي الوضع يختص بمقدار مخصوص ( ووضع معين ثابتين لمحله ( فلا يكون ) ذلك الحال ) مطابقاً لكثيرين مختلفين بالمقدار والوضع بل لا يكون مطابقاً إلا لما له ذلك المقدار (والوضع فلا يكون حينئذ كلياً، هذا خلف لأن المقدر خلافه . ( والجواب
The third: (of those aspects) (that it apprehends the universal concept, so it is immaterial. As for the first, it is obvious) because it judges among universals positive and negative judgments, so it must apprehend them. (And as for the second) because (the soul, if it had a position, the universal meaning would be inhering in a thing with position, and there is no doubt that the inherence in a thing with position is restricted to a specific quantity (and a specific position fixed for its locus, so (that state) would not be) corresponding to many things differing in quantity and position, but would correspond only to that which has that quantity (and position, so it would not then be universal. This is a reductio ad absurdum because the assumption is contrary.) (And the answer
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This is the third argument for the soul's immateriality: the soul apprehends universal concepts and makes judgments about them (positive and negative), so it must be immaterial. The proof has two parts: first, that it apprehends universals is obvious; second, if the soul had a position, a universal meaning inhering in it would be restricted to that position and quantity, and could not correspond to multiple differing instances, so it would not be universal—a contradiction. The response is indicated ('wa al-jawab') but the text cuts off. The argument is complete but the author's answer is missing in this window.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul can be material even though it thinks about universals.
Correction: The argument demonstrates that if the soul were material (had position), a universal meaning would be restricted to that position and could not apply to many instances, contradicting its universality. Hence the soul must be immaterial.

Checkpoint
What does the third argument conclude about the soul?
A) The soul is material.
B) The soul must be universal.
C) The soul is simple.
D) The soul is immaterial.

Key Takeaway
The soul's apprehension of universal concepts proves it cannot be material, because a material thing with position cannot grasp concepts that apply to many different instances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is not bound by physical limits because it apprehends universals, why do you still let your physical circumstances (health, wealth, comfort) dictate your spiritual state? Will you today choose to act from your immaterial soul's longing for truth rather than your body's impulses?
?
You have just learned that your soul's ability to understand concepts like justice, mercy, and truth proves it is immaterial. How does this truth confront the way you have been seeking fulfillment in material things? What will you change starting now to honor the immaterial nature of your soul?
?
The proof shows that your soul cannot perish materially. If you truly believe your soul lives on beyond the body, what are you doing today to prepare that soul for its eternal journey? Are you investing more in your temporary body or your permanent soul?

Jinn and Devils as Subtle Bodies
Author presents and refutes philosophical objections to jinn and devils as subtle bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic theological view that jinn and devils are real bodies with subtlety meaning transparency, and how this refutes the philosophical objection that subtle bodies would be either too weak or visible.

Core Idea
Jinn and devils are real, transparent bodies, not weak or invisible.

This passage concludes the discussion on intellects by examining the nature of jinn and devils. The author presents the religious view that they are bodies capable of assuming any shape and penetrating interiors, then addresses the philosophical objection that such bodies would be either too weak or visible. The answer lies in understanding subtlety as transparency, which preserves both their strength and invisibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the full argument. Notice the structure: statement of belief, disagreement, philosophical objection, and response.
The passage ends abruptly but the response is clear.
2
Identify the philosophical objection
The philosophers present a dilemma: if jinn bodies are subtle, they would be too weak; if not subtle, they would be seen. Understand why each horn is considered false in their view.
3
Understand the author's response
The author explains that subtlety here means transparency (shafafiyya), i.e., lack of color. This means they are not necessarily weak and can be strong without being seen.
Transparency allows them to be both powerful and invisible.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this refutation supports the reality of jinn and devils as obligated beings alongside angels and humans. This resolves the dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة لمباحث العقول في الجن والشياطين فإنها أيضا من الجواهر الغائية عن حواسنا ( وهي عند المليين أجسام تتشكل بأني شكل شاءت ) وتقدر على أن تتولج في بواطن الحيوانات وتنفذ في منافذها الضيقة نفوذ الهواء المستنشق واختلفوا في اختلافهما بالنوع مع الاتفاق على أنهما من أصناف المكلفين كالملك والأنس ) ومنعه الفلاسفة لأنها إما أن تكون ) الأجسام ( طليقة أو لا، وكلاهما باطل، أما الأول فلأنه يلزم أن لا تقدر ) هي ) على الأفعال الشاقة وتتلاشى بأدنى قوة وسبب من خارج يصل إليهما ( وهو خلاف ما يعتقدونه، وأما الثاني : فلانه يوجب أن ترى ولو جوزنا أجساما كثيفة لا نراها لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا جبال وبلاد لا نراها وبوقات وطبول نسمعها وهو سفسطة ) محضة ) والجواب : أن لطفها بمعنى الشفافية ) أي عدم اللون ( فلا يلزم أحد الأمرين لجواز
Conclusion of the investigations of intellects concerning jinn and devils, for they are also from the hidden substances beyond our senses. And they are, according to the religious people, bodies that form into any shape they wish, and are able to enter into the interiors of animals and penetrate into their narrow passages like the penetration of inhaled air. And they disagreed about their difference in species while agreeing that they are from the kinds of obligated beings like angels and humans. And the philosophers denied them because, of bodies, they are either subtle or not, and both are false. As for the first, because it would entail that they are not able to perform strenuous actions and would disintegrate with the least external force that reaches them—contrary to what they believe. And as for the second, because it would require that they be seen. And if we allow dense bodies that we do not see, it would be possible that in our presence there are mountains and lands we do not see, and trumpets and drums we hear—which is pure sophistry. The answer: Their subtlety means transparency, i.e., absence of color, so neither of the two consequences follows, because it is possible
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of the discussion on intellects, now turning to jinn and devils. It begins by stating that they are also hidden substances beyond our senses. According to the religious people (al-milliyyun), they are bodies that can take any shape and can enter animals' interiors. There is disagreement about whether they differ in species from angels and humans, but all agree they are obligated beings. The philosophers object, arguing that if these bodies are subtle, they would be too weak; if not, they would be seen. The author responds that their subtlety means transparency (lack of color), so they are not too weak nor necessarily visible, thus the objection is invalid. The text cuts off but likely continues to elaborate.

Key Terms
الجن
الشياطين
جواهر غائية
أجسام
لطف
شفافية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That subtle bodies cannot be real because they would be either too weak to act or would be visible.
Correction: Subtlety here means transparency (lack of color), not weakness. Transparent bodies can be strong and yet invisible, so the objection fails.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the philosophical objection that jinn and devils as subtle bodies would be either too weak or visible?
A) By denying they are bodies
B) By stating that their subtlety means transparency, so they are not weak nor necessarily visible
C) By saying that they are not obligated beings
D) By agreeing with the philosophers

Key Takeaway
The Islamic view holds that jinn and devils are real, transparent bodies, strong enough to act but not seen, thus refuting philosophical doubts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that jinn and devils are real, transparent bodies that can enter animals' interiors like inhaled air. Do you live as though the unseen world is as real as the seen? When was the last time your awareness of jinn or devils changed a decision you made?
?
The philosophers’ objection was based on what they could observe and reason. What aspects of your faith do you hold despite not being able to see or fully explain them? Are you willing to trust the revelation even when your intellect raises doubts?
?
Jinn and devils are among the 'obligated beings'—they have duties. How seriously do you take your own obligation to Allah? Does the reality of these unseen beings motivate you to be more mindful of your actions and intentions?
